THE RED PAGE
A Gentleman of Leisure —P. G. Wodehouse (Dy-
mock’s). A light and entertaining story,
which appeared somewhile ago in America
as The Intrusions of Jimmy. That two
separate titles should so often be used is a
mystery ; also a nuisance.
The Mountain of God —E. S. Stevens (G. Robertson
and Co.). A love story of English people
in Syria, by the author of The Veil. The
book was evidently written to explain the
Bahai doctrines, “ which might be described
as a religious progressivism, in which the
most humanitarian and practical ideas of the
West are wedded to devotion and mysticism
of a very high order.” The mountain is
Carmel, where the Persian exiles who preach
the new religion under ; Abdul Baha have a
monastery. The spread of Bahaism has been
remarkably rapid, and this story, shows that
“ The Master ” is a high-class spell-binder.
The Jews: A Study of Race and Environment, by
Maurice Fishberg (Contemporary Science
Series, 65.). The Jews of Western Europe and
U.S.A. are mixing with their neighbors and
losing their orthodoxy ; their birth rate de-
clining as they become free and prosperous.
The Eastern Jews remain poor, orthodox and
prolific. Russian oppression, therefore, helps
to keep the religion pure and maintain the
supply of Jews. These are the conclusions of
Fishberg’s wide and thorough study of the
present position of his race.
Victor Daley’s “Wine and Roses”
Twelve years ago and more, wrapt in the
glowing perfection of a Sydney June, I sat
looking at Victor Daley’s first volume, won-
dering how to get on paper the warmth
and color and the magic of it. “The
sun,” I wrote at last, “is master of Daley’s
verse. What though it begins with the
fading day, by Lethe, by Kendall’s grave-
turn the page, and we are in sunshine; the
world is aflood with it, the wine is full of
it, it sets the verse aflame.”
To-day I have been reading the second
volume. The twelve years may have some-
thing to do with it. . . but the sun has gone
in, and the colors have paled; and one
feels but rarely the thrill of the old magic.
Nor is it the true Daley magic: without
the glow lie seems often a mere understudy
of Kendall. Twelve years ago, it would
seem, we caught him just as the inspira-
tion began to slacken; we would not be-
lieve him when he told us that
All the glory and the glow
Of life are passed, and dead, and gone.
But he knew better.
So much, has to be said first. The lover
of At Dawn and Dusk must be warned
that Wine and Roses is no twin-sister.
The splendor of the earlier verse was
unique, and Daley could not recover it, any
more than the Henley of Hawthorn and
Lavender could re-acquire the splendid con-
fidence of the London Voluntaries. You
will not find another “Sunset Fantasy,”
another “Passion Flower.” But you will
find beautiful verse still.
In Avonaise, in Avonaise,
Where all is dead and done,
The folk who rest there all their days
Care not for moon and sun.
That is the Daley of “Cares,” wearier
and older, but magical still.
And softly over all is drawn
The quiet curtain of the grass.
That is the tender, delicate poet of the
Lines on Violet Varley:
And now I stand with back against the wall,
A beetle pinned against the wall of Fate :
I think—if God is, as I think Him, great—
That He will wipe my score out, once for all.
This is a new Daley, broken and old,
with vital forces well-high spent—past car-
ing, but not past appealing (you must read
the whole poem; one stanza cannot sum
it) to the eternal justice. And where one
cannot —as one can regarding the last three
quotations—sample the whole poem by
quoting a few lines, yet glimpses of the old
magic are seen: as this of the sea:
Yet in her heart-deeps tragic
The lost Atlantis lies ;
and this, Kendall-wise:
At times by some green-ey el ashed pool
She lies in slumber deep ;
Her slender hands are white and cool
As are the hands of sleep;
or the verses on Sydney Harbor:
The long reflections shine,
Quivering tremulously
On the waters velvet-dark ;
And those shining spirals seem to me
Tall golden columns Byzantine
Of palaces under the sea ;
or the great boast of Woman:
In ages ere the stars took flame
There brooded on my breast the dove,
And from my womb the white worlds came.
Notable, too, as a reminiscence of the old
Daley in his epigrammatic mood is this:
I learnt the language of the birds ;
A new Saint Francis I would be ;
But, when I understood their words,
The birds were preaching unto me.
Passing up and down the volume, one
comes at last to an understanding of its-
keynote. Daley was tired —he could sing
his splendid song no longer; he was disil-
lusioned —and he could not live without
illusions. He was disillusioned, that is,
as to the present; and all the more ear-
nestly he recalled, and sought to re-create,
the illusions of the past. Kipling and he
both agree that Romance is not dead; and
Kipling and the Daley of At Dawn and Dusk
would have meant the same thing by that
statement. But this Daley means some-
thing very different. Kipling’s Romance
is the ruler, the inspiring soul, of Reality,
but Daley bids us cast off the fetters of
Reality and seek the shrine of a dea,d Ro>
mance, the haunts of Blanaid the Fair and
Gold Gudrun —he longs to follow the
Green Harper to the Wood of Broceliande.
And that longing, that command, as imag-
ing a set mood of the mind, is fatal.
Rare and lonely is the man who dares
live without illusions; but the illusions
must be our own, not borrowed. No two
men see the same rainbow; no man, or
woman, dreams another’s dream —though
sometimes two dream the same by divine
good-fortune, and go on dreaming it, and
that is marriage. But to look for com
fort and nourishment in the dreams of
other lands and other ages is mere quest
of starvation —at any rate for so warm-
blooded a man as was Victor Daley.
What starved him? What wore his heart
out? It is a matter that Australians need
to consider very earnestly. What do we
do with our poets, that this is the end of
them? It is a cheap answer that it has
always been so—that in all ages the poetic
temperament has been found incompatible
with money-getting; that Milton only got
£lO, or £s,' or whatever the pittance was,
for Paradise Lost, and so on. In so far as
the general statement is true, it is irrele-
vant. We have managed, despite all pre-
cedents, to raise the wages of the artisan;
We do our best to encourage the inventor
(unless, apparently, we are Railway Com-
missioners) ; we are consciously and delib-
erately moving towards an age when every
citizen with capacities useful to the State
shall be given the opportunity to use them.
But for the poet, who is as essential a citi-
zen as the Professor of Chemistry, or the
Lord Mayor, we can provide, seemingly,
nothing but columns of statistics to add
up. A free allotment at Rookwood would
be simpler and less cruel.
N.S.W.
ISHMAEL DARE.
Lindsay’s "Petronius”
The company of great grotesques in
literature, which includes Pantagruel, Fai-
staff, Sanclio, Tartar in and a few others,
has no more finely-drawn character than
pot-bellied Trimalchio —a superior Chris-
topher Sly, with whom fortune played a
joke which lasted for years. He is the
central figure of a fragment which is all
that We have of the earliest novel. Whether
written by Petronius or not is unproven,
THE NOTARY.
but reasonably certain. The Arbiter Ele-
gantiarum, whose artistic suicide at the
order of Nero is mentioned by Tacitus,
may have been such a person as Sienkie-
wicz re-created in Quo Vttdis, or Marcel
Schwob drew finely in Vies Imaginaires;
all we know of him makes him just
the man to write a brilliant realistic
novel. After a score of centuries his work
seems as vital and interesting as most
fiction of our own time. It is more modern
in spirit than The Golden Ass of a little
later date; more modern even than Nash’s
Jack Wilton, the. best of the picaresque
romances of the Elizabethan period.
The text of Petronius, edited by S.
Gaselee, and the earliest translation into
English (that of William Burnaby and
others, published in 1694), have been
printed on a hand-press- by Ralph Straus,
of London —the Straus who wrote The
Scandalous Mr. Waldo and the biography
of Robert Dodsley, publisher. This large
quarto volume is a splendid specimen of
the printers’ art; but its principal value
is as a commentary on 100 illustrations
by Norman Lindsay.
* For the first time a Latin classic has
been illustrated by an artist who has thor-
oughly entered into its spirit. The time is
that of Rome’s decadence under Nero, and
the characters of Petronius are representa-
tive. They are pictured here in pen and
ink lines which seem to quiver with move-
ment and life. The subjects are admirably
TRIMALCHIO AT BALL.
suited for Lindsay’s pen, and his dexterity
has seldom been seen to such advantage.
This is not the place to praise his quality.
Let it go at the phrase of the S. M. Herald
reviewer—“the soul of Aubrey Beardsley
in the resurrected body of Hogarth.” As
a book of special interest for more than
one reason, Lindsay’s Petronius is bound to
go up in value. Already Hugh McCrae’s
Satyrs and Sunlight, illustrated by Lind-
say, has advanced from one to five guineas
and there are no sellers. A few copies of
Petronius are obtainable from Angus and
Robertson and Dymock’s Book Arcade at
£6 6s. each.

A venture of some interest to Australians
is the new quarterly review of the politics of
the British Empire, called The Round Table ,
published by H. C. Morgan, 55 Fetter Lane,
London, at 2s. 6d. per copy. It promises to
give “a regular account of what is going on
throughout the King’s dominions, written
with first hand knowledge and entirely free
from the bias of local political issues, and to
provide a means by which the common prob-
lems which confront the Empire as a whole
can be discussed also with knowledge and
without bias.” He is a feather-brained opti-
mist who expects discussion of any value
without bias.j still, like the star to which
you should hitch your waggon, it is a good
ideal for the editor to keep before him.
The first number, dated November last,
has unsigned articles on Anglo-German
Rivalry, India and the English, the Consti-
tutional Crisis in Britain, Home Rule, Cana-
dian and South African politics.
The clear and logical statement of the
causes which have led up to the present
relations between Britain and Germany is a
model for publicists. The writer does not
assume too much knowledge on the part of
the reader, but gives him all the necessary
facts, connects them and explains them
lucidly. If each number of The Round
Table has only one article of that quality it
•will be worth getting. There is no agent in
Australia at present ; but orders can be sent
to the publisher'direct, or through any book-
seller.
Tolstoy’s Politics
“E.D.” (Toowoomba) wants to know if
Tolstoy was ever a Socialist, and what was
his influence as a political writer. . . ,
Hard to say what Tolstoy wasn't at one
time or other, but after he got religion he
advocated political reform by means
of the Single Tax and withdrawal
from the army and the administration.
Seeing the State in Russia as a military
bureaucracy, supported by the Church and
working for the Devil, he thought the best
course was to drop out, and urge others to
do the same. As far as he was able he
went to the root of things, and preached
first principles, without caring whether
they were practicable or not.
If his teaching and example had been
generally followed, anarchy would arrive
before its time; but his-ffniluence has been
a vaguely diffused one, and the Tolstoyites
are not thorough-going.
Anarchy or no-government is an ideal
held by many who do not consider it within
the domain of practical politics. Oscar
Wilde is not regarded as a political au-
thority, but he put the case very well in
The Soul of Mon , /
H. G. Wells’ is an anarchist in a
sense. In the Days of the Comet he said:
“The anarchist world is our dream. The
way to that is through education, disci-
pline, and law. .... Socialism is the
schoolroom of true and noble anarchism,
wherein by training and restraint we shall
make men free.” Probably Tolstoy re-
garded that way as too long.
There are lots of people pointing
ways out of present troubles. “This age,”
says Wells in. The Neiv Machiavelli, “far
beyond all previous ages is full of powerful
men, men who might, if they had the will
for. it, achieve tremendous things.” Many
of these are anxious to mend civilisation
and put Society on the right track. As-
sume Society to be the central fact —the gi-
gantic, loosely-cohering conglomerate in
which there is so much unwisdom, injus-
tice, absurdity, etc. The reformers are tug-
ging. away to get the concern out of the
ruts, and their ropes ray out like the
spokes of a wheel. She does move a little,
this way and that; and one man shouts,
and then another, that if all would pull
his way the durned thing would move
ahead in a straight line. . . . There are
more on the Socialistic side than on any
other, and many of them have been cheered
by the example of Tolstoy, tugging honestly
at the other side, all alone.
The Romantic Movement in French Litera-
ture traced in a series of texts selected and
edited by IT. F. Stewart and A. Tilley
(Angus and Robertson). Here you have the
history of a movement which profoundly
affected French literature told by the docu-
ments—beginning with Madame de StaeTs
He L'Allemagne and including Victor Hugo’s
prefaces, Sainte-Beuve’s reviews, Gautier’s
Histoire du Romantisme, etc. The influence
of Balzac and others in deflecting and finally
breaking up the movement is explained in
the notes. The book is an admirable one for
students of French literature, for though, as
the editors say, ‘ ‘ the romantic theory of life
with its exaggerations and puerilities has
passed away; the romantic theory of
literature abides. French literature. .. .
has never abandoned the great prin-
ciple which the Romantic movement . . .
came to teach— that a work of art springs
from the emotions and is shaped by the
imagination of the individual artist.”

A new magazine from America — Adventure
(Ridgway Op., New York) promises to be full of
thrills every month. The contents list of the
December number has these titles: “ Home
Through Hades,” “A Poisoned Blockade,” “The
Sword-edge of Chance,” “Where Hell is Frozen
Over.” One yarn, “Fear,” has some good psy-
chology in it. .. . A story by Randolph Bedford
was to appear in the January number.

HEARNE’S
Bronchitis Cure
The Grandest Remedy in the World for
COUGHS & COLDS
ONE DOSE IS GENERALLY SUFFICIENT.
THOSE who have taken this medicine are amazed at
its splendid healing power. Sufferers from Bron-
chitis, Cough, Croup, Asthma, Hoarseness, Difficulty
of Breathing, Pain or Soreness in the Chest, experience
delightful and rapid relief; and to those who are subject
to Colds on the Chest it is invaluable, as it effects a
complete cure. It is most comforting in allaying Irrita-

Tickles the Palate
VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Our Kid Retaliates.
I have only just recently learnt to speak—
To be precise, it was yesterday week ;
And I learn with surprise
What I characterise
As libels —to put it more pithily—lies
Have been printed concerning Yours Truly.
Remarks have been made that occasion me pain,
I’ve tried to ignore them, but tried in vain;
For wherever I go
Rude grown-ups say, “Oh,
We’ve heard all about your antics, you
know ;
We’ve read about you in the Bclly ! ”
Whenever I go for my afternoon stroll—
I’ve just learnt to stagger—it harrows my soul
To think of that verse.
If it wasn’t for Nurse—
She’s so easily shocked—l’m sure I could curse—
Dad’s poems are horribly sloppy !
Unaccustomed to writing, I take up my pen
My mummie has hold of my hand ; but then
It’s hard not to blot,
And it wobbles a lot—
fo state that a dadda should promptly be shot
Who out of his kiddie makes copy !
He writes about me as if I were a freak,
But of his own faults lie forgets to speak.
He brings me home toys
That don't make a noise ;
He can’t know the least little bit about boys,
Or how could he be so neglectful !
I don’t think he’s strong ; when I ride on his back
In less than an hour he loses the knack.
Though I want him to stay
In the mornings and play.
He goes a ta-ta to his office each day ;
It doesn’t to me seem respectful.
I forgive him for writing his slushy ohl rimes,
But I find as a father he’s trying at times.
I don’t think I’ve luck
In the daddie I’ve struck ;
Why, when I take some of his matches to suck
He flies into one of his rages !
When I bring him the morning paper in bits,
All carefully torn up, he goes into fits.
It doesn’t seem quite
Good-tempered or right;
And he’s not sympathetic—nor even polite—
When out of his books I tear pages.
And though he my habits has harshly maligned,
I doubt if a stupider father you’d find.
When I wake up at four
He does nothing but snore,
Though I’m ready to play at Big Bears on the
floor.
Oh, he’s not a success as a daddie !
When I poke his eyes open to tell him I’m there,
I’ve frequently heard what I think was a swear.
When I sit on his nose
To wake him he shows
Me none of the gratitude you would suppose
A father would feel for his laddie !
N.S.W.
ARTHUR H. ADAMS JUNIOR.
Spring-tide.
Spring came with skies amber and blue,
Rich color, sweet scent!
I looked in your soft eyes and knew
All Spring’s coming meant,
Birds mating with fabulous song
Love woke in my breast,
A roulade of passion, and long
Lips unto lips prest.
Spring gave the world nothing so sweet
As your maidenhood ;
You laid your young life at my feet
For evil, for good—
Your heart, a voluptuous bird’s,
Homed close to m3' heart,
While I in a torrent of words
Sang madly my part!
About us buds burst, and above,
In Heaven’s deep blue,
The sun in a girdle of love
Ensnared me and you ;
And o’er the green glory of earth
Spring hastened to greet
The love-flower and treasure its worth,
Desirable, sweet.
Spring comes, and to greet her buds blow,
Birds mate in old scenes ;
I look in your soft eyes and know
All Spring’s coming means. . . .
A sunburst of gladness, rare scent,
Rich color, life seems
A vista of Spring-tides o’er-sprent
With passionate dreams 1
N.S.W.
W. M. WHITNEY.
The Beckoning.
Afar is fled the sweet intoxication.
The ruddy marriage torches glimmer low;
And Joy ebbs on, in waves of soft elation,
Around the house where Passion held us so.
And half-expectant, half in fearful wonder,
We venture forward in a foreign land,
Where ’mid the future mists that part asunder
We catch the beck’ning of a baby hand.
QUEENSLAND.

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to Tun Bullktik would oblige the paver
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bullktik," is the proper
superscription for “copy." Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
oflicials. The person may not be there; the official
always is.

lishers. The right to reproduce or translate any part of
the contents of this number is hereby expressly reserved by
the publishers.

acknowledge in the “Correspondence" column all contri-
butions submitted, whether in the form of Political,
Social, or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales or Sketches
(those dealing with Australian subjects, and not exceeding
tree columns in length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially
acceptable), Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper Clippings.
All communications will be regarded as strictly confi-
dential.

address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But no

are observed. When MS. is forwarded without stamps
for return it will be understood that the copy is to be
destroyed if not acceptable. Personal applications for
return of MS. cannot receive attention. Tiik Bullktik
desires, further, those sending MS. to distinctly under-

THURSDAY, MARCH 9, 1911.
THE ANTI-REFERENDA CAMPAIGN TO
DATE.
The first Lord Coleridge once turned to the
jury, after cross-examining a heraldry expert, and
said: “ Why, gentlemen, this foolish person
doesn’t know the rudiments of his own foolish
trade.” It is a sentiment which must occur to
the average citizen who considers the “Liberal ’
objections that have been lodged, so far, to the
Referenda proposals. On the evidence, “Liber-
alism” doesn’t even know the State Fright trade.
Nearly all those who assail the Federal Govern-
ment’s proposals leave details, authorities and
the legal aspect of the question severely alone.
Reliance is mainly placed on a whirling verbal
riot about “ robbery,” “buccaneering,” “filched
rights” and “fractured compacts”—large, abusive
phrases which sound effective enough at street-
corner meetings and so forth, but cut no ice in
cold print. Employing much the same tactics as
G. H. Reid used in his 190 G campaign, the anti-
Nationalists seek to convince the multitude that,
unless success lights on their cause —a cause which,
significantly enough, is barracked for by every one
of the low-wage, Freetrade, Anti-Sosh crowd that
has stood by reaction in the past—some un-
specified calamity will smite Australia. Reid s
menace or spook was a Socialistic liger,
an utterly incredible animal which was going
to destroy the Australian home, abolish the
marriage tie and plunge the country in anarchy
unless its author became Prime Minister. I he
opponents of the Referenda proposals have con-
jured up a similar horror. They blandly ask a
people who are not naturally half-witted, and
who have enjoyed over a quarter of a century of
compulsory education, to credit that the result of
an affirmative vote next month will be the
“spoliation of the States at the hands of the
Federation,” and a condition of things so mtoler-
able as to drive Bruce Smith to revolution and
bloodshed. m „
* * * *
Six years ago Australia examined G. Reid’s
Titter, and decided to treat it with lightsome
indifference, not to say scorn—as a result of which
Reid endured one of the severest biffs of his
career. The electors adopted this attitude be-
cause, being in their right senses, they appreciated
that, under Australia’s present Parliamentary
system, no home-bursting or anarchy could occur
without their approval. And it is the same to-day.
Certainly, if the Commonwealth Government is
granted the amendments it asks for, it will be able
todomany things it cannot doat present. Going on
the Privy Council’s decision in Canada’s case and
the High Court’s judgment on ours, it is unlikely
that, even if Federal legislators wished to, they
would be legally permitted to do the astounding
things, in the way of “ turning the industries of
the Commonwealth upside down ” (to use a recent
Sydney Telegraph phrase), that the State
Frights party says they want to. 1. he trade and
commerce power, as interpreted by the hignest
authorities, does not include the power to regulate
the details of trades. What sane person believes,
anyhow, that the Federal Arbitration Court would
investigate or attempt to investigate an industrial
trouble “ not extending beyond half a dozen mem-
bers of the Bottle Accumulators’ Union, Sydney
—a possibility dangled before State Frightism by
the Sydney Telegraph last Saturday ! But even
assuming an affirmative vote meant that the Aus-
tralian Parliament acquires a free license to
control not only all Australian industries, but, in
addition, every other detailof social life, what then !
It signifies no more than that the Federal Houses
would be invested with the same powers the State
Houses have held since they came into being.
According to the anti-Referendaline of reasoning,
N.S.W., and every other State, lays itself open,
at every general election, to be burgled, devas-
tated and rendered a laughing stock in the eyes of
tho nations. That is to say, at every election it
puts in the hands of its Wades, Woods, Holmans
and McGowens practically unlimited legislative
power. For over half a century N.S.W., like
every other State, has been in the grim position
that, at any moment, its Parliament might
suddenly pass legislation forcing every inhabit-
ant of the State to keep, under penalty of
death, a pet bloodhound, dressed in oilskins.
The N.S. W. Assembly is at liberty, if it feels that
way, to order Sydney to be razed to the ground.
It could declare the excited but innocuous Lee an
outlaw, and have his fellow-agitator, Joe Cook,
drawn and quartered for dropping his aspirates
in public places. Parliament has never done any
of these things. Of course, it never will. The
country possesses the surest of buttresses against
bad, mad or anarchical legislation, namely, the
fact that its electors, being sane, do not send
demented legislators to represent them in Parlia-
ment.
• * * *
Consider the measures which the Fisher
Government is asking for power to put on the
Statute Book. They are so little revolutionary
or evil, in themselves, that ex-Minister Garland,
of N.S. W.— one of the few State Right spokes-
men who have abandoned vague howls about
“ wrongs” and “robbery,” and handled the sug-
gested amendments more or less on their merits
—had this to say concerning them, last week :
They [the proposed amendments] consisted, firstly,
of “ power to give effect to the New Protection policy.”
He was a Freetrader, but if they must have heavy Pro-
tective duties, stuffing the pockets of manufacturers with
money taken from the people to the detriment of the
latter, then he believed in the manufacturer’s employee
getting a fair share of that money—a fair and reasonable
wage. If, in these circumstances, the New Protection
were a separate issue at the Referenda, he ventured to
think it would be carried by an overwhelming majority.
The second requirement of the Federal Government, as
stated by Mr. Hughes, was power over “industrial legis-
lation to cover those matters only partially covered or
altogether neglected by State legislation.” In so far as
giving the Federal,Arbitration Court power to make a
Common Rule, here again he was solid behind Mr.
Hughes; it was only an act of justice. Thirdly, Mr.
Hughes told the State Cabinet that with regard to the
new powers asked for in connection with trade and com-
merce and corporations, “no class of measures not
covered by existing powers is contemplated.” Then why
ask for further powers}
Even Garland, the Freetrader, approves of the
New Protection policy ; and Garland, the anti-
Socialist has nothing against the Common
Rule principle. But he jibs at the Trade and
Commerce clause, and cries, wildly : “Why do
they ask for these further powers ? ” The answer
to a query which is depressingly common, and
which would not be put by one wdio understood
the rudiments of the Referenda case, is that,
under the High Court's decision on the Excise Act,
neither the New Protection nor the Common Rule
may become operative unless the powers asked for
in connection with trade, commerce and corpora-
tions are also granted to the Federal Par-
liament. The State Rights party has been
moaning for days past that the Federal
Government lumped the Referenda questions
together, in an impudent attempt to bull-
doze voters and force those who approved of
the New Protection or Common Rule or both to
also support the hated Commerce Clause, or get
nothing. It was cited as yet another instance of
Labor’s arrogant, coercive temper. This paper
was inclined to think, at first, that herein “ Liber-
alism ” was deliberately slandering the Fisher
Government for party purposes. Garland’s
ingenuous bewilderment, above noted, suggests
that it was not so. Evidently the moans were
uttered not in malice, but in ignorance. State-
Frightism simply doesn’t know its own trade.
The whole situation, in regard to the Referenda
questions, may be stated briefly as follows : Aus-
tralia has an opportunity to give effect to legisla-
tion which, even anti-Laborites recognise, will
ensure a fair wage for workers in every industry
which benefits under the existing tariff. The jus-
tice of that principle is so plain that while the
New Protection proposals were before Parliament,
the most irreconcilable Freetrade papers spoke
vehemently in favor of it. A chance is provided
to for ever scotch the Trusts with which America
is crippled and with which this country is threat-
ened. (A cable of last Thursday mentioned that
America viewed “ with envious admiration,” the
Commonwealth Anti-Trust legislation—the legis-
lation which, though, seemingly, America doesn’t
know it, the High Court has declared ultra vires,
and which must remain so unless the Referenda
questions are carried.) The Federal Arbitration
Court is offered a chance to be of real service to
the nation. In the event of a negative answer to
the Government’s proposals, manufacturers may
continue to enjoy the benefits of the tariff, while
their employees will be liable not to bene-
fit at all, but rather, in many cases, to
be prejudiced by it. Inequality of indus-
trial conditions must arise; one result of
which will be to set inter-State freetrade at
naught. Brown, of Albury (N.S.W.), whose em-
ployees’ wages have been fixed by his State’s
Wages Boards at 455.. will go down (commercially)
before Smith, of Wodonga (Vic.), who only has to
pay his men 40s. And the language discharged
by the “ wronged ” centre at its rival across the
border will be awful. The only coherent objection
to the Australian Government’s proposals that
has been adduced so far is that, in the event of
the Common weal tli being given the powers it
wants in regard to trade and ciimmerce, it may do
something inequitable or. villainous to the States.
Put otherwise, Australia is asked oy local
“ Liberalism ” to reject laws which only last week
Britain’s leading Liberal paper described as “ les-
sons full of instruction for the United Kingdom,
lest the Fisher Government should use them to
“rob” and “plunder” Australians who have put
the FiSHER-ites where they are, and who, assum-
ing the latter behave like lunatics or criminals,
are at liberty to throw them out of public life for
ever. As a working Menace, it is on a par with
W. Elliott Johnson’s assertion during the
“Liberal” campaign against the Australian navy
scheme, that “if an Australian fleet came into
being its guns would be turned on the Mother-
land’s vessels ” —an argument which would be an
insult to the intelligence of a kindergarten class.
HONEST FOOD AND HOW ONE CITY IS
TRYING TO GET IT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Adelaide is lucky in many ways. Colonel Light
took the “office” from Nature, and established
the capital on a site which charms every visitor.
That is good. But there is something better.
The site and the design lend themselves with rare
facility to a faultless system of sanitation. That
is excellent. But there is something better still.
Adelaide is a city whose skirts trail wide sweeps
of lawn, color-splashed by gardens. That is de-
lightful. But there is something even more im-
portant still : Adelaide’s fine effort to secure pure
food for its citizens.
* * * *
The food adulterater is a contemptible and
cowardly thief. The burglar gives you a sport-
ing chance of catching him. He is a brute of
audacious enterprise. But the man who waters
your milk, “doctors” your bread, or crams a
pound of butter with hidden lies is a mean,
sneaking rascal who robs the invalid in the hos-
pital and the baby in the cradle. Adelaide was
late in beginning its campaign against this reptile.
For one tiling, the business had not been quite as
bad there as in many other places; indeed, in
many directions the record had been singularly
happy. But Adelaide had not been free from the
fraud by any means, and the crime was growing.
So the authorities decided to delve seriously into
the iniquity of food-faking. In 1908 the Govern-
ment introduced a Food and Drugs Bill, to repeal
existing laws which for many years had been as
dead as Scrooge. The officials could have been
as dead as him, too, for all the influence they had.
Yet, in the face of this historic farce, the Govern-
ment measure actually provided for “ voluntary”
co-operation on the part of local bodies. Dr.
Borthwick, Officer of Health, and Mr. T. E.
Ellery, Town Clerk, of Adelaide, long-sighted
and long-headed men, saw that it would not do.
Very few do anything voluntarily when it hurts ;
and, as this was going to hurt, the law required
provisions with hooks on them. Dr. Borthwick
and Mr. Ellery seized the skirt of opportunity,
and, with the powerful aid of the Adelaide
City Council, got the voluntary clauses
knocked out; and, before many were aware
of it, Adelaide and suburbs had been fixed with
an amendment embodying the principle of com-
pulsion. Most of the local institutions had been
asleep for 20 years, and they woke up with a
yawn. That was the birth of the Metropolitan
County Board, and Dr. Borthwick and Mr.
Ellery are its proud parents. Who is the father
and who the mother need not be fought out.
There is the child —16 months old, a healthy, pro-
gressive, useful infant.
* * * *
There was no time to lose, and none was lost.
The baby was given a sound constitution. An
advisory Board of technical officers met and fixed
standards. Each member has special qualifica-
tions. There is the chairman of the Central
Board of Health, the Professor of Chemistry
at the University of Adelaide, the Gov-
ernment Analyst, the chairman of the Health
Committee of Adelaide, and three other com-
petent men. The County Board’s field of
authority extends over a 10-mile radius from
the Adelaide Town Hall, and its personnel
is comprehensive enough to give parochial and
vested influence no chance. There is no loop-
hole for the subtlety of personal influence or preju-
dice. That trouble may easily happen with a small
committee ; but this Adelaide body consists of 22
men, representing various districts with various
views about various people and various things.
Then, again, it has been found that the district
delegates have become red-hot champions of the
movement. They have gone out into the little
world of South Australia —east, north, west and
south—preaching the gospel, crusaders for pure
food, Davids using the sword against the Goliath
of adulteration. Inspectors, officers, well-equipped
laboratories and an ambitious programme—the
new machinery started to thump. What has been
done has only emphasised the need to do things
still undone. And the record shows with what
earnestness, and with what zeal, the Metropolitan
Countv Board and its officers have worked.
Standards have been fixed for milk, butter,
margarine, ice-cream, cream, bread, cakes,
cheese, meat, flour, jam and marmalade, biscuits,
pastry, meals, infant food, fruit, jelly, crystals, pre-
served fruit, dried fruit and vinegar. And, later
on, other things will challenge attention—aerated
waters, confectionery, coffee, cocoa and temper-
ance drinks. A spacious net of supervision this,
and so fine in the mesh that only very acrobatic fish
can get out of it. Now, those standards mean
simply that you shall get what you pay for. If the
chemist in the laboratory proves that you are not
getting it—well, then the vendor finds that when
he sold his fake he bought a deal of trouble at the
same time.
* * * *
Not yet, but by-and-bye, the authorities intend
to deal with everything you swallow. When you
read the results of analyses, it makes you shudder
to think what you have passed down your throat
since the faker got abroad in the land—so many
frauds that we can thank Heaven we started off
scratch with our mothers anyway ! The most
elusive food-thief is the milk vendor; and Ade-
laide quickly found it must get a motor-car to
catch him. Dishonesty goes on fleet foot, and the
inspectors can tell of many exciting captures of
these offenders against the confidence of the pur-
chasing public. Samples are bought from the
carts every day, Sundays included. The munici-
pal policeman carries a supply of small bottles.
He takes out three, and has them filled from the
big can. The milk seller may have one for a check
analysis if he pleases. Another is sent to the
Government analyst, the third is kept in
the office. Of course, the milk adulterator
usually says he is as innocent of fraud as
the baby he is starving. But, humanly
speaking, the analysis cannot lie; and there
are the figures. Then excuses trip on the vendors’
tongue. The cow was not up to the mark, and he
had been going to get rid of her for a long time.
Or a boy had turned the tap into the can while he
(the vendor) was in the hospital—and he never
explains how the boy learned the trick. Occa-
sionally the dairyman stands up and argues that
the analyst is a liar. A fine settles the dispute.
Under the County Board law £2O is asked for the
first offence ; after that, £5O and £lOO. If the
initial fraud be a particularly flagrant one, the
magistrate may make the fine £5O, and the next
time the milkman goes to gaol for six months to
think it over. Where there has been a second
conviction the name of the individual is pub-
lished right through the metropolitan area
as a malefactor. And this means that 180,000
people or more, scattered over 155 square
miles hear about him. It is the brand of
Cain. This step has already been taken in one
instance, following a heavy fine. The policy of
“no quarter ’’has had excellent results. Before
the County Board began its campaign for honest
food, milk adulteration, fed by official ineptitude
and administrative inefficiency, was common.
Now, according to the chief inspector: —
In no metropolitan area of equal population in any
part of the world is a purer milk supply, as a whole,
guaranteed to the consumer than is supplied in this
Metropolitan County District.
There is every reason to believe that these big
words are true. The authorities go back to the
source. Dairies are inspected and kept up to the
mark. The Board has the handle of the whip—it
issues the licenses. The habits and history of the
vendors of the milk are noted, and a person with
a communicable disease is promptly dismi se t
from the business. No detail is too small, since t
relates to the people’s food. In the first year of
the Board’s operations 1800 inspections of dairies
were made. It was a busy 12 months for dairy-
men. This year the chief inspector has a bolder
scheme still. He is going to make a bacteriolo-
gical examination of the milk. As a matter of
fact, arrangements have been made already for
examining it for tubercle bacilli. Dr. Borthwick’s
object is to secure such a supply as shall be safe
enough without the pasteurisation and sterilisa-
tion which destroy useful bacteria. An eye is
kept on all consignments of country cream and
butter. As a rule, the merchants heartily co-
operate, and say where the stuff comes from ;
and, hand in hand with a powerful body like the
Central Board of Health, whose-operations ex-
tend far afield, no time is lost in tracing the sup-
pliers who are in need of a few earnest words of
advice. Samples are constantly being obtained,
and these tell the tale. Butter or cream obviously
"off color” is promptly destroyed.
« * * *
Practically the same vigilance is applied to
other food and drink stuffs—meat, fruit, fish, ice-
cream, sweets, beer, whisky, and even Judkins’s
lemonade. The municipal policemen are always
on the beat. No warning is given. The regula-
tions and the date when they will come into
operation are notified, and that is all. The man
who has just bought a pound of sausages may be
an inspector for all the butcher knows. Slaughter-
houses, butchers’ shops and carts, produce markets
and cold stores, ice-cream carts, hotels, jam and
sauce factories, restaurants, grocers’ shops, fruit
shops—the inspectors go to all these places and
more, fossicking after impurities in the people’s
food, buying samples and taking them to labora-
tories.
* * * *
The picture is not all drab. There are bright
patches. On the whole, the campaign has not
been extensively resisted. The County Board
showed it meant business, and, if necessary, fight;
and, after inspecting its powers, the vendor of
foods and drinks mostly saw that for the future it
would pay him to go straight, just as in the
past it may have paid him to go crooked.
Take a few of the analyses, and the tale
need not be adorned. The adulteration of
milk is becoming very rare. Last year no bad
marks could be found against butter, sweets,
spirits and glucose. Of 14 specimens of olive oil,
only one gave evidence of interference. The
County Board has conquered, and is still conquer-
ing ; and its record is beckoning other cities to
come along the same road.
E. W. P.
LABOR IN MAORILAND.
In spite of the long-announced downfall of
Joseph Ward in the 1911 election fight, the
M.L. Premier departs Coronationwards with
Findlay, his chief lieutenant, in that tran-
quil frame of mind familiar only to those
who have at odd times had a “dead bird”
in their possession. Joseph has just completed a
stump of the country waving his new 10 U’s; he has
assured the public that the finances are sound, that
Cohenstein is as affable as of yore, that the
bridge across the creek will be built before the
month is out, that the recent mists of depression
have fled before the rising Loan, that the winter
is over, and the time of the singing of birds is
near, and the voice of the turtle dove will soon
be heard in the land. And the public has
risen from the banquet table, and, witli
champagne in its voice, given three cheers at the
request of the mayor. Meanwhile, Plain Bill
Massey, the leader of the Opposition, is just the
same old Bill, and politically more plain than
ever. His method of fighting the Government is
about as encouraging and definite as knocking
nails in with the butt-end of a sausage ; also the
chance of his mostly-Tory gang again getting
power is as remote as the green hills of Sheol.
Meanwhile, too, Tommy Taylor, thernan who could
probably harass Premier Ward more than any-
body else, has not only taken off his coat to flail
the traducers of Joseph, but has given his word
that, unless Massey complies with certain de-
mands, that gentleman will be politically flayed.
* * * *
There remains only to be considered the position
of direct Labor. The strongest Labor body in the
Dominion at the present time is the Federation of
Labor, composed of the powerful shearers’ and
miners’ unions, and various of the hitherto
scattered and independent trades and labor coun-
cils ; and it is frankly opposed to both Ward
and Massey. What this new' body expects to
do at the coming elections it would be interesting
to know ; but by all the signs it will be nothing to
whoop about. The Federation appears to have
got into the control of some local Petek Bow-
lings, who pursue the millennium with vapor and
other intangible weapons. One of their first
demands was “ a new Constitution giving the
workers of this Dominion full power to control
their own affairs and to meet the needs of their
own life.” Their official manifesto issued last
August concluded :
With Karl Marx, we believe in fighting for the im-
mediate aims, for the enforcement of the monetary inte-
rests of the working class, and, in that bitter stiug = le of
the present, take care of the future.
The president of the Federation agrees with P.
Bowling that, “ had the workers been organised


upon class-conscious lines, making an injury to
one an injury to all, the Newcastle coal-
strike would not have lasted one week.
He says that the need of the present is “a Par-
liament of industry,” whatever that may be, and
the building up of a federation of all labor that
will reflect the intelligence of the working class
a federation that aims first at the education of the
working class in the principles of industrial
unionism and economics. Without an educated
working class, he declares, sound organisation is
impossible; and to this end the M.L. Federation
has purchased much literature on industrial
unionism and economics to distribute amongst
workers as a brain tonic. Said President Webb
recently :
Once the masses think on class fines, it’s good-bye to
craft unionism and Arbitration Court, along with the
capitalist system which produces them. The so-called
Labor leaders will be the servants, not the masters, of
the Labor organisations, and the intelligence of the
thinking mass must and will rise in rebellion against the
chaotic form of Labor organisation which now prevails.
Which may or may not be correct, but it is vague
and unreliable —like M.L.’s Dreadnought, sta-
tioned at Hongkong, will be when war is ham-
mering with both hoofs at the front door of the
Dominion. The dream of a Socialistic Common-
wealth may come in time if the gods and men work
for it, but it is not to be captured by throwing
verbal chaff on the out-going wind. In fact, it
will be found at right angles to Dreamland, at the
end of a long,long road, and the things that
block the way will take a lot of steady shifting.
And the patriot who comes armed, like the M.L.
Labor Federation appears to be, merely with a
vague, high-flown remark, won’t shift much. The
Labor movement in Australia, which is shap-
ing to be the most important in modern
political history, is essentially practical, practi-
cable and fairly simple in its methods. It could
be improved, of course—but not by the views that
Peter Bowling has recently been hawking for
the amazement of the credulous and innocent
Maorilander.
* * * *
All of which is by way of showing that the forces
presently to be opposed to I O U. Ward are not
such as to keep him awake after normal hours
wondering whether the end of 1911 will find him a
broken political jug that went to the pawnshop
once too often. It is perfectly safe to say that
Joseph will still furthur help to wear down the
familiar step at Cohen’s door before the M.L.
Labor party evolves itself into a solid organisa-
tion, with a simple but effective pledge and plat-
form, instead of sky rockets and colored lights.
Which is a great pity.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Some Oversea Opinions of Australian Labor
Legislation.
It is a habit of the local “Liberal” to represent
that vast masses of capital are continually being
diverted from Australia as a result of “ wild-cat
Labor legislation.” It is also claimed that the
Australian Labor party’s doings inspire “think-
ing foreigners” with contempt and distrust.
These large, vague assertions are, in the nature
of things, difficult to rebut. The seeker after
details concerning some specific case where
the Commonwealth was deprived of capital
owing to the machinations of Labor, is gener-
ally referred to a letter which appeared
in Melbourne Argus or Sydney Telegraph
signed “Union Jack” or “ Clapham Junction,
wherein the writer mentioned that he had arrived
in Australia with the object of investing a million
sterling, but, solely owing to the Labor Party s
insensate conduct, had decided to hurry back to
London and take his cash with him. Satisfactory
proof of the oversea nation’s contempt for Aus-
tralian legislation is equally hard to secure. I he
oversea nations never by any chance mention it.
On the other hand, the papers constantly publish
items like these, which appeared in the cable page
of the Australian metropolitan dailies last Tnurs-
day:
London, Tuesday.—Admiral Fremantle, speaking at
the .Navy League dinner, said that Great Britain should
emulate Australia and Maortiand, and secure British sea-
men tor British ships, even at the cost of fewer ships and
some restraint of trade.
Ottawa, Wednesday.—A sensation took place in the
House of Commons last night during the debate on
reciprocity. . . ,
Mr. Clifford Sifton, an ex-Mimster in the Laurif,r
Cabinet, formally abandoned the Liberal party. After
quoling Australian action against the Meat Trust
and pointing out that Canada was exposing herself de-
liberately to similar dangers, Mr. Sifton declared that
the Prairie West was being made Chicago’s back yard.
New York, Tuesday.—The determination of the Com-
monwealth to discourage the American Meat Trust is
viewed with envious admiration by the Americans wdio
are powerless in the hands of the Trust.
As recently as last Federal elections the Liberal
party laid it down that if the Laborites continued
to insist on living conditions and a wages schedule
in the Australian coastal trade, designed to
attract white men of British extraction,
the United Kingdom would regard this coun-
try as hurrying to its doom. For 10 years
Australian Laborites have advocated the employ-
ment of none but white men on ships trading
between Australian ports; and for 10 years
“Liberalism” has barracked for Lascars and
Seedy boys—in the name of England ! And
now it is an English Admiral, possessed of special
knowledge, who tells the indigenous “Liberal”
that he is an ass. The Labor party’s anti-Trust
legislation has always caused “Liberalism” par-
ticular apprehension. It was going to do Aus-
tralia more harm in the way of demonstrating to
foreigners that this country was quite mad than
the Graduated Land Tax itself. The American and
Canadian viewpoint is that they wish to Heaven
they had an instalment of the alleged Curse
themselves. It is not too much to say that
Australia’s efforts to deal with the Trust evil have
given the Commonwealth the best advt. it has
ever had in the U.S. In the last few months the
British Government has adopted, practically in
its entirety, Australia’s Alien Immigration Re-
striction Act —the measure which evoked the
Petriana lie and which George Reid pronounced
“a blot upon civilisation.” According to the
newly-arrived Admiral King-Hall, the authori-
ties at the British Admiralty and War Office have
“nothing but admiration for Australia’s plucky
and self-respecting determination to defend itself
on land and sea.” Nor did he so much as hint at
the "Liberal” defence alternative of hired
British ships and an occasional presentation
Dreadnought. It was said of Oliver Cromwell
that “he first made the British navy feared
abroad.” Similarly, the Australian Labor party
may claim that it has provided the first Govern-
ment whose measures won the “ envious admira-
tion ” of foreign countries.
A N.S.W. ’‘Argument” Against the
Referenda.
A true-blue Geebung erupted in the Sydney
press last week as follows:
A large proportion of the Labor party will vote “ No ”
to those questions that affect State Rights. The incident
over the Federal loan business showed them that Victoria
was first in the field in grabbing what she could of the
loan money available. I do not blame her either, for she
is playing for keeps, which is one of the first laws of
nature, and we should do the same. But we will not be
doing it if we surrender our State Rights.
The grotesque suggestion that N. S. Wales was
“wronged” because Victoria burst into the
Federal Mont de Piete a pace or so ahead of her,
would be negligible were it not apparent
that the “Victorian oppression” gag is to
be used freely by the baser sort of N. S.
Wales “Liberal” during the Referenda cam-
paign. Already the Ma State Storeys, Lees and
Onslows are wailing about the possibility of
“Melbourne domination”; and they forecast
“ruin” for their State if anything of the sort
comes to pass. It is an ancient and utterly dis-
credited line of argument. Old John Robertson
originated the gospel of fear and hatred of Vic-
toria nearly half a century ago. In those days the
patriotic Sydney importer spat with fury when a
Victorian entered the Reform Club. Various
N.S.W. politicians carried on the evil old tradi-
tion. It reached its full expression during the
fight for Federation. Jack Want described
the inhabitants of Victoria as " thieves and
wolves.” In George Reid’s famous contest with
Barton, East Sydney was placarded with posters
stating that Reid’s pro-Bill opponent had been
“bought with Victorian gold.” The Sydney
Telegraph, in a leading article, propounded the
view that “Victorians were only united in the
desire to plunder N. S. Wales.” These slanders
undoubtedly won a lot of votes for the anti-
national cause. And they are expected to do it
again. A decade ago the State Fright alarmist
cried that N.S.W. would be ruined if Federation
came to pass. Now he howls that N.S.W. will
be ruined unless the Referenda questions are
negatived. Now, as then, there is a complete
absence of details as to how the catastrophe is
going to occur. In a flood of whirling words
the Wades and Woods lay it down that, if the
Referenda are carried (1) the Victorians of the
Australian Parliament will be invested with cer-
tain vague, new “powers” to harm N. S. Wales;
(2) that they will instantly use them. On pre-
cisely similar lines 11 years ago the anti-National-
ists of N.S.W.—the identical people who are now
fighting the Referenda proposals—predicted ruin
to N. S. Wales at the hands of Victoria, if Feder-
ation was accomplished. And this is what the
S. M. Herald (13/2/T1) had to say concerning their
prophecies:—
N.S.W. is enjoying a period of vast material progress.
Every test that can be applied proves this. The returns
of oversea trade, the inter-State movements, the expand-
ing railway earnings, the record bank’s clearing-house
figures, the reports of our industrial undertakings, the
volume of business on the investment side of the stock
and share market which has never been larger, the out-
put of our factories, the buoyant state of the labor
market, and the extreme difficulty in getting some classes
of labor at all, all point in one direction, and that is of
unexampled progress.
Nor do the most rabid of one-time anti-Federalists
question the truth of that assertion. The
N.S.W. elector should judge the N.S.W. State
Fright prophet of disaster on his past form. This
wordy fraud has foreseen the downfall of the
State, in every national measure that has been
proposed within living memory. Meanwhile, the
State has grown steadily richer. Not one of his
prophecies has been fulfilled. It is surely a fair
thing to conclude that none of the demented
tilings ever will be fulfilled—and to vote accord-
ingly.

The People Who Are Expected to be Grate-
ful for Being Used as Bait.
When Premier John Weary arrives back in Vic-
toria from his Coronation jaur.t—if he doesn’t
happen to stay iD the Ageut-General’s office—
there will be a pleasant surprise waiting for him.
The State elections will be drawing close ; and
one of the first noises he will hear from the deck
of the incoming steamer will be the speeches of
Labor candidates; and, with one accord, they will
be explaining how the State Land Tax came to be
imposed. So, as soon as John Weary lands, he
will have to get to work to explain matters and
answer questions; and some of the questions, like
those the Queen of Sheba put to Solomon
some time ago, will be very hard to answer.
There is no doubt that, when the Weary
Government imposed its land tax, it did
so with the deliberate object of putting a spoke
in the wheel of Labor. It was loudly avowed
that the tax was necessary on account sometimes
of the prodigality, and at other times of the par-
simony, of the Federal Labor Government. Then
the exemption was cut down to £250, so as to
catch almost all land-owners; but, as showing
conclusively that revenue was not really wanted,
the tax was, in a very off-hand manner, reduced
from a penny to a half-penny in the pound. The
best proof of the conspiracy, however, was the
haste with which theFatmen passed the measure.
Finally, after the Bill had been passed, the admin-
istration was made as irritating as possible. The
Federal Government announces that owners of
less than £3OOO worth of unimproved land-value
need not send in returns at all. But every
land-owner in Victoria has to fill up his
form and send it along, even though he owns
only a pocket-handkerchief allotment. Even the
trustees of cemeteries have to send in returns.
At first, as Premier Weary expected, the Labor
party was heartily cursed. But then the Labor
men began to have a few words to say ; and, as
time goes on, they will have many more things to
say. Especially they will ask what the land-
owner, and especially the small landowner, thinks
of a friend who will deliberately put a piece of
steel through his (the landowner’s) innards for
the express purpose of making him believe
that the injury was done by somebody else.
John Weary and his “Liberal” Government want
to catch the fish of office at the next elections;
and the Labor party will ask the small landowner
what he thinks of a “ friend ” who will put him,
wriggling, on the hook in order to land the fish.
For that is the position : The Weary Government
has jabbed the hook through the small landowner,
in spite of the protest of the Labor party, whose
members sought to raise the exemption. Now,
the small landowner is expected to be grateful to
the Government which used him as bait. Well,
John Weary will see when he comes back.
The Sanctity of Water-Melons.
The Fatman’s indifference to life and limb where
the sacred rights of Propputty are involved was
naively instanced in the N.S.W. law courts a few
days back. One Rowland, a mere lad, with some
other lads, one evening last year invaded the holy
domain of a Parramatta fruit-grower, with the
objectof “lifting” a melonortwo. Before they had
the chance of exploiting the melon patch, however,
Propputty was right on top of them with a loaded
gun. They ran off ; but Propputty, strong with
the instincts of his class that human life is a cheap
and paltry affair beside a melon or two, fired on
the retreating boys. Rowland received over
30 pellets in his legs and lower body, was taken
home by cart in a state of collapse,
and was for 14 days in hospital. Then Prop-
putty, to rub salt in the unfortunate lad’s
wounds, prosecuted him for trespass; whereupon
the magistrate, keeping sternly in view the
sanctity of water-melons, fined the lad 31s. 6d.,
with the option of gaol if he didn’t pay. As a
matter of law, the shooting was as entirely with-
out justification as the trespass; and Rowland
thereupon sued Propputty in the Supreme Court
for damages for his very serious injuries. At the
trial, Justice Pring told the jury that its task was
simply to assess the damages, as the plaintiff
legally was entitled to full compensation for his
injuries. But the jury, men of propputty (all
Supreme Court jurors are men of propputty),
still keeping in view the divine rights of water-
melons, awarded the plaintiff one farthing ! An
attempt was made, last week, to induce the Full
Court of N.S.W. to send the case down for a
re-assessment of damages on the ground that a
farthing was utterly incommensurate with the in-
juries inflicted ; but the Court, while frankly
admitting that Propputty had no shadow of right
for the shooting, refused to disturb the verdict.
So the boy Rowland has been shot down and also
fined 31s. 6d.; and itnowstandsforlawin N.S.W.
that the pettiest coin of the realm is ample com-
pensation for him. Years ago in England, by the
harshness of the law, 1 fe-transportation followed
as a matter of course acts of paltry theft. This
was properly abolished long since. But now a
propertied jury of N.S.W. has invented a scheme
of self-help in substitution. “Don’t rely on the
law,” it says ; “protect your pumpkins yourself
with a gun. And if you chance to shoot a man—
pooh ! Life and limb are very cheap. Keep a
farthing ready and we’ll stand by you 1 ”
The Enemies Within.
Frank Lundie, the thin piece of wire and whip-
cord who does most of the cracking for the
A.W.U. in S. Aus., got a very necessary lump of
ice down his back the other day. Lcndie’s idea
seems to be that it isn’t any use having Labor
Governments in office unless Laborites make the
pace hot while their rule lasts; consequently, he
and a few Other “Dreadnoughts” have been
shaking S. Aus. up, and doing things which they
wouldn’t have dared to do if Labor Governments
had not been in power. First, there was the
laborers’ trouble, over which they tied Verran in
a knot; then the carters’ trouble, in connection
with which there was a nearer approach to an-
archy than there has been in regard to any other
labor trouble in Australia for many years past;
and, finally, there were ructions between the Mil-
dura (Vic.) and Renmark (S.A.) fruit-growers and
their employees. It was in connection with this
last trouble that the ice was applied where it was
badly wanted. Since the dispute affected more
than one State, Justice Higgins, of the Common-
wealth Arbitration Court, summoned a confer-
ence. Lundie, allegedly for the Laborers’ Union,
answered exactly as he has been answering
Verran, the S. Australian Premier: “We will
settle quicker and more satisfactorily without your
aid,” with the additional information that they
wouldn’t send representatives. The ice arrived in
the shape of a telegram that Mr. Lundie and his
colleagues had better think over it, and if they
didn’t change their minds quickly a tine of £5OO
would follow for a start. It was so unlike the
reply that the S. Australian Government has been
in the habit of giving, that Mr. Lundie and his col-
leagues thought over it very promptly. Attorney-
General Hughes supplemented the ice with some
acid, and between the two it is to be hoped they
will mend Lundie’s ways, not only for his own
good, but for the good of the small tribe he repre-
sents. No sooner had McGowen become Premier
in N.S.W. than he had trouble with the same
kind of people. In that case Griffith did the
plain talking. Unfortunately, there was nobody
to do it in S. Aus. Verran is a weak man, and
Lundie and his little group knew it. The true
friend of the Labor party would have taken par-
ticular care not to expose that weakness by trad-
ing on it. But that would have called for
a larger view and more self-denial than they
were capable of ; they preferred to snatch a tem-
porary victory at the expense of the prestige of
Labor Governments.-* And their leader richly
merits all he has got from Justice Higgins and
the spokesman of the Commonwealth Govern-
ment. His own union might now appropriately
follow it up with some pointed words of counsel.
Manning the Australian Navy.
The Old Soldier’s views :
“ W.M.” (B. 23/2/’ll) makes remarks about the Aus-
tralian warships. He wants someone to suggest to Mr.
Feauck, the Minister for Defence, that the men, presum-
ably on board the new destroyers, should be given better
sleeping and eating quarters. He wants the quarters 10
times bigger, and 1000 times better ventilated. “ W.M.”
does not say where space is to be found for the enlarge-
ments. The modern warship is nothing but a big iron
box of machinery, always, in the nature of things, un-
comfortable to live in. In the British navy, crews of
destroyers, when they live on board, get an allowance
called “hard-lying money." There is good reason, no
doubt, for “W.M.’s” complaint; but it applies to all
modern warships. The space occupied on a, battleship by
the gun-mountings, which have to be built into the frame
of the ship, is enormous. Then there are the ammuni-
tion-hoists, one for each turret, and secondary gun-
positions, to secure the safety of the excessively-
dangerous ammunition on its way from the magazine to
the gun. On the larger ships the crews have certainly
more cubic space per man than on destroyers, but even
they are crowded. The ventilation difficulty is always
felt on modern warships. There are few or no portholes;
they weaken the sides. And it is still worse in action, be-
cause, as every pound of available power is then wanted
to drive the ship and work the armament, there is none
to spare for the air-pumps. It is quite possible that if aa
action were very severe and long-continued, someone down
below, especially among the sick and wounded, would be
suffocated before it was over. That, of course, would be
with things at their worst. Yet with things at their best,
the modern warship is not the sort of place to choose for
a permanent residence. It is built for fighting, not for
living in. Nevertheless, the inconvenience does not ex-
plain why only three men re-engaged out of 79 who had
finished their five years’ term of service here recently. If
all that “ W.M.” wants were provided, two more might
have engaged for another five years ; but it is unlikely.
No ; if the Commonwealth intends to do anything with
its navy it must provide depot ships or barracks for the
crews when they are not at sea, so that they can have a
good rest on shore between cruises, and it must also give
them a £lO or £2O bounty on re-engaging, and higher pay.
If it cannot make up its mind to that, the only thing is
compulsion—national service, in fact. Now, which will
it choose? National service means short service—three
years at most. The present volunteer folly means long
service, difficult recruiting and constant shortness of men,
unless high bounties are paid and costly privileges given.

Beer.
“ Curse o’ Moses ” reflects on humanity :
The word has gone forth that beer is to have its sting
drawn, and to make glad (or sad) the heart of man with-
out producing the malady which fills the all-night trams
with song. There is joy in Wowserdom over the an-
nouncement, and arrangements are being made to pro-
perly inaugurate the millennium which must follow.
Tankards will still foam and gilded locks bow over shining
beer-pumps ; but the devil in the cup will have taken his
way back to the eternal Tophet. In England, where the
purgation of the tipple is recorded as having begun, they
probably know all about it by this time. Already John
Smith is swearfully wondering what has happened to him
that his thirst should be so thoroughly quenched while
his soul remains sad. Unfortunately for the Wowser side
of the calamity, there is no living person who really likes
beer for its taste and color alone. If man were a wholly
natural animal, active, naked, hairy and unworried, he
would prefer plain water to every other drink. Unfor-
tunately, again, man is civilised. He puts himself under
a tremendous mental and nervous strain, in order to live
an altogether unnatural life, and every fibre of his being
cries out for something to ease the tension. Alcohol does
it at a price. The price is big, but he deliberately decides
to pay the bill. The truth which every Wowser misses,
and which every person who tries to pull the fangs of
beer will find himself up against, is just this : A reason-
able amount of alcohol makes a man feel exactly as he
ought to feel without it. The only line that the beer-
emasculator can safely take is to preach a gospel of pure
animalism. Let us abolish cities and farms, mines and
ships, schools and hash-houses, cleanliness and trousers,
and all the varied and complicated enormities that go to
make up civilisation, and go out to face nature with
neither shame nor pride. Then we may be able to untie
Civil War.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We are on the eve of a civil war.—B. Smith.
It is revolution.—Solemn Lee.
I heard a most terrific little row,
Making a gentle uproar on the heights
Of frighted balconies. I heard the jar
As of grey tomcats in tormented nights;
And someone mentioned that a civil war
Was then in progress. Horror, like a cow.
Sat on my chest and smothered all delights.
Clear were the 6kies, soft was the pleasant sea,
But all my heart was wrapt in horrent gloom ;
I dreamt of fields of death and vales of woe,
And thought to hear the awful cannon boom,
And brazen-throated trumpets start to blow
Like German bands gone mad. I thought to see
Men, like fat mutton, rushing on their doom.
A civil war ! Great Scott! My brothers bleed !
Somewhere the loud bolt shrieks along the sky.
The flames of burning cities, slaughter-red,
Lift their appealing clamor. Women fly
Affrighted through the havoc, and the dead
Are absolutely corpses. Gory steed
And mangled rider in red ruin lie.
Give me a sword ! A butcher’s knife will do.
Where is the battle, where the ensanguined row?
What is the racket over, and who wins ?
I must away to slay the blood-stained cow
Who smote my brother ! Our Australian sins
Are heavy on our shoulders. Strike me blue !
The mark of Cain is on the nation’s brow !
I followed that terrific little row
Until I found a place of swinging lights.
High-set on a suburban balcony,
And found a small collection of State Frights
Throwing some words at William Smith and me
Words that seemed weighty as the zealous wow
Of the house-poodle yelping e’er he bites.
The speakers did not fill me with much dread ;
Their angry words provoked a weary smile
From those who gathered round them. Though
they threw
Some rocks that missed Australia by a mile,
They seemed a rather tame and worn-out crew ;
And as I listened one dull speaker said—
“ This is a civil war.” And then I knew.
I knew it was a very civil war-
A war with mops against a rising tide ;
A war with broomsticks ’gainst a giant’s flail;
A war in which the only things that died
Were spite and malice, and some odors stale
Of long-departed fishes. Scratch or scar
The gods to such a battle had denied.
A civil war ! Great Scott! And this was all!
Fat’s last, lorn hirelings in their sorry plight,
Making a bluster and a dull pretence
Of keeping up an ancient parish spite
To save Australia from plain common-sense.
War ! Let the six-legged cockroach fiercely crawl
To battle with the snining stars of night!
N.S.W.
MARY McCOMMONWRALTH.
Room for the White Man For’ard?
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
London, Ist March.—At the Navy League dinner last
night Admiral Sir E. R. Fremantle said Great Britain
should emulate Australia and Maoriland, and secure
British seamen for British ships, even at the cost of fewer
ships and some restraint of trade.— Cable.
From th’ Thames to Madagascar,
An’ from Glasgow to ’Ong Kong,
We’re relyiu’ on th’ Lascar
For to shove th’ ships along.
’E will ’andle coals or cargo,
’E will polish brass or plates,
From Colombo to Otago—
An’ ’e wants no White Man’s rates.
Is there room for th’ White Man for'ard ?
Is there room for an ’Art of Oak ?
For th’ smelt of th’ nig is ’orrid.
An’ ’c isn’t a decent bloke.
’K ain't no good when there's trouble,
For 'e ain't got any nerve !
Will yer send ’im ’ome at th’ double.
For a reel White Naval Reserve !
When lead flies th’ nig’sa starter
For the bunkers or the bush,
For ’e ain’t no bleedin’ martyr ;
So ’e never needs no push
When th’ friends of ’is good bosses
Starts to give ’is bosses ’ell;
’E just jabbers to ’is josses
An’ goes ’omeward like a shell.
Is there room for th’ white man for’ard l
Is there room for an ’Art of Oak l
Me sea-kit ain’t much, ’cause it’s borrurcd,
An’ me family plate’s in soak;
Th’ Admiral says that yer need me —
Which I’m willin’ to bet is true.
I’ll sign on if yer'll pay an’ feed me,
An’ I’ll fight later on for you!
N.S.W.

Every day the man on the land tends to become
a man on or at a machine ; and the time is coming when
he will need to be an engineer, as well as a farmer. It is
the oil engine that is working this tremendous revolution ;
and already it has lifted a good deal of the heavy drudgery
the tiller of the soil had to tackle. For farm or station
simplicity of construction should be the keynote. Good
results cannot be expected from mechanical puzzles; it
annoys any man to find that he has purchased a box of
elusive tricks instead of an easily managed engine.
Steadiness, reliability and endurance ; these you must
get, and one great safeguard is to have an Australian
guarantee. All theserequirementsaremetby the engines
supplied by the Invincible Motor Construction Co. Ltd.
As to simplicity—anyone can operate these engines. As
to strength—their guaranteed horse-power is much below
their actual power. As to the guarantee—the great
Works of the Invincible Co. at Woolwich, Sydney, stand
behind the Five Years’ Guarantee given by the co. A five
years’ guarantee remember, given by the leading Aus-
tralian concern in this line.
Write for illustrated lists. Invincible Motor Construc-
tion Co., Ltd., Works Woolwich, Sydney. Showrooms,
35 fitt Street, Sydney. And at Melbourne and Adelaide.*

Now that the premature retirement of Lord
Dudley from the position of Governor-General is
definitely announced, and previous contradictions
of rumors are proved to have been mere coquetting
with the truth, it seems reasonable to ask that
future occupants of the most highly-paid office in
the Commonwealth shall be required to take it
seriously. The reasons, genuine or otherwise,
given for Lord Dudley’s retirement are very
good reasons why he should never have
been appointed. We are gravely informed
that he and his wife “ naturally desire to be near
their children, who are about to resume their
schooling in Great Britain.” Obviously, an earl
whose young children are bound to be educated in
Britain, and who wishes to live within 12,000
miles of them, is unfitted for an appointment to
five years of Governor-Generalling in Australia.
Such a one comes here on a political mission with
the certain knowledge that he will not wish to
fulfil it. All things considered, an Australian-
born Governor, possessed of an Australian wife,
would seem to be the only sort of Excellency
likely to regard this country as “home” for a
long period, and unlikely to be suddenly attracted
to Britain for domestic reasons. If an Excellency
is essential to the Constitution, his position is
worthy of being taken seriously. Therefore, the
practice of appointing figureheads who merely
desire to put in two or three years “ abroad,” and
are humming “Home, Sweet Home” all the time,
should be promptly discontinued.
Melbourne Trades Hall Council struck an awk-
ward subject for discussion the other night. A
delegate moved: “That this Council gives its
unanimous support to the Shop Assistants’ and
Warehouse Employees’ Federation of Australia
(Vic. branch), in the endeavor to abolish trading
after 6 o’clock on Friday nights.” Let it be noted
that the S.A. and W.E.F., although affiliated
with the Trades Hall, doesn’ttalkof bringing strike
force to bear on the employers of shop and ware-
house labor. It merely asks for the support of the
Trades Hall in an endeavor to procure the rule of
6 o’clock closing on Fridays, the day when shop-
keepers are authorised by law to keep their em-
ployees at work until 10 p.m. And the Trades
Hall Council, apparently, is indisposed to give the
support asked for. Thus unionism is in conflict
with unionism. The Labor which stands solid in de-
fence of the Eight Hours principle candidly admits
itself unfavorable to that principle where other
sorts of Labor are concerned. Workers who
claim a monopoly of the title protest that exces-
sive labor for shop hands, on at least one day a
week, is necessary to the convenience of fac-
tory hands and other workers. They would
thus sacrifice Peter to suit Paul. In short,
they are now confronted with the fact that,
whereas trades unionism has hitherto professed
anxiety to extend the eight-hours principle to all
branches of labor, and has sneered loudly at the
black-coated and “ genteel ” worker who remained
outside the Trades Hall sphere of influence,
they are not actually prepared to do unto their
black-coated brother as they do unto themselves.
To tell the bald truth, Labor— i.e., the Labor
which in Melbourne returns members to Parlia-
ment —brooks no interference with its personal
convenience. At the Trades Hall the other night
it talked of “the needs of the people” in a
purely class spirit. It was thinking only of the
workers in trades who knock off work at 5 p.m.
They are the people for whom shop employees
must stick to an unhealthy occupation for 13 hours
on Fridays.
:
A few banks and other companies which make
all, or practically all, their profits in Australia
have their head-offices in London ; and some of
these concerns have lately developed a habit of
refusing to transfer shares from the London to
the Australian register. The real objection, no
doubt, is, not to Australian shareholders, but to
the possible loss of control and the pickings
which attach thereto. This is not a ques-
tion of compelling foreign shareholders to
sell to Australians. But if Australians,
among whom the profit is made, choose to pay
the price which English shareholders ask, then
no co. which is permitted to do business in
Australia should be allowed to practically refuse
to the Australian the right to buy. Australia has
money to invest now ; and, from the national
point of view, it cannot invest some of that money
better than in buying shares, the dividends on
which are sent out of the country. Every foreign-
held share means a debt by this country to some
other country ; and the purchase of that share by
an Australian means the extinction of that debt.
Any rule or regulation adopted by a private
co. to prevent that purchase is therefore an
attempt to keep this country in debt to the
foreigner, and to compel this country to earn
dividends not for its own people, but for
some foreign people. The Australian Govern-
ment has not the right to say to the com-
pany with a foreign registration: “You shall
transfer shares to an Australian register if an
Australian buys them.” But it has the right to
say: “ You shall not do business and make pro-
fits in Australia unless you establish an Austra-
lian register, and give Australians the right to
buy from the foreign shareholder and transfer to
the Australian register without restriction.” And
no Commonwealth Companies Act will be com-
plete unless it says that unmistakably.
Colonel Ryrie, the dull, uninteresting person
who is carrying the-half-mast flag of Toryism in
the North Sydney Federal by-election, asked a
meeting the other evening why the Commonwealth
should “control all labor and industry and leave
the State Government with nothing but the con-
trol of diseased meat, obscene literature and the
cemeteries.” if Ryrie had taken the trouble to
read the S. M. Herald that day he would have
been given one reason why Frights should
be permitted to administer only dead meat.
Referring to the desolation of 65,000,000 acres of
Western land, handed over at various times by
past “Liberal” Governments to their friends the
big grazing lessees, the paper said :
The carrying capacity of the country is small. It used
not to be so low, but the greed of the early lessees in
overstocking the country and their half-hearted attempts
to cope with the rabbit pest caused the disappearance of
some of the best fodder plants. And when grasses
and herbage became scarce, edible trees and shrubs were
ruthlessly cut down and left to rot, instead of being
lopped. Thus the soil, mostly of a sandy nature, lost the
factors that bound it together, and the extraordinarily
high winds that prevail at times in the west lifted the
soil in the air, converted level ground into a succession
of sand hummocks, which continually shifted according
to the strength of the wind. The so-called “ Darling
showers” were the outcome of the denudation of the land.
That is what the Frights party, of which Ryrie
is a representative, can do in a State Lands
Department when it is not hampered by an
overbearing, tyrannical national Parliament. And
that is only one instance in one department. The
other failures, and jobberies, and crab-walking,
and parochial meannesses, and utter futilities are
matters of history which Ryrie may read for hin? :
self if he is so ignorant that he does not already
know them.
