4.
Pinkney is manager of Durant’s steel
works, and not so much a villain —in the sense
the chronic melodrama-patron understands the
term —as a person of weak and reckless character.
By comparison with one of Bilanderson’s full-
blooded, gore-stained machinators, he is a mere
anaemic Wowser. Lieut. Sommers, naval officer
and inventor of the Sommers gun, resembles a
human being more than he does a stage hero.
They both love Frances Durant, who in no wise
differs from any other fair young girl in a hobble
skirt. Marsh is one of Durant’s employees, a
gun-inventor and a perfectly credible old toiler.
Pinkney has swindled Marsh out of 90 per
cent, of the royalties due on his invention,
assuming it proves a success ; but of this Marsh
is ignorant. The demon jealousy impels Pink-
ney to procure the ruin of Sommers’ gun in the
forging. Sommers suffers from the trouble which
afflicts lads of the hero breed wherever the drama
flourishes—namely, rank, blithering, chuckle-
headedness. Being warned that there is shady
work afoot, he turns up at Durant’s to see his
weapon forged. The man in charge of the job is
fighting-drunk, and ill fitted, in consequence, for
the deed of subtle villainy he has to perform.
But Sommers sees nothing. His gun is destroyed
before his eyes. All he achieves by his visit is
an absurd suspicion that the blameless Frances
has been misbehaving herself in a locked office
with Pinkney. The parties are next discovered
at Madeira. A Scotland Yard detective informs
Sommers that his gun has burst, killing and
maiming several innocent seamen. All hands start
aboard Durant’s yacht for England and an
“ enquiry.” How the yacht runs on a reef, how
her whereabouts are made known, by wireless, to
the Mongolian ; how that immense vessel steams
to within 3ft. of the wrecked passengers and hauls
them aboard with a rope, require to be seen to be
appreciated. Pinkney’s undoing and subsequent
removal by the Law occur in time to allow the
suburbanite two (2) alcoholic nerve-soothers before
flinging himself aboard his last tram.
“ VIA WIRELESS ,” AT SYDNEY HER
MAJESTY'S.
Ambrose Manning as
Marsh (in the toils
of the villain).
Eardley Turner as the
gun manufacturer and
kindly old father.
Such is “ Via Wireless,” which was pro-
duced at Her Majesty’s, Sydney, last Saturday
night. The writer regards it as a deliberate
attempt to undermine practically all the melo-
dramatic traditions that have come down to us,
through Bland Holt and his rivals, from London
Adelphi and the old Surrey-side playhouses. Not
a single revolver, howitzer, rifle or fowling-piece
is discharged in the entire show. The villain is a
non-smoker. The hero flaunts the deadly cigar-
ette in the eyes of his worshippers. There is no
cloaked, betrayed and distracted female; no angel
child ; no infuriated father avid for blood ; no lost
marriage lines, Faces at the Window, dying howls,
stabbings, poisonings or corpses in the limelight.
The old-time melodrama folk were unreal; taken
in conjunction with their surroundings they be-
came preposterous. Because the people in V la
Wireless” are natural, every-day folk, their strange
adventures become almost conceivable. As a re-
sult, there are thrills in the new show which even
the Sydney Telegraph's snapshots of niggers
being eaten by sharks cannot give. The gun-
forging scene, the wireless business on the Mon-
golian, and the representation of the wrecked
yacht are about the best things in their line that
have been done in Sydney. Miss Evelyn Kerry,
Miss Emma Temple, Miss Elsie Wilson, Leonard
Atwill, Vivian Edwards, Eardley Turner, Charles
Blaekall and Ambrose Manning are some of those
who help to make the production a success.
* * * *
The Bulletin’s remarks this week con-
cerning “ Our Miss Gibbs ” are penned by the
only man in the office who has not seen the lady ;
or, if he did see her it was away back when she
first appeared—so long ago that he has forgotten.
It is comparatively easy to remember ordinary
matters of history, such as Plutarch censuring
Herodotus for not having sufficiently boomed the
fame of some of the Grecian cities; the murder of
Amaziah, son of Joash, by the inhabitants of
Jerusalem ; or Xerxes lashing the Hellespont for
disobedience, and ordering for it a pair of hand-
cuffs or leg-irons or something; but even the
Oldest Inhabitant couldn’t be expected off-hand
to recall whether he had or had not at some
remote period seen Miss Gibbs. Anohow, there s
no hurry; and the writer intends to have a stall
one night about a fortnight after the referenda
are carried. By that time the last howl
of the scattered and overwhelmed State Frights
will have died away in the tall timber, and peace
will be upon the land. The advertisement an-
announces that the piece is “going for the second
century,” and if it was good enough to last one
century it ought to last another one. By then
The Bulletin man who hasn’t seen the show will
have repaired the oversight or died in the attempt.
* * * *
“The Belle of Brittany ” continues to draw
admirers to Sydney Criterion. Some meritorious
features which went unnoticed last week are the
male choristers, Miss Nelly Cavanagh, and two of
the fairest and finest equerries or principal boys
that the writer has ever seen gathered together on
a stage.
* * * *
Having been renovated, the Standard
Theatre (Sydney) was re-opened on Saturday
night as a variety hall, Strenuous advertising of
a “ shadow dance by lovely types of womanhood ”
was ho doubt largely responsible for the audience
that packed the building, and furtive whiskers
here and there proved that the Wowser had
rushed in where angels appeared for bed. But
long before the Wowser had arrived the police had
had a private view of the unrobed posings which
appeared in shadowgraph against the sheet, and
satisfied themselves that the only shock the
Wowser was likely to suffer from the exhibition
waS fhe shock of disappointment. There are
several novelties in the programme, and the
management promises to shake the old style of
variety entertainment into a new lease of life.
* * *■ #
Fasola, the eminent Indian lion-loser, still
heads Sydney Tivoli bill; and his smart perform-
ance would be more enjoyable than it is if he
would direct the orchestra to play with less vigor
or else order it to go out and have a drink. On
the night the Religious Editor attended, at least
one raw, hungry howl by the cornet or trombone
should have been no-balled. If the uproar is
necessary to drown the noise of illusion-working,
this paper will remorselessly deduct 10 per cent,
from its astonishment and 15 per cent, from its
applause. If some distracting sound is necessary
to the performance, the mellow-toned gong which
the gorgeous brown wizard hits when the most
important tricks are about to be completed, might
be smitten continuously. The brazen thing gives
forth soft, soothing vibrations, and does no violence
to the ear whilst the eye is being entertained.
Alexander Prince, the concertina artist; Cooper
and Lait, the musical comedy pair; Miss Odeyne
Spark, and Baby Watson are also amongst those
present.

An all-Australian show will be on view at
Sydney Criterion on the 30th and 31st inst.—
“The Grey Kimona,” a musical comedy with a
libretto by artist D. H. Souter and music by
F. Wynne Jones. R. B. Orchard is to play the
hero, with Miss Amy Murphy as his lady love.
Others in the cast are Miss Rosalind Smeaton and
Arthur Gibbs. Also, Miss Pressy Preston, who
was one of the most alluring features of J.C.W.’s
“ Hqmpty Dumpty” show and the sweetest of
Gretchens in “ Mother Goose,” is returning to the
stage in a part that should suit her.
* * * *
At time of writing, Spencer’s Theatre-
scope is still at the Pahice ; but the Lyceum, re-
juvenated and decorated, will reopen with a
matinee on Saturday. Among the star items are
“The Golden Supper,” a film in which beautiful
realism is said to have run mad. It certainly
is a very fine thing, and worth going far
to see. Napoleon flits across the uncertain
screen, and whatever faults he may have had,
Napoleon was never uninteresting. One is
brought back to the present by a fine film
of the launching of the new Dreadnought, the
Thunderer. There are the usual “comics,” and
the industrial side is represented by “ Karlsbad
and its Pottery Industry,” from which one learns
how the business has changed since the days of
Omar Khayyam, when the stuff was thumped
hard.
J * * * *
West’s Pictures, at Sydney Glaciarium,
boom with “The Sirens of the Surf.” People
who are under the impression that Australians
are not interested in things Australian can have
the truth made clear to them by blowing along to
the Glaciarium any night and listening to 4000
people deciding which is the prettiest of
the 30-odd belles that have been culled
from the beaches between Cronulla and Manly.
Writer frequently goes surfing, and sees a varied
assortment of beauty ; but, of course, in the surf
one never looks. When, however, the ladies
stand on a revolving pedestal by the seashore,
and allow themselves to be biographed—well,
what does one pay the price of admission for?
Although the Sirens of the Surf is the star film, it
is not the only one at West’s. The usual pro-
gramme is gone through; but the glory of the
beach-girls eclipses all else.

The amazing drama of “ Revenge,” dimly
remembered in Melbourne as one of Bland Holt’s
occasional excursions beyond the limit, was staged
at the King’s on Saturday by Max Maxwell.
Absurdity is clothed in military and naval
uniform in this story of foreign affairs. Inco-
herence wears a supposititious crown. Also, the
comic relief makes reference to the bond of lan-
guage between Britain and America, and a naval
captain does his courting under the ironical super-
vision of a seaman from the fo’castle, which
suggests H.M.S. Pinafore. There is nothing
to be said in favor of "Revenge ” except that it
doesn’t belong to the order of “bedroom dramas,”
so called, and has nothing pornographic in its
title. Seeing that two of the latest atrocities iu
British bellowdrama (not yet inflicted on Aus-
tralia) are “ The Midnight Sin ” and “ The
Soiled Shop Girl,” one may say that
"Revenge” is sweet. Some parts of the
performance are distinctly good as bellow-
drama work goes. Miss Beatrice Holloway
is an appealing little heroine; Mabel Russell

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
At the time of Stuff-and-Nonsense, in the land of
Lordknowswhere,
There schemed a burly Villain with pomatum on
his hair, ,
Who ran an insurrection, which occurred behind
the scene,
For the purpose of effecting his designs upon the
Queen.
His villainy was awful, and from nothing did he
shrink ;
He even put some lyddite in the captain’s pot of
ink.
The ink, of course, exploded, and the wretch
began to sing;
Now, wasn’t that a wicked plan to overthrow the
King?
The Captain’s wife was giddy, and he’d waxed
exceeding wild
On learning that the villain was the father of his
child
(A midshipmite in buttons, who had loved his
mother dear,
Till he heard behind a curtain that her character
was queer);
And when the ink explosion left bun sightless for
a time,
He swore to be Revenged upon the author Of the
crime.
His hate, alas! seemed hopeless, for he lived as
in a fog— .
He was just a Poor Blind Soldier mooching round
without a dog ;
Still the midshipmite adored him, and, amidst the
war’s alarms.
The Queen found time to nestle in her Poor Blind
Soldier’s arms.
True, she wasn’t yet a widow, but she hadn’t long
to wait,
For the Villain stabbed the King and took
possession of the State.
(Sing a song of sixpence, a pocketful of rye,
Four-and-twenty dramas baked in a pie !)
The Villain grew more wicked than he hitherto
had been,
He meant to kill the Captain in the presence of
the Queen :
But that humorous deceiver wasn’t blind, as you
might think;
His eyes had quite recovered from the fatal pot
of ink.
He could see to slay the Villain with pomatum on
his hair; '
Revenge was what he wanted, arid he got it then
and there.
And the Queen, whose gentle passion had been
modestly confessed,
Went and nestled more than ever on the Poor
Blind Soldier’s chest.

handles a difficult slie-boy character very expertly;

** * *
To-morrow (Friday) is the last of “The
Gay Grisette” Gordons at the Royal. Melbourne
will see the company never more, but it faces the
impending loss very philosophically, because the
other C. and M. opera co. will be back in the near
future. Meanwhile a serious-minded co. is to deal
with “ Lucky Durham ” for three weeks. H. R.
Roberts takes the name part in Wilson Barrett’s
play ; Miss Beatrice Day is the principal lady in
the case; and Hugh Buckler and others make up
a cast which is limited to 13 names. The fact
that “Lucky Durham” has the Devil’s number in
the cast possibly explains why it was Barrett’s
last play.

Every Wednesday and Saturday two per-
formances of the panto, transpire at Her
Majesty’s; on four other days Jack scales the
Beanstalk only once. On Sundays the Beanstalk
doesn’t grow at all. By the way, a recent Bulle-
tin par. was misprinted to say that the evening
curtain rose at 7.15, instead of 7.45, since when
the multitude has started to assemble at the doors
half an hour earlier than before. The Bulletin
gracefully acknowledges this tribute to a wide
circulation, but regrets the error which has
caused the multitude to bolt its evening meal.

The change of hellowdrama companies at
the .Princess’s has brought Melbourne tip against
the o d-istablished fact that “Men Love
Women.” A drama in sundry acts, staged last
Saturday, professes to explain why ; but its bear-
ing on the question is not specially direct. The
hero who gets “jugged ” for a murder he didn’t
perpetrate suffers his cruel fate at Simla, and a lot
of hot air from the Himalaya Mountains envelops
the proceedings in general. Incidentally it may be
remarked that the lady who wasn’t murdered has
really been poisoned, and is suffering from a mere
trance of death, which resembles the real
thing. Therefore, the hero is not hanged.
Nevertheless, he has a hard job to marry the
heroine, after all. She gave her vast fortune to
the Indian Famine Fund, with a view to securing
his hand, but a villian intervenes and the engage-
ment is nearly broken off by the hand of cruel fate.
Finally, the drama proves to its own fatuous
satisfaction that a properly-loved woman doesn’t
regret her sex. Hilliard Vox villainises with
lurid effect; Miss Frances Ross is the lone, lorn
spinster, reduced to impecuniosity for hero Wing-
field’s sake; whilst Cambourne and Miss Clara
Stephenson provide comic relief of a sort. The
mounting is mostly picturesque, and an Indian
potentate, who loves the heroine with a pure
brown passion, adds to the pomp and circum-
stance of an inane narrative.
** * *
A new arrival at the Opera House is Leip-
zig, a conjurer who doesn’t mix his legerdemain
with mechanical illusions. In a smaller building
Leipzig would be more strikingly clever, but
under present conditions his art of making things
disappear and reappear is very smart indeed.
The advertisement calls it “ incomparable,” any-
how. Six American Dancers are quitting the bill
this week, but Wild, Willie and West will
knock about till further orders. The three
silent navvies are droll by inference and
innuendo, as well as by direct’assault. Ethel Loflus
is another forcible success. Ethel has a loud,
hoarse, Cockney utterance, three clean, quaint
songs, and a cheerful sense of grotesque humor to
match her other possessions. Ethel, in short, is
a “ trick,” and a new variety of “ trick ” at that.
Lucia Collins passes as a decorative feature of
the show. Her voice lacks music, and her attem pt
to sing “ The Little Widow,” in imitation of her
late mother, Lottie Collins, is merely a reminder
that the widow of old wore deep mourning over a
red petticoat. But she has the family good looks,
and her millinery is poetic.
** * *
Another big drama of local manufacture
was tried at the Melbourne Glaciarium on Satur-
day last. This film biographs “ The Mystery of a
Hansom Cab” with due regard to the narrative’s
blood and thunder. Most of the episodes are
given in an inteiesting manner, and are
described by an assiduous person who trusts
little to the audience’s imagination. In-
cidentally the murder, which is supposed to
occur at one in the morning, is pictured at one in
the afternoon—a piece of excusable license on the
part of the adapters, who desired the benefit of
daylight to photo, the crime in the hansom cab.
The acting in the picture is generally good, and
makes dumb show look more like an art than an
aberration.
** * *
A pictorial version of “John Halifax,
Gentleman,” arrived at Melbourne Olympia on
Saturday. Scanned closely, the picture is better than
the novel, for it has the maximum of story with a
minimum of snuffle. All the principal incidents
in the book have been included, .and are acted
with character and force. The lady who plays the
daughter of Fletcher, the miller, is quite natural
and pleasing, Fletcher himself is vigorous and
human all the time, and John Halifax presents a
model of conduct that all love but few copy.

Max Maxwell has moved on from Adelaide
Royal. The co. has gathered in Miss Maud
Plunkett, a young and particularly gifted Ade-
laide amateur ; it is understood that she is to
have the reversion of Miss Strong’s female-viliain
parts, when that lady takes her lurid smile away
to the U.S.A., which is almost immediately.
Another horse-drama now holds the stage at the
Royal—“ The Chance of a Lifetime,” finely staged
by William Anderson, with the historic theft of
the Ascot Gold Cup as its central incident. The
Tivoli has a star turn in the football-playing dogs.
A strong co. includes Holt, who makes strange
imitative noises on an American basis; and the
juggling Martin, who also comes from the land of
Peary and Lynch. At West’s, the Beauty Com-
petition rages with unexpected fury. For the
first week, a youthful No. 17 went ahead by hun-
dreds ; then the public fancy seemed to change,
and a mature No. 15 is first into the fifth thousand.
Perhaps the farmers—in town, in myriads, for the
Show Week—prefer them maturer? The Empire
is relying largely on the pictured bushranging ex-
ploits of John Yane, while the Casino takes
lunch-time snapshots at busy corners, reproduces
them at night, and invites the public to come and
win prizes for recognising itself. Harold Hard-
wick is at the City Baths, Johnson and Jeffries
are fighting their shadowy battle at various
suburban halls, and—possibly as a counterblast—
Edward Reeves has recited “The Sky Pilot” for
the edification of the Methodist Conference dele-
gates.

O.K. : Already there is a quarrel over the first
appea rance of the flickergraph ; and it is even alleged
that Boer War Pictures were the earliest, or nearly the
earliest, put upon the screen in Australia. As a matter of
fact, the flickergraph was familiar to Australia in 1897,
when Rickards "starred” it as a principal turn, and
showed Queen Victoria’s Jubilee Procession, etc. It was
then said to have been going strong in London for the
previous six months as the Biograph, though the original
title of living pictures on the screen was Cinematograph.
Phil Newbury supplemented his shilling pops with a Bio-
graph in 1898. The Boer War didn’t start until October,
|99, and the first pictures taken in S’Africa were not shown
in Australia uruil some eight months later. Actually the
first cinematograph film shown in these parts was staged
by the McMahons; but the picture wasn’t magnified in
those days. Tbe same people, by the way, staged the
first phonograph—in 1890. It was a very spluttering and
cranky work of Edisonian art, worked in conjunction
with an explanatory lecture delivered by one Professor
Douglas Archibald, M.A.
“The Shover”: Bent, at one time Premier of Victoria,
and now in another place, was well and unfavorably
known among Victorian audiences for his habit of burst-
ing into platform-song on the slightest provocation. It
now appears, from the reports of A. Deakin’s recent out-
break at Ballarat, that Alfred has added himself to the
ranks of show-men in the same line. At this meeting,
“ led by Mr. Deakin, the audience sang a verse of the
National Anthem, and the meeting terminated with
cheers." Certainly, the reporter might have mistaken
the National Anthem for “MyCountry, ’tis of Thee,”
which has the same dreadful tune. But that isn’t likely.
A. Deakin isn’t thinking of his country just at present.
He seems to be thinking mostly of the wretched person
who took his job, and of ways and means of getting that
job back.
That go-ahead body, the Adelaide Literary Theatre,
has issued its programme for the coming winter. It is
going to tackle 15 or 16 authors, including Ihsen, Maeter-
linck and Sudermann, Shaw, Yeats and Masefield, Upton
Sinclair, Gertrude Robins and Louis Esson. The subscrip-
tion has been doubled ; but ss. for a seat for eight perform-
ances cannot be called excessive. The first venture, in
April, will be Rann Kennedy’s semi-religious mystery,
“The Servant in the House,” which is like “The First
Floor Baek ” —only more so.
H. N. Southwell is about to light beacon fires all over
the continent, so as to give warning of the advance of
the Sheffield Choir. Commencing in Sydney Town Hall
next Monday, a series of public meetings will be held in
each city to suitably provide for greeting the Yorkshire
Dumplings. At the Sydney meeting, Mayor Allen Taylor
is to preside, and brief speeches by VV. A. Holman, G.
S. Beeby, Judge Heydon, W. P. Manning, Bruce Smith
and Win. Atkins (who is hon. boss of the local Yorkshire
Society) will tell us all about it. This oratorical pill will
be gilded with a good musical programme.
Of the 100,000 shares in the Amalgamated Biograph
Co. which has taken over the two Melbourne picture
businesses of J. and N. Tait and Johnson and Gibson, the
vendors soak up 20,000 and the public 30,000 ; the other
50,000 will be sent to market later. Of the £30,000, a
first slice of £11,500 will go into the vendors’ pockets in
payment for the stock-in-trade ; the 20,000 free shares
are for the goodwill. The second slice will pay for the
Biograph Theatre, to be built in Flinders-street, next
Bali and Welch’s. The Taits have an option to lease this
little bit of the earth for 30 years.
“Bloggs”: The current London papers are sizzling
over one “Harriet" (nobody knows her identity), who
wrote to various butlers, attached to the stately homes
of Old England, asking for news of scandals in High Life.
She said she wanted details of the wickednesses current
among the British nobility, in order to send them to
American “Society” papers. The London sheets are
falling like tons of masonry on the Yankee press which is
capable of “ so unscrupulously libelling a virtuous and
deserving class.” Yet they don’t fall like even £cwt. of
masonry on the British stage, though, that is the place
where the aristocrat (home and foreign) is more villain-
ously slandered than anywhere else. Who ever saw a
musical comedy, whose action was laid in the U.K. that
didn’t contain at least one utterly deplorable Debretter ?
In melodrama, in 99 cases out of 100, if the villain
isn’t an army captain he is a Bart. The melodrama has
yet to be written whereof the main villain is a stone-
mason. H. A. Jones, Pinero and the rest fill theirsociety
plays with gambling dukes, erotic earls and viscounts,
and financially unstable barons. In tbe matter of hurling
mud and other unpleasant matter at folks in high places,
the British drama beats the Amurrcan news-sheet out of
sight.
“Stargazer”: The actor is civilisation’s spoilt boy, and
he puts on all the airs of the petted kid. Notice him
(her or it) performing in the evening after a matin6e ;
observe the languor and the weariness, hear the sighs of
the weary spirit. The pro. has put in 2f hours in the
afternoon, possibly is “on ” for 1J hour, and is all-out
when called upon to repeat the dose in the evening.
His obvious lassitude and his groans of anguish advertise
his martyrdom to an audience that is expected to be
compassionate. The stuff he (she or it) is handling may
be tawdry balderdash, the acting calling for as much
intellectual effort as directs the caperings of an ape on a
twig, but the pitiful bids for sympathy go out over the
footlights just the same, and the night’s performance is
scamped and flat. So it remains for me, a solitary, cal-
lous scoffer, to cry : “ Yah ! get work ! ”
“Haresfoot”: Max Maxwell returns to Melbourne a
much bigger man. When he was with Bland Holt he
had the misfortune to have three good men in front of
him—Baker, Styan and Norman. So his parts were apt
to be quite beneath him. He first showed me what he
could do as Sneer, in Sheridan’s “Critic,” at a matinee ;
and long did he persevere with such things as Gecko, in
“Trilby.” His position with Bland was not altogether
unlike that of Phelps with Macready. Brooke would not
submit to such jamming down. Macready engaged him
and billed him as Ghost in “Hamlet.” “I only play
Hamlet,” said Brooke, and quashed the engagement.
After three years of pin-pricks, Phelps determined he
would have no more of it either ; but the wisdom of his
spouse prevailed, and he signed on for another three
years. Then said Mac: “Come with me to America.”
“No,” said Phelps, and he let Jack Ryder go. And it
was well ; for Macready, like Irving, lost ground by
going across the pond.
“ Harrison O.”: Concerning Mrs. Jordan, the famous
actress who, although she never married, was, neverthe-
less, a'great mother, “ Busk ” (B. 16/2/’ll) states she had
four children by Ford. This, I think, is incorrect; at any
rate, I can trace only two children, both daughters.
Ford was a city magistrate and a scion of one of the pro-
prietors of Drury-lane; furthermore, he was bad-tem-
pered and unpopular. The general public was under the
impression that the two were married. “ Busk’s ” state-
ment that the actress gave up Ford for the Duke of
Clarence is strictly accurate, yet hardly does the lady
justice. When Clarence “ proposed ” she went to Ford,
told him of the royal proposition, and offered to decline
if he (Ford) would agree to marry her. He, however,
refused to make her an Honest Woman, so she flew to
the dook. Afterwards Ford pursued her, and appears to
have acted like a beast throughout.
Violinist Ernest Toy has become professor of the fiddle
at the Marshall-Hall Conservatorium, Melbourne. Ernest
was born in Bendigo, and “topped” his musical educa-
tion at Berlin. He has travelled over a good part of
Europe and Australasia on professional tours, and last
year was noticeable as leader of tbe J.C.W. Grand Opera
orchestra. Altogether, he is a very clever young man,
usually surmounted by an absurd little hat.
“Busk ” : The cablegrapherislike a lion catching mice.
He condescends to tell us that two supes were wounded
in a recent stage fight. How many others? Irving’s
“Romeo and Juliet ” opened with a great stage battle.
The saying was that men were wounded every night, and
there was always one in hospital. Bandmann, as lago,
stabbed Mrs. Steele as Emilia ; she was laid up three
months. Hans Phillips, as Richmond, hit Creswick as
Richard 111. ; but old “ Crezzy ” came up bravely. As
long as that magnificent wig stuck on, he wouldn’t care.
Darrell’s London season was spoilt by a knife wound;
that was in “The Sunny South.” Warner, too, was badly
wounded as Michael Strogoff, and had to be replaced by
H. J. Byron,
“ Battleaxe ” : Re Dickens as an actor (B. 16/2/T1). Sir
Henry Hawkins, in his Reminiscences, referring to cer-
tain private theatricals arranged at Knebworth by Lord
Lytton, in which Charles Mathews, Macready, Douglas
Jerrold and others took part, comments as follows:
“ Next was another illustrious man whose name will live
for ever, and who was not only one of the greatest
authors of his time, but also the most distinguished of
non-professional actors. Had he been on the stage,
Mathews himself could not have surpassed him. This
was Charles Dickens.”
“ Melb ” : The disappearance of the old Queen’s makes
me think again of Beauchamp, who was an actor there
and afterwards became stage doorkeeper at the Theatre
Royal—like poor old Charley Burford in Sydney, the
Cassio of Brooke’s first Australian appearance, and the
Joseph Service of the Melbourne Royal opening. To what
base uses, Horatio ! However, Beauchamp rose again.
He disappeared from the door, and turned up in a few
years with a long white beard as Eider Beauchamp, preach-
ing Mormonism ! He was a mate of Joe Smith, thepresent
Mormon president. But Melbourne’s best exhibit in that
way—after Elijah Dowie, of course—was Wroe, a follower
of Joanna Southeott. She died without bringing forth the
Messiah. A Manchester Museum still keeps the 500-
guinea silvercradle and Royal diapers, etc., prepared for the
Expected. Wroe developed the cause into the Christian
Israelites, or Beardies, who still have their fine Milestone
church in Fitzroy, Melbourne. When he Avatar-ed,
there was yet another development. Jim Fisher, of
Nunawading, charcoal burner, started the Church of the
Firstborn, which mounted up to 120 ladies and gentle-
men, meeting in a log palace ; but that caper is pretty
well known to moderns through the revelations at full
length in the newspapers.
“Char”: Though Hall Caine still goes down in Mel-
bourne, his vogue is quite dead in London. Despite
countless huge posters representing the Pope blessing
Tittell Brune, “ The Eternal Question ” was a dead frost.
The Divorce Commission was sitting at the time the show
was produced, and Hall connected his work with the
Commission in some vague way ; but even that didn’t
avail to save the situation. After three weeks, “The
Eternal Question ” left the Garrick, allegedly to “go on
tour until a more suitable West End theatre could be ob-
tained.” The “ more suitable theatre” is still unfound.
“Varden”: Dickens approved of a few dramatic
adaptations. What he most objected to was their anti-
cipating the end of his novels. Yet he even approved of
just one of these daring affairs, “ Nicholas Nickleby,” at
the Adelphi, with Mrs. Keeley as a fat little Smike,
Wright as Squeers, O. Smith as Noggs, Sam Emery as
Browdie, and Yates as Mantalini. He authorised
Albert Smith’s adaptation of “The Cricket on
the Hearth”; but, then, 11 others, unauthorised,
were acted in London at the same time ! Amid
all the presentations in Melbourne, Jefferson’s Caleb
Plummer stood forth pre-eminent. He also acted,
Newman Noggs. His support in “ The Cricket on the-
Hearth ” included : Dot, Mrs. Heir ; Bertha, Rosa Dunn
Tilly Slowboy, Emma Neville ; Mrs. Fielding, Mrs,.
Phillips (Mother of Hans); May Fielding, Dolly Green
(Doloreo Drummond); Peerybingle, Heir; Tackleton„
Lambert; Stranger, Ireland (Harrie’s father). How
sweet was Rosa Dunn (Mrs. L. L. Lewis) as the blind
girl 1 How touching the scene with her father, when she
said, “I see you in your nice blue coat, with the brass
buttons.” Jefferson wore at the time an old sack, in-
scribed “ Glass, with care,” on the back, and was paint-
ing spots on toy horses.
Tittell Brune’s moderate success, “The Girl of the
Golden West,” has been made into an Italian opera, to
music by Puccini, and played successfully in New York.
The music seems to adapt itself to the melodramatic
action all right. (What would be the tuneful “ motive ”
or “phrase” to the heroine’s extraction of the ace from
her stocking?) But the Italian librettist has been up
against a hard thing, for he had to struggle with such
speeches as : “ Say, boys, I’m homesick, and I’m broke,
and I don’t give a damn who knows it. I want my folks !
I’m done ! ” The gentle Italian renders that by singable
phrases such as “ Son rovinato ” (I am ruined), and makes
the cowboy want to see “ la mamma mia.”
A Bert Levy yarn : “When Sarah Bernhardt was acting
in Melbourne a regular wave of French lessons passed
over the city. I remember a story told of a ladies’ school
visiting the Princess Theatre. All the girls sat in the-
front row in charge of their teacher. Each one had ai
book of ‘ Camille’ in French and English, while they en-
deavored to follow the tragedienne. In a very quiet
scene one of the damsels was heard to remark : ‘Oh, she
forgot to say ‘ Exit.’ It’s in the book.’ ”
“Harrison O.” : First act, a young ladies’ seminary ;
second act, a beer garden. Always the way with musi-
cal comedy, nowadays. Skirt-clad collegians and alcoholic
vegetation occupy prominent places in “The Orchid,”
“ The Girls of Gottenberg,” “ Miss Hook of Holland ” and
other pieces as numerous as drunks in a no-license dis-
trict. Now we have them in “ The Gay Grisette.” Most
of the characters in the “ Grisette ” are cheap reprints of
alleged classics, the copyright of which expired before
they were published. Captain Jack Canary is a good
imitation of Otto of Saxe-Hildesheim, in “The Girls of
Gottenberg ” ; Colonel Pom Pom is a second edition of
Baron Popoff, of “ The Merry Widow ” ; and Rev. Amos
Basingstoke carries a chest-protector and shrieks remem-
brance of Frank Thornton in “The Private Secretary.”
I note, with patriotic approval, by the way, that the
grand old Union John did its best to wave proudly from
the flagpole of a French chateau which, throughout the
first act, is constantly visited by French military officers
with a habit of observing “ Yive la France.”
From Captain J. Neville—whom this paper doesn’t,
know — writing from Calcutta : “ The Bulletin will re-
member the ‘ Cornish pixie,’ whom I had on exhibition
in Sydney and elsewhere in Australia. I now write to-
inform you that he died this morning (8/2/T1), after a
short but painless illness ; that he is being buried this
afternoon as a member of the Church of England, and
that his age is given as over 21 years. We had just con-
cluded a tour of S’Africa, and arrived here some 10 days
ago. At this place he contracted a severe cold, and died
of acute nephritis this morning. He was under the care
of Dr. W. S. Allan, 3 Esplanade-row, Calcutta, during his
illness.”
A DARLINGHURST IDYLL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Each evening, as I take my way
Towards my home, I pass
A stream of mummers going west»
The wild-hued chorus lass
Flits by, and comic men galore,.
And “ leads” of every class.
Munching the remnants of a bun,
Fitz Curse goes hurriedly ;
His mild, dyspeptic countenance
And inoffensive eye
Do not suggest the Perfect Fiend
He will be by-and-bye.
Fitzcurse—encountered in the street —
Resembles nothing more
Than one of those meek men with bags
Who to the city pour
Each morn, and then trot home again.
One meets them by the score.
The dashing, front-row ballet girl,
Observed at half-past six
P.M. is destitute of dash.
The colors do not mix
Upon her hair or hat or cheeks ;
Her claims to charm are nix.
A spotty youth, in spectacles,
And dusty shoes of tan,
Proceeds unnoticed to his job :
No eager females scan
His face with looks of passion—yet
He is a Beauty Man.
And I, that ridiculeth them,
What do they think of me ?
A short, fat, futile man, no doubt;
A shop-band, possibly,
Or ill-paid clerkling, hasting back
To his vile hash-house tea.
So night by night we pass like trams,
Upon the crowded street,
Nor say a word —our furtive eyes
With ill-hid scorn replete,
And all the vague intolerance
Of folks who never meet.
n.s.w.
10-2-4.
A MODERN AMENDMENT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mr. Hammond, the Australian aviator, yesterday made
a fine flight over Melbourne. —Neivs item.
The earth-folk heard the biplane sing'
Its crackling anthem in tbe blue ;
The airman steered the darting thing
That swooped and soared on canvas wing
And bowing, took a bird’s-eye view.
A stout man shuddered on his feet
And asked us how a man could dare
To soar so high above the street.
He moaned, that man of weight and meat:
“ None but the brave deserve the air ! ”

AGTRESS WORSHIP.
"She’s on the stage!”
Melbourne Advocate proposes that the
name of (Jollins-street should be altered to Bat-
m in-street. A misprint, no doubt, for Fatman-
street.
“10-2-4”: A subject I should
admire to see handled by Havelock Ellis,
Lombroso, Joe Cook or one of the other
Masters of Sociology, is the astounding
capacity for indignation that a man ac-
quires as a result of becoming a suburban
alderman. I have again and again
seen the irreconcilable. He looks as
mild a proposition as ever wore the
plug hat and bulbous gamp of hopeless
respectability. lam told, by those who
should know, that his temperament
—outside council hours —is a sunny one.
Encounter him in the street, and he is
merry and bright; bump him in the
execution of his duty as a suburban
alderman, and you get a roaring lion
and a sniffing jaguar. Everywhere it is
the same. All over this vast continent
aldermen are perpetually engaged bark-
ing blood-thirstily at one another over
the merest trivialities; yet the out-
laws are mostly the mildest of men in
the home circle. What is the answer
to it? Does the burden of civic res-
ponsibility weigh the holder down so
that his nerves get on edge? Is he,
contrariwise, maddened by a haunting
suspicion of the futility of his job?
Does his sense of the aesthetic become
frayed by ceaseless debates re the
wisdom or otherwise of deporting the
corpse of Jones’s dog from its resting-
place on the border line of the adjoin-
ing bailiwick, or abolishing the open
drain outside the beer-palace of Smith?
These are problems I yearn to see
resolved by Forbes Winslow or Dill Macky,
or some other medical authority.
* * * *
Professor Abell, of the chair of Naval
Architecture at Liverpool University, says that
future Dreadnoughts will be oil-driven, almost
submerged and protected by a thick, horizontal
deck. It seems that they will either have to be
something like that or not be at all. The 15-inch
naval gun recently arrived, and the possibility of
a weapon that size tires out the lay imagination.
Meanwhile, both British and German sea-gunnery,
with the present 12-inch giants, has become horri-
bly accurate and ruinous. The new battleship
Neptune, at a range of five miles, threw five 12-
inch shells within 150yds. of the target. The
experts reckon the broadside would have crippled
any battleship afloat. A cable a few days later
said that a German cruiser had performed much
to the same effect; so there is seemingly no great
difference between the markmanship of the two
great rivals. The heavyweight battleship’s punch
is already terrible enough to make sub-marining a
matter of urgency ; in another year or Jso it will
be a matter of necessity.
* * * *
“ J.W.” tells the story of a “ scoop ” :
lie the alleged scoop by Melbourne Argus (B. 25/2/’ll).
The N.Z. Herald did a similar trick a while back. When
Alex. Hogg was bumped out of the Ward Ministry, he
stumped Maoriland advocating a third party. After his
visit to Auckland a meeting of union officers and others
was convened, and the press was informed that no re-
porters would be admitted, but that a report would be
supplied, which promise was kept. Next day the city
■was agog over a column report in the Herald, which
asserted that a Herald reporter disguised as a lumper
had attended the meeting and had hurrahed for Labor
and groaned at the mention of Ward in approved style.
Likewise, the lumper-reporter detailed how in an excess
of enthusiasm a Labor Ministry was formed on the spot
and the portfolios whacked out with magnificent gene-
rosity. In brief, he depicted Labor’s rapacity and selfish-
ness and disrespect for law’n’order and propputy in a
manner that delights the typical Tory. When the
Journalists’ Institute took the matter up and talked of
maintaining the dignity of journalism the Herald men
burst up the show.
However, Labor waited. About last May the same re-
porter-lumper ran across the President of theLL.P. at
Auckland, and casually inquired whether anything was
happening in Labor circles? With child-like simplicity
the President was mysterious. Mr. Reporter smelled some-
thing, was all attention at once, and courteously invited
the President to come up to the office and have a smoke
and a chat—just for information, of course, not for pub-
lication. In the course of an hour’s conversation the
unsuspecting President let out that efforts were being
made to effect a coalition with the Opposition (Massey)
Party ; that the organisation was proceeding apace, as
many as three meetings being held per evening, all behind
closed doors ; that funds were being accumulated, etc.
After a final cigar, Mr. President departed, promising to
give the Herald the tip when things were ready.
Next morning, as he expected would be the
case, the Herald had a column of information
obtained from “ a prominent Laborite,” with a
sub-leader commending Labor for getting a bit
of sense. Also it Press Ass’d the news, and for a fort-
ni«ht the Opposition-Labor combine was the main topic.
Then, questioned by the Herald as to further develop-
ments, Mr. President grinned, and declared that the
thing had never been talked about or discussed by Labor
not even suggested. With a breezy “Ta ! Ta ! ” and a
promise to look in if anything fresh occurred, the Presi-
dent dodged out, leaving the Herald scratching its head
and slowly waking up to the awful fact that it had played
with edged tools and been damaged.

One-half of a Collins-street (Melb.) shep-
herd’s address to Flinders-lane and adjacent
places, on the occasion of a recent dinner-hour
service, was devoted to a condemnation of back-
biting, the other half to abuse of that very good
friend to the clergy—the Devil
A vision of the Fiend we had,
With leech-like figures, darkly clad,
Upon his back —a multitude
In greedy imbibition glued.
It called for constant care and skill
To gnaw and gnaw', and not to kill.
This is his fate, poor devil, doomed
To be devoured, and not consumed.
HIS FATHER WAS AN ANGLER.
TEACHER: “Now, who was the man
the whale swallowed?”
BRIGHT PUPIL: “Ananias!”
The lawyer who “ takes silk,” as a batch
have been doing lately, has some curious language
cast at him. The quaint phraseology of the
Letters Patent issued to him is a relic of the days
of Queen Bess, who started the business in IGO4
by appointing Francis Bacon to be Queen’s Coun-
sel Extraordinary. And the Oath matches the
Letters
I do declare that well and truly I will serve the King as
one of His Counsel Learned in the Law, and truly council
the King in his matters when I shall be called, and duly
and truly minister the King’s matters and sue the King’s
process after the course of the law and after my cunning.
For any matter against the King, when the King is party,
I will take no wage nor fee of any man. I will duly in con-
venient time speed such matters as any person shall have
to do in the law against the King as I may lawfully do
without long delay, tracting or tarrying the party of his
lawful process in that to me belongeth. I will be attend-
ant to the King’s matters when I be called thereto. So
help me God!
“After my cunning” doesn’t mean now quite
what it meant when it was first written. But, all
things considered, it would be a pity to change it.

“A. Borig What a hardy annual is the
(Sydney) North Shore Bridge proposal! Rum-
maging in an old Sydney Gazette of 1835,1 found
an article advocating the connection of Dawes
Point and the North Shore. Said the 1835-er
However difficult such an undertaking may appear to
a common observer, to an engineer who knows what he
may have to contend with, and the means to be em-
ployed, these appear but trifling, particularly since the
discovery of the means of working under water. By the
success of such an undertaking, the value of the land at
the opposite side would increase to an enormous amount;
the various coves might then be connected, and roads
constructed branching in all directions from the Hawkes-
bury and neighboring towns.
That was 75 years ago, and the engineers are still
explaining how easy it is !
* * * *
A young road-hog, employed by some
Tooralc people, came up the other day before the
Prahran Bench. The Bench, J.P., referred regret-
fully to the fact that in fining the chauffeurand in
disbelieving the evidence of two Toorak ladies it
was passing trouble along to old friends. Neverthe-
less, though they were old friends, the Bench’s
sense of duty triumphed, and it fined the road-hog
£3. But at that point Duty apparently felt satis-
fied, or retired hurt, or something. Any-
way, when the prosecuting inspector asked that,
in view of a number of previous convictions,
the road-hog should have his license revoked,
The Bench, J.P., said, “ We are not going to sus-
pend the license.” So the the young roadhog, with
his carload of Toorak, may still whirl up the dust;
and even if he kills anybody, his license seems
safe. Licenses seem safe no matter what hap-
pens. When anybody gets killed, it is the fault
of the deceased. It always is. It is never the
fault of the driver (who always gets off sooner or
later—generally sooner), and nobody dreams of
laying the corpse at the feet of fool magistrates,
who won’t revoke licenses, even when they get the
straight tip from the police and the records to
do so.
* * * »
The recent Criminal Sittings in Adelaide
illustrated the foolishness of the ‘•compromise”
verdict in a case in which the jury cannot decide
whether or not you (say) stole a horse, and finally
decides that you embezzled its shoes. In this
Adelaide case, prisoner was being tried on what
is elsewhere called a “ capital charge,” though it
is not so in S. A. The victim described the terrible
and long-continued struggle. Prisoner merely
said in defence that he was a welcome guest, and
that he and the lady together consumed during
the seance three bottles of beer and two of
wine. Also, he pointed out that he has only one
arm. The jury was hopelessly puzzled between
the two stories, and came back into court to say
that it couldn’t agree; then it went away, and
returned with a feeble verdict of indecent assault
—which carried one year’s gaol for a man who
either is innocent or deserves a long term. Judge
Homburg seemingly shared in the puzzlement;
for next day he called the prisoner up, said un-
flattering things about the jury—which had gone
home—and’ reduced the sentence to three months.
• » * *
When the Rangatira lined up
alongside Port Melbourne pier on the
last day of February, an interesting
and interested little group of old-time
immigrants went down to have a look
at her and get their memories refreshed.
For the Rangatira was the latest repre-
sentative of the modern emigrant ship
—had brought over 1000 new Aus-
tralians from Tilbury Docks to the old
Town Pier without one sight of land,
save a glimpse of Teneriffe and a hasty
glance at the blurred outline of Table
Mountain as it showed through the
fog. The old-timers were impressed ;
it was all so different from “their
time.” Very few babies or childrtn
then and few women, and the men
were all in mad haste to rush away
to “ the fields.” To-day everything
is tabulated and registered ; no mad
rush, no bother. But the old timer
would have none of it. “Too much
Guv’ment now—we just up with our
swags and found our own way around.”
The new comers had other opinions.
The liner unloaded 530 settlers at Mel-
bourne —the rest went on to Sydney
that same evening—and before she left
all had been planted in comfortable
lodgings and their luggage had been
delivered. Three days later less than
a tenth of them remained in Melbourne.
That shipload of 1000, so easily
handled, so quietly and swiftly dis-
posed of, was in reality a magnifi-
i cent advertisement of the “re-
sources of this great country.” If
the Commonwealth and the shipping
cos. can keep that stream in full flow,
there would be less fear of what may
happen after 1915.

A cow of a meeting was held in
Lismore (N.S.W.) on a very recent
date. The object of the bovine gather-
ing appears to have been to strew
cow-grease along the path of the Rural
Workers’ Union. The employees whom
these cow men are so anxious to check
are. of course, dreadful fellows entitled to no con-
sideration at all. Only the day before one of them
had allowed himself to be drowned while attempt-
ing to swim across the swollen Tweed River to
obtain relief for one of his master’s cows.
A SONG OF SUNDAY OFF.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A deputation representing the Council of the Churches
urged the Postmaster-General to-day to respect the con-
scientious objections of telephone attendants to work on
Sundays. Rev. J. T. Robertson said it was a moral
offence for a Christian telephone attendant to do work
which facilitated gossip on Sundays about the frivolities
of the week.
The toilers of the telephone
Must cultivate the>r moral tone ;
One day a week, and one alone,
Is suitable for that —
The day when ev’ry Christian Tarings
His thoughts to bear on holy things,
And reads about the Jewish kings
Whom other kings “ begat” ;
Six days a week they meekly bear
The constant strain of “ Are you there?”
And shudder when subscribers swear
And scoffers gaily scoff ;
But as an antidote to cure
Their poisoned minds of words impure,
And ease the pain their hearts endure,
They want the Sunday off.
No other day, in any case,
Can offer them the time and place
For saying prayers and finding grace
Upon their humbled knees ;
They contemplate with cold disdain
Mere aids to recreation vain,
Who work the Sunday tram and train,
For they are not as these.
The toilers of the train and tram
Have got no souls to save or damn,
They cut no ice, nor put on jam,
Nor dream of playing “ goff.”
They, like the members of the Press
(Who have no misdeeds to confess),
Are always working more or less,
And need no Sunday off.
But operators at the ’phone
Must have their day to sigh and groan,
And bow their heads before the Throne
Whereof the parsons speak ;
Their day to worship and adore,
Or loaf upon the sad sea shore
Collecting grace—a goodly store—
To last them through the week.
And then, if sinners ring the bell,
A fiend whose name we know too well
Will roar the mandate : “Go to”
(Just here we pause to cough).
The Wowser saint, by Heaven blest,
Who runs the show and knoweth best,
Will recognise no Day of Rest
Except the Sunday off ;
And, ere we be much older grown,
lie’ll wowserise the telephone.

OCCASIONAL CONSTIPATION is a fertile source of
many of the minor ills that flesh is heir to. The best way
of restoring a state of healthy activity to stomach and
bowels is by taking a wineglasSful of “ Hcnyadi Janos ”

“ MEN’S OUTFITTERS,”
309-311 George Street, Sydney.
According to cabled information, the
M.L. Government has purchased a nugget of gold
as a Coronation gift; and if George V. has the
same luck with it as the man had who should have
found it, he will have the misfortune to break his
royal neck. Over 20 years ago an old miner
named McCarthy was working a claim at Ross,
on the West Coast, in full confidence that he
would find a barrow-load of gold owing to
an extremely vivid dream. In that dream,
which came to him more than once, he
saw himself dragging the golden egg out
of its nest and polishing its shell with his
shirt sleeve. Unfortunately the dream never
showed the exact part of the claim where the
property was hiding; and one day, while mentally
ticking off the places where it possibly might be,
he fell over the tail-race and fractured his neck.
After that the claim was abandoned until last
September, when a couple of men named Scott
and Sharp had ago at it. They found the old
man’s nugget all right; but as he was then too
dead to take any interest in the matter, they
could do no more than drink his health. The
nugget is a very fine specimen, shaped like a
man’s head, weighing 990 z. 12dwt. 12gr., and
valued at £4OO.
Once upon a time, the poor benighted
Fijian lived the Simple Life and was happy.
When he was hungry the ripe banana dropped
into his mouth ; when thirsty, the milky cocoanut
fell at his feet—if on his head, it didn’t matter,
for that was armor-plated by Nature. He enjoyed
tlie song and dance, and went surf-bathing with-
out a clammy costume. To give life a zest and
keep himself in condition, he now and then had
a collar-and-elbow tussle with his neighbor, or a
elub-swing'ing contest with his enemy. The sur-
vivor of a fight ate the victim to save funeral
expenses, and no one was any the worse. Life
was a long holiday. Next it was written :
“Better a cycle in Fiji than five years on the
plantations.” That was after the white man had
come into his paradise and tempted Fiji with rum,
and he had drunk thereof. For when he awoke the
Fijian found himself encased in a white shirt, with
a hoe in his hands, working for one white brudder,
while other white brudders preached to him out of
The Book to repent of his manifold sins and work
harder. And each preacher said to him ; “You
must believe alonga me and not alonga other
white preachers, or you be damned.” By and bye
the benighted heathen found that, with the glass
beads and tomahawks, the white man had brought
him the whooping cough and the small-pox and
other invisible devils which made his people
wither away. Poor Fiji was sad, and to shake off
his sadness he had a song and dance on Sunday.
Then the preachers all brandished The Book at
him. Whereat Fiji got very angry and burned The

Book. So when, in March, A. n. 1911, the
Methodist Missions had their annual corroboree
in Sydney, the Boss got up and lamented that
poor Fiji was still tike a child ; and he called for
help to save him, and stop him from disturbing
the Sawbath, and teach him to work better for the
white brudder. A Rev. Dr. announced that the
Sunday-school kiddies at Strathfield had given
them toffee money, even £52, to help in the
Blessed work. Whereupon the meeting whooped
for joy. and went home rejoicing that Fiji is likely
to be plucked from the burning after all.

“ Carton ” : Slowly and silently she laid
the white, lifeless little form in the place that had
been prepared for it. Tenderly and gently she
rose, and gazed for the last time on that which
she had borne. Mute, dry-eyed, alone, for a short
space she leant over the little rounded body
that was fast growing cold. Suddenly those
who were near, all unconscious of the scene
•that was being silently enacted within a
stone’s throw, were disturbed by the ear-
splitting, heartrending shriek that echoed
through the stillness of that early summer’s
morning. Again there was silence; then
a second shriek; then shriek upon shriek.
Hastily getting into a pair of slippers, I
rushed in my pyjamas to the spot. I raised
the lifeless little body, carried it to the house
—and had it fried with some bacon for breakfast.
The old spotted hen had done her duty again.

“Miles”: “E.S.C.” (B. 16/2/’ll) makes
a far bigger “bloomer”—or rather three of them
—than either Seymour, Lucas or De Neuville.
He speaks of the “rifles ” of the troops employed
in the Gordon Riots. The rifle was not intro-
duced into the British army till nearly 11 years
after the Gordon Riots. Also, Lucas is perfectly
correct in showing the bayonets on top of the
barrels. The bayonet was first fixed in the barrel;
then (about 1760) it was fixed on top, where it re-
mained till approximately 1825 or 1826. (I speak
from memory, not having my Greener here.)
The introduction of the rifle led to the
bayonet being placed on the side (Snider and
Martini-Henry), where it remained until
about 15 years ago, when the introduction
of the magazine rifle led to its being placed below.
So De Neuville is right, too, if “E.S.C.” refers to
Rorke’s Drift, for that fight was in 1879, when
most of the troops were armed with the M.-H.
rifle, Mark 1. De Neuville is recognised as a
most accurate historical painter, and for his de-
tails would naturally go to either the Tower of
London or the Woolwich Rotunda. “ E.S.C.”
might do likewise before criticising. Possibly he
would regard it as a “ bloomer ” if he saw pictures
of troops using leather cannons, four-barrelled re-
volvers or bronze swords ; but the pictures would
still be right if the dates were right.

N.S.W. Works Minister Griffith has ex-
tended the glad hand to a deputation of local
government bodies, which made application for
power to keep the unsightly hoarding fiend at
bay, and cast his placard to the 44 winds and his
colored shriek to the rubbish-tin. When the
Local Government Act was passed the councils
had authority to deal out trouble to the dis-
figuring nuisance, and some of them did
it to the extent of 10s. a foot. But a
word in the Wade Government’s ear sufficed to
gain for the paste-pot permission to roam at will,
and leave its unsightly tracks over the beauty
spots of the city, suburbs and harbor. Griffith
has now stated his intention of re-delegating the
control over advertising hoardings to the local
bodies, and the unclad lady whose buzzom bulges
over Smith’s corrugated corset will be ordered off a
considerable number of streets before long, arid
the outraged feelings of harbor trippers will be
soothed by the removal of the awful paunch
belonging to the naked infant who was fed on
Bullpup Concentrated Stuffing. The trouble is,
the scheme doesn’t go far enough in its optional
form, because there are several councils whose
views on the unsightliness of their neighborhood
are regulated only by the cash accompaniment.
It comes back to the urgent necessity of a Minis-
ter for Grace and Elegance, armed with an Act of
Parliament with a long reach ana a big punch.
“D.” : Said the Rev. C. J. Prescott, in
his presidential address to the Methodist Confer-
ence: “ The Church has no right to become the
friend of the working man if the price of it is that
she become the enemy of the rich man.” What
of the Scriptural declaration re the rich man, the
camel, the needle’s eye, and the Kingdom of
Heaven? What of half a hundred similar pro-
nouncements from holy writ, teaching that the
rich man is out of the scope of grace by simple
reason of his riches ? But why argue ? The plate’s
the thing. Give and be forgiven.
Captain Young, of the steamer Nolise-
tnent, rolled into Melbourne last week witli an
idea for a sensational sea novel. Last November,
whilst bound for Buenos Ayres, he picked an un-
clothed mariner out of the ocean some 60 miles
from St. Vincent. There wasn’t a ship in sight
at the time, or a smudge of smoke on the horizon
to indicate that there had been one. When the
lone man had been hauled in, and dried and re-
freshed, he explained that he had fallen overboard
from a steamer called the Milton, he didn’t quite
know when, and had been swimming ever since.
He only remembered tearing his wet shirt off in
strips, and guessed that he had got rid of the rest
of his clothes whilst swimming in a trance. When
he tried to get a rest by floating, the attendant,
fish mistook him for a dead body and started to eat-
ins arms and legs, and he had to swim ceaselessly to.
keep them off. Yet that man’s first words, on
being helped up the rescuing steamer’s ladder,,
were a sort of apology for having screamed for
help. “ I thought you were going to pass me,”'
he gasped. They landed him at Buenos Ayres ;;
and unless he has been killed by slipping on a.
banana-peel, he is probably afloat once more..
Davy Jones has plainly no use for that man.
“Kaysim,” from N’York :
Two or three years back one misfit light upon a tiny
paragraph about Australia, tucked up in a corner ot a
New York daily, about twice in a twelve-month. Now
the word “Australia” hits my homesick eyes with the
force of a southerly buster at least twice a week from the
leading pages of the dailies. In the delectable 20 minutes
in which it takes the subway cars to whirl me to and
from business morning and evening, the jamb of humans
is too tight to give one the chance to move anything but
eyes, and these I occasionally lift to meet the words
“ Pure Australian Wool,” writ large and arrestingly in
commendation of somebody’s wear for men. That “ad ”
is the only comfortable piece of furniture in the subway
system. The costliest blankets are advertised by the
best and most expensive firms as being made of
Australian wool. And on January TO, in Moquin’s,
the principal entree of the day read: “Australian kan-
garoo braise au vin blanc ! ” cents—half a crown.
Moquin’s is, next to Martin’s, the nest French restaurant
in the States. Another Australian animal is being uti-
lised in the adornment of fashionable and wealthy New
York women, who will this winter wear Australian
’possum furs. And they’ll pay for them a price that
would almost buy the selection where Billjim caught
them.

The angry words of a newcomer to the
Northern Territory :
The Northern Territory has a heavy handicap in the
clique that runs things at Port Darwin. Anyone coining
in with a view of “locating” is met with a hot volley of
stinking fish or a cold shower of stand-offishness. There
is no social life of any kind. But, though that is bad,
there is something worse. Local merchants buy the most,
inferior stuff imaginable. Goods condemned in the South
are considered good enough for the North. I ordered!
some groceries from Port Darwin. When they arrived!
the currants and raisins were full of maggots, and the
bacon and everything else full of weevils. I threw the lot
out; then wrote and stated the condition the things were
in and deplored the fact. I received a reply that they
did not keep groceries that would suit me, and they sug-
gested that I should try somewhere else. I need hardly
say that I did ; also, I need hardly say that I got no re-
fund of the money I had paid. I don’t believe weevils
and worms are better eating in the Northern Territory
than anywhere else, and I don’t believe they will attract
settlers, and I don’t think the people whom we are trying
to get into this part of the Commonwealth are less en-
titled to pure food than others. Wherefore the sooner
the Commonwealth Government pushes a vigorous hand
into the business the better.

IN A YEAR OR TWO.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
•‘Th’ pris’ner ” sat in the spiky dock ;
His head was bandaged—his face was white
Like one who’d suffered a fearful shock.
“ Probably been in a brawl last night.”
That was the judgment the court-house horde
Passed on the man who waited in pain,
Till someone read from a sheet—“ James Ford—
Dhrunk whilst in charrge of an airyplane ! ”
N.S.W.
TILL THE RISING.
BALLADE OF REASSURANCE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Sweetheart, I would not have you grieve,
When in your glass you look and see
Your beauty vanishing ; nor fear
Its ultimate effect on me.
In ev’ry age, in ev’ry clime.
In ev’ry dialect and tongue
Thinkers have laid this maxim down :
“Those whom we love are always young.”
You say your hair is turning grey,
And that your eyes, which used to hide
A thousand, thousand dancing lights,
Each one of which I deified,
Are fading :—Darling, smile again ;
Move unperturbed the world among ;
Heed not Time’s devastating hand ;
Those whom we love are always young.
The fairest flowers of them all
Must bud and blossom, bloom and die,
(A natural provision which
Applies, of course, to you). Shall I
Attempt to cavil at a law
Whose beauty myriads have sung?
No ! No ! mine own. And, anyhow,
Those whom we love are always young.
Envoi.
Angel, be reassured by this.
Which is a moral certainty—
However old and plain you get
It will in no wise worry me.
I still will love, although my heart
At other, fairer feet be flung—
Accordant with that mighty truth :
Those whom we lov e are always young !
N.S.W.
J.B.D.

The Westport Coal Co., Maoriland’s biggest

This is Maoriland’s biggest coal-mining concern ; and it
appears to be also one of the most prudently managed
cos. in the Dominion. One has so often to find fault with
Maoriland public and private finance that it is a
blessed relief to be able to say that, so far as
financial management goes, the Westport is a model
which Australian coal cos. would be all the better
for following. The past year’s figures show a Diyr
improvement on those of 1909. That, however, is mainly
due to the fact that during 1909 the co. suffered from a
fire, which not only lessened production, but cost
£12,000. Now what appears to be the normal has been
reached again. Certainly the old 15 per cent. div. has
not been reached ; and probably it was not expected,
since the capital has been watered up from £280,000 to
£360,000 by the transfer of reserves—a process, by the
way, which is now being repeated to the extent of £40,000.
This latter amount will not, however, be represented by
new shares, but will be applied to fully paying the 400,000
shares, which lately have had a liability of 2s. Apparently
this transfer will not affect the visible reserves ; the amount
will come out of a contingency fund, which the directors
had up their sleeves, and which they spent in construction
work at the Denniston mine. The work done here has
increased this mine’s possible output from 900 to at least
1200 tons per day.
The table shows £5OOO a year written off. This
is a sum put aside for the return of capital. The manage-
ment of the Westport doesn’t humbug itself as to the
.nature of its property ; it realises that the best of mines
must be worked out some day, and that every ton taken
out brings it a ton nearer its end. lienee, what is called
a sinking fund has been established, and vested in trus-
tees. The total amount paid to them has been £35,000 ;
interest accretions bring the total to £39,862. But be-
sides the amount set apart for sinking fund, and shown
in the table as written off, unspecified sums are written
off revenue to provide for unmentioned contingencies.
This table shows how the chief Property item has
shrunk, in spite of the expenditure on new works :

In six years, therefore, £91,486 has been earned and put
by. Meantime regular dividends have been paid and very
large sums spent on works.
Last year’s output was a record. For some few

A large quantity of the output is consumed by the war-
ships, which burn Westport even when lying in Sydney
Harbor; and this no doubt had some influence indeciding
the Admiralty to make more use of Auckland Harbor in
the future. But the worship demand cannot be relied
upon. The oil-propelled warship is already here.
Moreover, Queensland will some day certainly be a com-
petitor for the supply of smokeless coal. Nearer at home
the co. has its troubles in the opening up of State mines
to supply the railways and other good customers. Still,
as to a market, the Westport has no troubles worth
mentioning compared with those which most Australian
cos. have to face.
Two or three interesting changes have been
made lately in the constitution and management of the
co. General-manager Joachim, who has nursed Lhe
co. during 25 of its 29 years, has been made
managing director. At its present rate of pro-
gress, another 29, years will see the sinking fund
reach the total of the capital. The shares hitherto have
been £4 10s. paid, with a 10s. liability. Lately they were
divided into £1 shares with a 2s. liability, which liability
is to be wiped out by a bonus and an equal call, as men-
tioned just now. The life of the mine isn’t officially
estimated, although it is declared that the sinking fund
will total the paid capital long before the endoomes.

It is something of an anomaly that this bank has not in-
creased its capital for just over 30 years. A while ago
the profits of the bigger banks increased so much that
the choice lay between putting up the dividend beyond
10 per cent, and increasing the capital while continuing
to pay the 10 per cent, rate all round, on new capital as
well as on old. The bigger and most prosperous Austra-
lian banks which were on the 10 per cent, mark resolved
upon the latter course ; and from several points of view
it was the wiser choice. The Union and the ’Asia, how-
ever, both managed from London, chose to increase the
.dividend bevond 10 per cent., and thereby un-
doubtedly cried aloud for hostile attention. Certainly
tthis paper is not prepared to say that the Union
•wants more capital in the business. But that is
.only for two reasons: (1) because it holds such a very
(large sum in liquid form, and (2) because it seems quite
.certain that it has a large internal reserve. First, how-
ever, let us drop in this little block of figures
Liabilities.
Aug., 1909. Aug., 1910.
TCntes £440,176 £463,404 Inc. £23,228
Rills " 1,409,778 1,580,783 Inc. 171,005
Deposits .'.'19,879,569 21,449,290 Inc. 1,569,721
Liquid assets ..10,230.046 10,942,914 Inc. /12,868
Advances .. ..13,872,594 14,979,367 Inc. 1,106,7,3
Premises .. 560,762 547,579 Dec. 13,183
So the total liabilities are £23,493,477. The shareholders’
capital and visible reserves amount to £2,871,382. The
bank therefore holds about £8 3s. of the public’s money
for every £1 of the shareholders’; and, under ordinary
circumstances, that would be too much, and would call
for more capital in order to make things reasonably safe
both for shareholders and for creditors. Not that in this
case creditors need worry even if the proportion
got much larger, because they have the protection of a
shareholders’ reserve liability of £3,000,000. But, while
SJ to 1 is, in general, too big a proportion, obviously the
risk is reduced if a very large proportion of the borrowed
money is in coin or practically non-shrinkable securities ;
and in this case no less than 47 per cent, of all liabilities
is in that form. Further, it is quite clear that £1 of the
shareholders’ funds in one bank may be worth much
more than £1 in some other bank. That is to say, while
one bank’s assets may be overvalued—in which case the
reserves may not really exist at all—another bank s
lot may be undervalued, in which case there is
a hidden or contingent reserve as well as the
visible article. One can’t tell, of course, what a bank has
up its sleeve ; but one can form some sort of an opinion.
First, there is the test of the earnings. On its capital
and visible reserves the Union’s profits last half-year
were at the rate of £9 18s. Bd. per cent, per annum ; and
when a bank keeps nearly 50 per cent, of its deposits and
other liabilities in liquid form, and then shows nearly 10
per cent, profit, one can only conclude either that the
bank is doing a very risky business, which happens to be
turning up trumps at the moment, or that its assets are
under-valued, and that it therefore has hidden reserves.
Here is another comparison :
Liabilities.
Aug., 1900. Aug., 1910.
Notes .. .. £439,205 £463,404 Inc. 5*5 p.c.
Bills .. 1,808,665 1,580,783 Dec. 12'5 „
Deposits .. 15,226,186 21,449,290 Inc. 4'l „
Assets.

Premises .. 731,375 547,579 Dec. 25*1 ~
Note the shrinkage in the premises account. In these
10 years the bank has spent probably quarter of a million
in land and buildings, some of it rent-producing, yet the
item stands at £183,796 less than it did 10 years ago.
That is as good a proof as anyone can want that the
bank has been building up a very solid internal re-
serve. In the table large sums are shown as
written off. Most of these have been struck off the
premises account; but some—the past half-year’s
£15,000, for instance—have been used to write down
Consols. No doubt it is very proper to write down these
reserve-fund assets; but, all the same, they don’t bring
in a ha’penny less interest than they did before they
were written down ; so, from one point of view, the
amount written off is an internal reserve also. Probably
that explains why this writing-down is done out of dis-
closed profits, instead of being treated as an ordinary
shrinkage to be charged to Profit and Loss.
“ And, gentlemen,” said the acting chairman to
the shareholders in London, “ you may feel sure that
your kindly recognition at these meetings of their valued
services is greatly appreciated by the staff ”; and a
“cordial recognition of the staff’s services was carried
unanimously.” It is to be hoped that the clerk’s butcher
will cash the appreciation for at least a pound of break-
fast chops. By the way, the dividend was declared in
cash.
Apropos this matter, those English shareholders
who want to do something more for the staff think that
the best thing that can be done is to strengthen the
Superannuation Fund. It may be a good thing, but it
is not the best thing. Better salaries would be more
appreciated, especially by the younger men. And
that system would, in the end, also pay the bank better.
When a man who, instead of getting a bigger salary, gets
a bigger interest in a superannuation fund, or an interest
in a bigger superannuation fund, it is natural that the
chief officials should be very much disinclined to
remove him for anything but actual dishonesty ;
so the service becomes crowded with amiable incompe-
tents and never-sweats. Dissatisfied officers also remain
rather than sacrifice their pension rights ; and dissatis-
fied officers are a weakness in any concern. There are
several good insurance offices in Australia. Let the
Union and other banks pay their men better salaries,
and (if they please) let them insist on their officers insur-
ing in one or other of these concerns. They would find
it would pay better in the long run.

*This includes “ premiums purchasing paid-up policies

No table which has to be followed by explanatory notes
is satisfactory ; wherefore the table on top of these re-
marks isn’t satisfactory. As the directors put it: “ The
figures in the revenue account are presented in a slightly
different form this year.” As a matter of fact they
are presented in a form so different that it is
almost useless to try to compare the last line in the table
with those that march ahead of it. No doubt there is a
simple way out of the difficulty, only one can’t be sure
that it is quite the right way. If the bonuses employed
to purchase additions to policies were shown separately,
we could just cut them off the total; and then we could
deduct the bonuses thus used from the total bonus
declared for the year, and show among the claims the
balance as bonuses paid in cash. That has been the
society’s own practice hitherto. But, unfortunately, the
bonuses employed to purchase insurance are lumped with
other things—“ Single premiums, including single pre-
miums purchasing paid-up policies allocated from cash
bonuses not drawn.” Hitherto no single-premium business
has been shown ; and probably 95 per cent, of the item this
year represents bonus additions to policies. If we regard
the whole lot as of this nature the last line would run
this way :
Ex- Written Added to
Dec., Income. Claims. penses. Off. Fund.
1910.. £121,588 £39,352 £28,961 £7388 £45,887
For purposes of comparison with previous figures, no
doubt that is a much fairer statement of the case.
Though it doesn’t show so large an increase in
the income this way, it still shows a very satisfactory in-
crease indeed. A growth of more than 100 per cent,
in 10years is excellent; it means that in the past lOyears
the co.’s income grew more than it did in the previous 22
years of the society’s life. During the last period there
have been considerable changes in the management.
George Crowley, who was “ manager ” (iri rather small
type) 20 years ago, became general manager in 1900, and
managing director in 1906 or thereabouts. There can be
no doubt whatever concerning the enthusiasm which he
has thrown into the business. Nor has he waited for
other and larger offices to do things. He hasn’t been
afraid to “play his own game.” For instance, among
the payments is a line : “ Payment to members by way of
Hospital allowances.” The investments are also a
rather unusual lot. In 1907 the co. began to purchase
stocks and shares in public cos. ; and in the beginning of
1909, when the item stood at £9665, the directors ex-
plained that “this represents an investment in the shares
of a few of our leading- banks and other first-class trad-
ing cos. At the present market values a profit of con-
siderably over £lOOO could be made by a re-sale. The
item has now reached £27,463, besides £lO,OOO debentures
of public cos., together nearly 8 percent, of the total assets.
Mortgages (property) represent 38 per cent,, public securi-
ties 15 per cent., freeholds and leaseholds over 14 per cent.
The management has explained at various times, referring
to the high rate of interest earned on the investments,
that a good deal of money is lent in comparatively small
sums, and more or less on the security of the borrower’s
character. This is a class of business which the big office
can’t well touch. There is a lot of risk both in this class
of advance business and in investment in trading cos.
shares ; and any responsible general manager who cannot
give the business personal attention is not likely to con-
sent to its being done at all. This society’s management
is skilful and watchful ; and, out of business of the kind
mentioned, it is certainly liable to make good profits
for the policy-holders. Indeed, a profit of £3500 is shown
on investments for the past year. (This is included
with the income in the table.) But it is a matter of
backing the management, which in this case, there is
little doubt, means the managing director ; and it is a
question whether trust funds of a life-insurance character
ought to be put into such securities, even though they
are selected by one whose judgment may in general be
unusually good.
For the reason mentioned early in this post
mortem report, it is very little (if any) use comparing the
expense rate with that of previous years. If, how-
ever, the single premiums are discarded altogether, and
the whole expenses are thrown on to the other business,
this would be the result: —
Expenses Compared with Total Income.

The true figures for the past year are probably slightly
under those given in that list. Still, as they stand they
compare very favorably with those of the previous couple
of years, notwithstanding that the new business—always
the most expensive item—has been larger than it was in
either of those years. The mortality experience is un-
usually satisfactory ; and though the actuarial report
does not accompany the accounts now available, it is set
out in the directors’ report that the annual profit or sur-
plus has jumped along this way:—l9o6, £8322; 1907,
£8935 ; 190 S, £11,204 ; 1909, £16,824 ; 1910, £21,167. And
meantime a contingency fund of £6OOO has been pro-
vided to safeguard investments. It isn’t a large reserve ;
but it is a reasonable start towards a larger one.
With regard to the change in the manner of
presenting the accounts, the old form seems the better.
The new form no doubt swells the figures ; but it seems
to be undesirable that the same amounts should figure in
the accounts twice—first as claims when the bonuses are
declared, and then as claims again when the same
bonuses added to policies, fall due for payment to the
policy uolder. * * *
