Thi? co. was floated in 1902. Australia in general and
Sydrtey in particular had been celebrating various things
during the preceding two or three years—the inaugura-
tion of the Commonwealth, the visit of the Duke, and
“fall,” “relief," etc., in S’Africa. The co., formed on
the basis of 3£ years’ bloated profits, soon struck not
only the usual headache in the morning, but a couple
of bad drought years in the country; and there
were a lot of bad debts to provide for. It
looked as though the co. had been over-capitalised
seriously ; and no doubt it had been to some extent. The
two Toohey estates, which had got £85,000 for goodwill,
met the co. most handsomely. They agreed to the straight-
out cancellation of 50,000 of their shares, on condition
that all the other shares—their own included—were
written down from 20s. to 16s. That reduced the capital
from £550,000 to £400,000. No sooner had that vigorous
pruning been done than Sydney floated into good times
again. The directors, however, evidently still had a few
things that they thought ought to be written further down
or off ; anyway, the disclosed profits for the next couple of
years were very moderate. Then they started to bubble ;
and they have gone on bubbling. The progress is even
better than the list above shows, because latterly larger
sums have been appropriated on account of depreciation.
This shows the, visible amounts written off; —
July, 1907 ..£2500 I July, 1909 ..£3OOO
Jan., 1908 .. 2500 Jam, 1910 .. 4000
July, 1908 .. 2500 July, 1910 .. 3000
Jan., 1909 .. 3000 | Jam, 1911 .. 4000
Those amounts come off for depreciation of plant and
machinery. The mon^y—£24,soo in four years—has not
gone in upkeep ; the directors report: “ All expenditure
in connection with machinery and plant has been de-
frayed out of current revenue, as heretofore.” Presum-
ably, therefore, it has gone into new plant.
Whether anything is written off buildings and
leaseholds, and if so, how' much, isn’t to be told from the
published accounts, which haven’t nearly as much to say
for themselves as Spoof Smith or W. Judkins. All the
details, in fact, can be gathered into this small or after-
noon-tea table: —
July, ’O6. July, TO. Jan.,’ll
Liabilities, less cash .. .. £55,569 £29,514 £25,248
Breweries, hotels, etc. .. 322,139 338,415 345,338
Stocks .. .. ~ .. 59,945 56,993 55,/12

It is fairly obvious that the turnover has increased ; the
two lines, “ Gross profits on brewings, etc.,” and “Man-
agement, wages and charges,” show that pretty plainly.
To comfort W. Judkins, this can, however, be assumed to
be due to the development of the ginger-beer branch of
the business. With £345,138 in a lump, represented
mainly by premises and properties, it is clear that the
shareholder and investor have to take a very great deal
on trust; if these assets or any of them are depreciating,
and the management is not writing off enough out of cur-
rent revenue to provide for that depreciation, then the time
will inevitably come when something large will have to be
written off in bulk, either out of reserves, or, if re-
serves are not enough, out of capital also. On the other
hand, the management may be taking a very conservative
view, and may be writing off more than enough. Nobody
who can’t get behind the counter can tell. But if the
directors are doing just the fair thing, then excellent
progress is being made, and a net profit for the past 12
months of a shade over 10£ per cent, w'as made—a piofit
which allowed very nearly 5 per cent, to be laid by. In
three years, ori the same assumption, £49,000 has been
stored up, in addition to whatever has been written off ;
and that amount laid by ought to be earning £2500
a year at least for the co. The reserve appropriations
for the past half-year, by the way, include £lOOO to start
a Public Risk Reserve and Contingencies Fund. A Public
Risk Reserve makes a frequent first appearance these
days. The idea is to have something in the stocking in
case a trolly of beer runs over a Wowser and the Wowser
recovers damages for the insult, and things of that sort.

Winchcombk, Cabson, Ltd., with head office in
Sydney, was put on the market in July last; but th“
business carried on by Winehcombe, Carson and Co., Lid.,
was taken over as from April 1, 1910. The old co. was
formed in 1899, and was of the private sort. The new
co. is in 400,000 shares, of which, however, only 205,755
have been issued. The vendors took 105,755 shares; the
public—mostly constituents —jumped at the chance of
taking the other 100,000, and rushed them to a premium.
On these latter shares 10s. was paid on Dec. 31, leaving
a 10s. liability. It was a walk-in-walk-out sale. The old
co. transferred even its reserve fund (L 10,254) to the new
one. On the other hand, the new co. was loaded with
£41,983 of goodwill; so, discarding that as an asset, the
new co. really started with a deficit of £31,729. The
position then and since then may be put as follows :
Towards De-
Profits. Dividends. Reserves, licit.
April, 1910 - £31,729
Dec., 1910 .. £13,549 6 p.c.= £5670 £7879 23,850
From this it appears that the average amount of capital
in the business during the nine months was £126,000.
The profit was at the rate of £18,065 a year. On the
mean capital employed, that profit would show a return
of 14j per cent. Seeing that only a 6 per cent. div. is
paid, a very large margin is allow ed to lay by and eventu-
ally wipe out'the goodwill item. Depreciation has also
been provided for, but to what extent is not disclosed ;
and as the new co. was to pay “ expenses, including stamp
duty, legal costs and other fees connected with the in-
corporation of the new c 0.,” these non-recurring items
have presumably been charged up with the ordinary ex-
penses. This probably explains why the £20,774 which
was stated to have been averaged in profits for
five years was not quite reached last year. Still
14 per cent, is not to be despised. Seasons will, however,
come when it certainly will not be realised in a stock and
station agency and produce-selling business of this sort,
unless all past history has to be thrown overboard as
valueless for reference. As before remarked, the co. on
December 31 had only called up 10s. per share. It had,
however, received £10,037 on account of uncalled capital,
and this and other debts brought the liabilities up to
£88,385. Further, the co. is extending its business in
Brisbane, where it is building ; **b it was
quite certain it would want more capital that it had on
December 31 last; and as the present is an unusually
good time to get it, the co. very wisely asked foranother
ss. per snare after the accounts iyvere made up.

The co., getting into the usual trouble in the ’9o’s, coni-
promised with its creditors iii 1893. With the bi£
ideas then prevalent—and the remarks of to-day fre-
quently recall the remarks of 1893 and thereabouts
—the society undertook to pay off the principal
debt in seven years, and meantime allow 5 per,
cent, interest. In 1895 the society, having sobered
up, did the usual thing: it crawled in, in a pern-
tent spirit, and said it would try to pay the principal
when it could, and meantime would the depositors
(representing about £139,000) plea-e take 24 per cent.! 1
In 1900 it interviewed Justice Holroyd, and told him
that when it had previously been suffering a recovery it
had whipped the cat too severely. It proposed, there-
fore, still another compromise : If the Court would allow
it to go ahead with ordinary business, it would write
off half its capital, so as to allow for shrinkages in the
value of assets, and present a clean balance-sheet; and it
would pay the old depositors 4 per cent, on certain con-
ditions. At that time only about £37,000 remained to
pay off. The new scheme was accepted, and things went
ahead as follows: — ,
July,’ol. Jan., TO. July, TO. Jan., 11.
Liabilities .. £37,015 £31,117 £30,865 £33,188
Advances .. 19,998 68,757 62,841 i 0,455
Foreclosures.. 72,305 43,182 38,899 35,266
Costs, etc. .. 2,778 114 946 911
Meantime, as the first table shows, steadily-increasing
dividends have been paid on the written-down capital.
These have been helped by very substantial profits on
sales of properties—profits in this case being of course the
excess over the written-down values. As properties have
been sold the money has been put out in new' advances.
In many instances, no doubt, these advances are amounts
ow’ing by purchasers who have bought properties on
terms. The extent of the real building society business
which the co. has secured, is, therefore, uncertain.
And no doubt, in these days of State banks and other
similar aids to the struggling little man to get on his
feet, the field for the building society is much restricted,
and its chances of making big profits are hobbled ; while
tiie Starr-Bowkett societies are powerful competitors for
such building society business as there is.
The cinematograph businesses of Johnson and
Gibson and J. and N. Tait have been floated in Melbourne
into a co. with a capital of £lOO,OOO, half in reserve. The
amount was over-subscribed at the first fell stockbroking
swoop. Flickergraph cos. will presently be counted by the
hundred, and a lot of them are going to be the graves of
confiding capital and unreasonable hopes; for “pictures”
are the one form of show business in which the, newcomer
with no previous experience can supply practically the
same entertainment that is offered at the old-established
cinematograph theatres. Moreover, he can do it during
six months of the year without providing cover for the
audience, and on warm nights his cheap and easy way of
doing it is an additional attraction. Wherefore keen
competition is inevitable ; for many of the competitors
will be prepared to lose money—for a while, as they
think—by undercutting. # #
The British Broken Hill directors are holding
on to the Elmore flotation process better than Block 10
did. But the slimes are the trouble. The British is throw-
ingabout half its possible profits into its rapidly accumulat-
ing slime dumps, which is not good for the shareholders.
Even the De Bavay process, which at the Amalgamated
Oo.’s works is making good-sized profits, is only doing so
because spelter is at such a pleasant price. A slimes
dump, which means lost profits, is being piled up there
too ; and when it gets high enough to trip up the chair-
man of directors the Amalgamated Zinc Co. wil] also
start thinking. # # #
Broken Hill Junction is about doomed as an in-
dependent mine. B. H. Central manager Hebbard has
once more told the directors what he thinks about the
derelict concern, and especially its patchwork mill and
its as-good-as-new steaming plant. He has also told
them how much money they will have to spend in order
to ensure a bare margin of profit on the ore in sight at
present metal prices. It all works out something like
this : If a man builds a house at a cost of £lOOO, and sells
it immediately for £7OO, how much profit does he make?
In other w'ords, if the directors spend the thousands re-
quired to turn the mine into a profit-making concern at
the present price of metals, the visible profit on the whole
of the ore in the mine would not reimburse them for their
outlay. The outlook is, therefore, not encouraging.

The Bigelow Patent Rock Breaker commends
itself to mine managers who welcome an effective
machine which is cheaper to maintain and repair than
Gyratory and other crushers. This machine is of the
well-known Blake type, but has sonie additional improve-
ments which have justified in practice all the claims
advanced by the patentee. The work of crushing is done
on the down-stroke of the pitman instead of the up-
stroke ; there is no strain on the pitman cap ; two large
side rods resist the thrust of the toggles, and enable the
jaws to be regulated within a large range; the back
toggle is so made as to resist all legitimate strains of
crushing, but will shear under exceptional conditions
and save the machine. The shaft and eccentric bearings
are self-lubricating ; the jaws are convex and concave,
and with the cheek plates are made of steel and are
reversible. The Austral Otis Engineering Company, of
South Melbourne, have installed a great number of these
machines, and hold the sole manufacturing rights for the
Commonwealth.*

Diamond mining in Inverell district (IST.S.W.)
is getting a move on again. A syndicate, headed by Dr.
Flateau, who has had bad luck with four tin dredges in
Glen Innes district, has made a start on what is known as
the Old Farm at Copeton. A trial shaft bottomed at, 60ft.
on 3ft. of wash gave 15 carats diamonds and 201 b. of
tin to the load; and another washing of 15 loads is re-
ported to have given 352 carats, valued at £4OO. Other
shows are bucking up, partly by reason of a better local
demand for the stones.
* * * *
At the Queen Bee (N.S.W.) the main shaft has
been sunk 20ft., making it 63ft. below the 570 ft. level; so
it will not be many weeks before another level can be
opened. The deepest level has been driven 84Sft. to the
north, and the end shows soft slate and quartz carryinga
little copper, not payable at present. Southern ore-body
prospects have also been poor lately.

Great Boulder (W.A.) now estimates its ore
reserves at 760,177 long tons, containing 670,821 fine ozs.,
valued at £2,423,135. At the present rate of output,
this puts the mine about 3J years ahead of the mill; or,
put in another way, there is an approximate net profit in
sight of 11s. per share. Compared with the estimate of
12 months ago, the present one shows a slightly increased
tonnage, but a drop of £101,865 in gross money value. ,
# • • *
IVfore prodding with the diamond drill at
2800 ft. in the BOulder (W.A.) shows the body of ore
located plumb on the Horseshoe boundary to be 9ft. wide
ahfiworth 28d\vt. Additional boring at that depth from
the'Boulder shaft also shows the two narrow rich veins
to have 3ft. of 89s. stuff. So far as Boulder is concerned,
the question of speculative value is wholly confined to
the' things that development may bring forth below
2650 ft., where the co.’s present, bottom works are situ-
ated. In the case of the Horseshoe, whose shaft is still
some 800 ft. short of'2Booft. in depth, there are possibili-
ties in the shape of the discovery of good ore at the
various levels to be opened up en route. Ivanhoe also
Has a bit up its sleeve in this respect, as the ore body cut
by the drill is probably the downward continuation-of
the Boulder loop lode, on which little or no work has
been done below 500 ft.
* * * *
Golden Horeshoe (W. A.) has tacked a number
of improvements on to its new mill. Coarse screens are
used on the battery boxes and the pulp is run through
tube mills. The stuff is thus ground fine, and a lot of
time is sfived in doing it. Both the battery and tube
mills are driven by- electricity. Other additions to the
plant are a 65ft.-in-diameter tailings wheel, 12 extra
Wilfley tables, and four 200-ton cyanide vats filled by belt
conveyers. The fact of valuable ore being struck by- the
Boulder bore in Horseshoe ground 800 ft. vertically below
the present bottom level takes some of the roughness off
the track which the co. is now treading.

Hans Irvine, ex-newspaper man, had rare luck
when-he pouched the Black Range (W.A.), chiefly for
himself, but incidentally for a decent lot of sports in
Western Victoria. This is the way things have gone to
date:—
Tons. Oz. Gold. Dividends.
Battery .. .. 110,071 103,614 1 „ Ifia Kf ,„
Cyanide .. .. - 26,236 / 500
The high yield accounts for the creamy milk in this
cocoanut. Costs nowadays’ are being cut sharply, and
work out at 14s. 3Jd. per ton for mining and crushing,
3sj 4d. for-hauling and pumping, Is. 3£d. for office and Mel-
bourne expenses, and Is. 1-Jd. for etceteras, the whole
coming to 20s. Jd. per ton. Development meant 7s. 1 jd.
per ton additional for the past six months. This is stiff; but
the co. has spent a lot of money in going north to find out
if the stone makes beyond the crosscourse there, and has
had the luck'at No. 5 level to prove the pitch a good one.
This isn’t the best.piece of news by any means, though,
because at Nos. 7 and 8 levels assays for a run of nearly
400 ft,, oyer a width of 3Jft., average lfoz. per ton. Well
may Haris Irvine do a gush that values “ continue to ini-
prove-with depth,” and that shareholders can “ look with
eVety confidence to divs. for some years to come.” He has
made a. reputation as a .mining tipster, so one could wish
he had built his prophecy on a wider base than 45,000 tons
of ore in sight. Allowing 28,000 tons handled annually,
that is a two years’ reserve ; so it is, clear .that a big slab
of stone yet unproved is counted on to occur beyond the
crosscourse Or at greater depth. Nevertheless, seeing
that No. 8 level is only 550 ft. vertical, and that the shoots
have lengthened in the bottom levels!, Irvine can be given
some latitude, though even for a successful prophet it is
safest to stick to ore proved on three sides.

Anxious inquiries are being riiade in the West
concerning the whereabouts of the Derwent Jack Syndi-
cate. When the Bullfinch boom was in full blast, this
concern acquired a lease some three miles from the main
camp, and as a- result of reports that the 2oz. lode was
“ exactly similar to Bullfinch,”:the £lO shares issued to
the public .soared to £26 10s. Then the market tumbled,
andlatterly the shares have disappeared frdtri the quota-
tion lists. Recently word reached Perth from Adelaide
that the syndicate was about fojloa.t itself info a larger
co. ; but attempts made by, interested parties to confirm
this report or even get word aS t<s what is going on' behind
the scenes have 1 proved futilbib If this should meet the
eye of the lost, stolen or'strayed syndicate, it is requested
to make an effort to come forward and show itself; and
any reliable information respecting its intentions will be
gratefully received, especially by VVestralian shareholders.
The four battlers who own the Gimlet (W.A.)
paid for some quick sinking on the Gimlet South. They
sank an underlay shaft, 13ft. by 7ft., 375 ft. at the rate of
15ft. per working day—two shifts for three weeks, and
three shifts for one week. There were three men in the
bottom, and one used to give a hand to the engine-driver.
The ground was steatite, with iron and quartz leaders in
it, and it all went through the mill at the rate of 13’4 tons
per stamp per day. It averaged 9s. per ton over the
plates and 9s. in the slimes, and the ore was as slimy as
a snail farm in France.
* * * *
Babylonian (W.A.) came into the world with a
scream, and has held its breath ever since the float. It
was in 150,000 shares of 10s., of which 80,000 were issued
at 2s. application and 2s. allotment, and 70,000 fully-paids
and £5OOO cish went to the Greenfinch G.M. Syndicate.
Reports stated that “bulk samples of assays from the
ironstone lode, exposed for some distance by potholes
and where reef was exposed, gave a return of loz. 15dwt.,
and other assays made by Mr. J. L. Wallace gave from 2oz.
tolOoz. from the whole of the samples he took.” That un-
compromising pessimist, Mr. B. B. Pettigrew, also stated
that “gold shoot proved 400 ft. long ; surface values from
2oz. to lOoz. per ton.” A few weeks ago they costeened
the “ lode,” and found it to be floaters lying on granite.
The show may have a chance yet in the iron lode from
which the floaters presumably were shed ; but the 70,000
shares and £5OOO cash look a vast deal to pay for the
chance.

Cabled that the Westralia United Goldfields,
Ltd., has been floated by Herman Mandelstam and some
others of the Badly Chosen People, and that the W.U.G.
will have a capital of £750,000, and will work, among other
things, a collection of blue ducks at Kalgoorlie, known as
the North End mines. The cable doesn’t say how much
of the £750,000 is paper and hot air, nor whether the co.
is registered in England, where 5 per cent, of the capital
must be put up before registration ; or in Jersey, where
you can register a £20,000,000 co. if you have just enough
to pay the fees.

Randolph Bedford sticks to his high opinion of
Bullfinch: —“Bulletin (9/2/11) prints the yarn of ‘an
old mining man who swears he hasn’t a penn’orth of per-
sonal interest in the matter.’ The O.M.M. makes many
misstatements. No. 1 shaft of the Bullfinch bottomed
in ore ;,No. 2 is in schist, because lodes have a habit of
going out of shafts at some time or other; andtheNo. 2 ore
body dipped out at 90. There is a quartzite bar between
Nos. 1 and 2, and that is why two shafts were sunk 40ft.
(not 30ft.)apart; and as the No. 1 ore body is distinct from
the ore body in No. 2 shaft, driving from No. 1 wouldn’t
have proved the height of the richer ore in No. 2. No. 3
shaft was sunk in country and off the track of the ore
body. No. 4 shaft is 70ft. from No. 1, and is in 6oz. ore,
with 7oz. ore in the drive from No. 1 advancing to meet
it. The water shaft ore body, 275 ft. to the S.W., is un-
doubtedly identical with No. 2 ore body, and the work
now being pushed on will prove it so. Fred Morgan
states the value of the great body of ore in the water
shaft at 1305., and his statement is more to be
trusted than the 9dwt. prospect of the mysterious
‘ visiting engineer,’ and the anonymous ‘ indepen-
dent expert' who wasn’t permitted to go below.
The Bullfinch i- big enough to beat all the mysterious
‘independent experts.’ Prophecies are bad things to
make, as a rule ; but I dare say that, on the evidence of
the newest development in the water shaft, the connec-
tion of that working with No. 2 shaft should add
£500,000 to ore in sight dow'n to the 100 ft. level."
* * * *
Shareholders were all smiles at the Gold In-
dustry of W.A. lheeting, held in Perth on February 16.
The co.’s accounts show a profit of £46,098 on transactions
to date, and the previously-declared dividend of £35,000
—equal to 100 per cent, on the subscribed capital—was
vociferously agreed to. The co. now holds, among other
assets, 40,000 Bullfinch Prop, shares, and in his address
from the chair Vincent Shalleross said that the Board had
refused £2 apiece for 10,000 of these shares, and had also
declined to sell at even a higher figure.
Last year Whim Well (W.A.) produced 5738
tons shipping ore averaging 16 per cent, copper, and 16,462
tons smelter ore containing 5 per cent, copper, together
equalling 1741 tons fine copper. Total ore shipments
for the period were approximately 8500 tons of 16 per
cent, by steamer, and the balance,averaging 11 percent.,
by sailing ship.

The pioneer productive mine of Collie (W.A. ),
the Wallsend Proprietary, is a roaring hell again. Old
smouldering walls in abandoned workings, which were
directly responsible for two deaths from “black damp”
in New Year, week, have broken out beyond control.
Pqison fumes are now within a long hop, stop and a jump
of the old pit mouth, seriously jeopardising £llOO worth
of doal-cutting machines. The happening will have the
effect of hustling along the opening of a new tunnel on
the company’s extensive area.
Bendigonia hasn’t done much of a crawl yet.
This is the show the Argus puffeth in the hope that a hit
will swell up Bendigo’s business and ease down the call-
list. Bendigonia’s object is to prove that Bendigo
saddles exist at Ohewton. Scientists wheeze out that the
beds of Silurian there belong to the same age as those
to which the New Chum, Hustlers, Moon and other famous
bores at Bendigo belong, and that gold should, in conse-
quence, be got. Two Bendigonia bores have, however,
hit nothing but barren slates and sandstone, whereat
“ Geology ” Dunn went and covered himself with sack-
cloth and ashes. Queen-street luck came with; thb third
bore, which seems to have alighted on a, speck.of gold.
The Bulletin does not want to check John Brandon’s
vigor, because it likes a straight go. Still, with a wide
experience of boring results, it cannot yet boil its billy
with enthusiasm because a speck of quartz and gold have
come out of the core of a diamond drill. All the same,
it gives J. Brandon hearty ehbouragement to go on
boring. On the law of averages, it’s time for him to get
a decent show, to take away the taste of the failures that
have floated in and out of Queen-street during the last
five years.
★*’ * *
Two of the Finks keep a watchful eye over the
fortunes of the Great Southern Consols, Butherglen
(Vic.), just as one or two of them used to keep a finger
moving in the Burnt Creek pie. Their invasion has not
helped the decaying alluvial show. Just £385,000 has
been dropped into the Great Southern Consols, of which
nearly every shilling went down No. 1 shaft, for the mag-
nificent return of £19,000 in divs. Of course, the £385,000
didn’t all come out of shareholders’ pockets. That isn’t
the Victorian way; their bit was £46,000, leavings the
mine to provide the rest. This policy leads I/ictprian
deep lead shows into the worst of plights. Otie call to
keep the banker sweet, a six months’ run of work, then a
bit of a share flutter should there be one or two decent
yields, and away back to the banker again .for another
advance. If hard cash had been put up, panelling
stopped till wash was opened up and drained, and a
power of men to then rush out the wash, the Consols’
12Jdwt. dirt ought to have paid well, and should pay
still. With the run of gold narrow, this, indeed, is the
only way to make it pay. Manager E. Visick has started
a new shaft to better affairs, but if he crawls in the old
style the same round will be recorded. Good ground, he
says/ extends from 29 to 30 rises. Why not get a slab of
the gutter drained? That will give shareholders a show.
* ■* * *
• Champion, Ten Mile (Vic.), is an out-of-the-way
claim that, having just crushed 520 tons for ‘2490z., talks
divs. and booms as freely as any Bullfinch fledgling-. Cham-
pion is no novice. He has been in the share ring 1 ever
since the Star of the West shed brilliance on the mining
horizon. Crushings like the forementioned promise to
keep him there a bit longer. He is not a squib so far
as size of reefs'go. At No. 3 intermediate there is 14ft.
of indefinite matter described as “formation”; and, at
No. 2, stone 7ft. wide is boldly claimed to be pay-
able. So things are happier with the Champion than
they have been for many a day. Yet the Board
let one Morrison, a director, fork out £l7s' to meet cur-
rent exes, until the clean up. The same Board announces
that “ with the enormous quantity of crushing dirt in
sight, the future of the mine seems assured ” ; wherefore
dependence on Morrison, whether Morrison acted out of
generosity or not, was poverty policy. Champion has a
future, provided it can live down the reputation of the
Jamieson district for patchy ore. Water is available and
power cheap, so low-grade dirt will pay. The trouble
will be to convince the speculator that the Champion’s
big body has much vital matter in it.
* * * *
The Wonga, Stawell (Vic.), of which great
things were expected on account of the Moore vacuum
filter process, blew out as badly as any of the' other
attempts to work this alluring proposition on a big
scale. It only worked through about 60 tons or so, after
which operations ceased. There was some talk of im-
porting an electric hoist from America; then came a
re-sampling of the faces and the pronouncement that the
stuff was too poor to pay. As every load carted from the
open-cut was supposed, after exhaustive testing, to be
“ highly payable,” and as the highly-payableness could
hardly have evaporated in the open air, the end came as a
surprise to Stawell; but as the co. went into liquidation
and sold the plant, everybody has arrived at the harrow-
ing conclusion that “ there must have been something
wrong," though nobody knows exactly what. Whether
it was too little capital, too little gold, too little
experience, or a combination of the lot, the fact
remains that the latest attempt to work the
Wonga has resulted in more shattered hopes, reputa-
tions and banking accounts ; and the only people to date
who have no reason to shiver at the word Wonga are
the old-time miners who rooted out the best runs of
the stuff and crushed it and passed it slowly and care-
fully over copper plates and blanket tables. And most of
them are dead and past shivering, anyhow. Present
situation is that any man who wants a big Stawell repu-
tation can get it by merely dropping in and making the
Wonga lode pay. There is undoubtedly a lot of gold,
though it seems only a fair thing to add that it is mixed
with a lot of dirt.

For three months to the end of January Mt.
Elliott (Q.) smelted 11,450 tons of ore for 1521 tons
copper, thus obtaining an average extraction of practi-
cally 13 per cent, copper. A ton of blister can thus be
run out of between seven and eight tons of crudes, as
against 40-odd tons at Mt. Lyell and 30-odd tons at Mt.
Morgan ; and while this high extraction lasts, blister can
be produced for £2B per ton. Immediate needs are pfo-
videdfor; but a change must come someday. At 31ft.
down the winze below No. 4 level the ore assays only 7
per cent. ; and No. 5 level (550 ft. vertical) has been driven
219 ft., without meeting with better than 4 per cent. ore.
* * * *
Last fortnightly crushing of the New Brilliant
Freehold, Charters Towers, was 711 tons for £2708, or £3
16s. 3d. per ton. The deeper levels are not looking too
well; but the Freehold gives a wide scope for prospect-
ing. No. 5 level, on the hanging-wall reef, is showing a
decided improvement at 272 ft. from the boundary. This
is at the same vertical depth as No. 5 in the East Mexi-
can. There is a fair quantity of backs over this level in
the Freehold lease, although ultimately the reef will
slant upwards into the Mexican. The latter co. has not
done any prospecting for this body, because its levels are
fairly close to the boundary. It is becoming certain that
both the hanging-wall and footwall shoots are pitching
from east to West and into the deep levels of Mills’
United. Fortunately for the Freehold, it has 1200 ft.
along the line, besides having a number of parallel and
cross reefs to explore.

The Roma Mineral Oil Co. (Q.) has been search-
ing for a plant to bore 4500 feet. At a meeting of the
shareholders held recently it was announced gloomily
that no further applications had been made for shares.
One Joseph Porter wrote to say he had a plant capable of
boring to any depth ; and as the co. is mighty anxious for
a “friend in need,” Porter may possibly prove to be the
“ friend indeed.” The co. adjourned its meeting to talk
with Porter.

Witty, in the Logan (Q.) district. Nothing much was
done with it till a few. months ago, when a local co. was

and shafts have been sunk, and assays show results rang-

gave a return of joz, of pure gold. Boring has been com-

The Gqorge tin mines, comprising a group at
Sandy Creek (Q.), were floated in London lately with a -
capital of £50,000, under the name of the Standard-Con-
solidated Tin Mines. Present issue is 30,000 £1 shares ; and
J. Bull has an option over the remaining 20,000. Gerald

Times—gave an exhaustive and, on the whole, favorable
report oh the proposition. 1 As there is a good tonnage
of 3 to 4 per cent, stuff in the dumps, the co. shouldn’t
waste any time in rushing up a mill and. thus getting the
benefit of the present boom prices for tin.

northern slope of the mountain, where large boulders of
ore have been found, but so far no defined ore body.
Large sums of money- are to he spent on the central rock-
breaking plant, 'aerial ropeway and another reservoir.
Reserves of payable ore, with tin at £lBO, are fairly large,
because at that price very low-grade dirt will pay; ;but
the reserves of better r grade (for instance, those at the
brown face) are not heavy; the average of £ percent,
oxide proves that.

Every engineer in charge of factories and mills
should give close attention to the power wasted in trans-
mission of power. The Hyatt Flexible Roller Bearings
save 20 per cent, power and 75 per cent, of oil, so one of
the first duties is naturally for engineers to direct their
special attention to these bearings and to car boxes for
trucks. Hyatts are in continuous use in Cement Works,
Ore Dressing Mills, Factories, etc., in Australia. Bush-
ings of flexible rollers may be applied to loose pulleys or
truck wheels, etc. Should engineers require further
details, they can apply to the Australian Agents, W. &
J. Lempriere, Melbourne ; or E. W. Hursthouse & Co.,
of Wellington, the Agents in New Zealand.*

McKENZIE’S EXCELSIOR BAKING POWDER.
A BORIGIN-
ALITIES
“Jimmie Pannikin”: Evidently, sheep
have a better effect on man than cows have.
Most sheepmen, in my experience, have improved
their standard of living as they have prospered—
built better homesteads, surrounded themselves
with the comforts of civilisation, worn better
clothes, enlarged and beautified their gardens,
and entertained more often and more hospitably.
On the other hand, the cattlemen, as a rule, “pig
it,” just as they had to do in the old days of
strenuous struggle. Their bank balances bulge ;
but their homes and their home-life remain as
painfully plain as of old. One famous cattleman
right out back had no outhouses on his premises
—and still has none. When he bought the station,
he pulled down the fences round the expensive
vegetable garden and the lovely flower garden,
and turned the cattle and horses in for a “ blanky
good blow-out.” When remonstrated with, he
said he had “no flamin’ time fer picnickin’.” He
is only one of many, and if any Abo. can elucidate
the matter I’d take it as a friendly act. No
suburban Abo. need apply.
AN UNJUST CHARGE.
THE MEENISTER: “ McPherson , you hue brought
dishonor to Bonny Scotland the day."
THE McPHERSON: “ Dinna ye (hie) fash yerself,
mon. It didna (hie) cost me a nickel."
According to Howard, Chief Protector of
Black Brother in Bananaland, the Northern
Binghi is an accomplished person in the art of
making new words. Barracking for the Austra-
lian black brother as against the South Sea
Islander, Howard mentions that when the
islanders first became acquainted with hooks they
didn’t invent a name for them ; but waited until
the missionaries told the English name, and
then they called books as near to that as
their tongues could take them. Binghi, on
the other hand, noticed that a book had
two covers, that it opened and shut, and that
it was hinged at the back. It reminded him
of a mussel, so he promptly called it “ mooy-oom,”
which is what he calls that shellfish. “ Yacca” is
his word for work, and he calls reading, writing
and anything else done with a book “mooy-oom
yacca.” He calls the sun “ beegy,” and as it is his
clock, he calls anything in the shape of a watch or
a clock “beegy.” With him “paiango” means
sick, so when he found out what medicine was for
he called it “paiango-ba” (sick stuff). From
“mawgool” (head) he made “ mawgool-aba ”
(hat), “aba” being his word for covering of any
kind.
“DaveH. R.” : An appalling amount of
ignorance has been displayed by suburban Abo-
liars concerning the friendliness of native birds.
Why, blow it, some of these buslimen seem to be
stunned with amazement because a tomtit perches
on their nose, or builds a nest in Mrs. Bushman’s
hack hair. Bah ! Time after time, when I have
been “whaling,” and have bad nothing but the
bit of tea I’ve cadged at the last station, and have
put the billy on to boil, ducks, sometimes as
many as three, have flown up and dived straight
into the billy, and thus provided me with a meal.
Greater love hath no bird. The only trouble was
the feathers. Once, on the Barwon, I heard a
tremendous quacking amongst a mob of ducks,
and eventually out they all waddled on to the
bank and drew straws, and as soon as they decided
which bird had the shortest,' all the rest fell upon
it and plucked it before dropping it into the
billy. That’s what I call friendliness. And
then this gas about bird surgery! Surgery,
forsooth ! Here ! I’ve seen (and many a time
at that) a herd of birds corroboreeing over
one of their mates with a broken leg, and when
they’d decided on the best kind of bandage, some
of them have flown in and torn strips off my
shirts and other clothes, and taken out my needle
and cotton and sewn a bandage round the wounded
limb; and once a magpie flew into my humpy,
took my bottle of Friar’s Balsam out, and applied
some of it to the wound of a mate who had
suffered from a hawk. These instances are the
rule Outback; and if it’s Outback we’re talking
about, let’s have it from the men who know. I
tell you straight, old sundowners like “J. Panni-
kin” and myself and a few others are getting
full up.

“Stoopid”: This is the plain tale of a shock-
ing case of broken resolution. Two ladies and a
small male cherub —the cherub asleep—shared the
first-class carriage on a S. Aus. long-distance
journey with me. In the course of a fond eulogy
from the mother, it transpired that the cherub was
four. After a time the angel awoke—apparently
with a liver, because, on my shutting a near-by
window, it addressed itself to me with a flow of
profanity which made me blush for my early edu-
cation. Whereupon the fond mother exclaimed :
“ Oh, my ! his father will he so disappointed.
This is the first time he has sworn for twelve
months .”

“Martin K.” : In Bulletin 19/1 /’ll
“Giddap” asks: “Is the wood mouse marsu-
pial?” Some of him are. During’97 I was cut-
ting “ snap and rattle ” for Boulder mines in Bulla
Bulling (W.A.). There were a few mice about.
On high moral principles, I kill all mice, snakes
and gypsies. Killed a mouse one day. On con-
ducting a post-mortem on the deceased (always
conduct post-mortem on snakes, sometimes on
mice, but never on gypsies), found the usual mar-
supial pouch with three dead young ones and one
still alive in it.

“Leo Lear” : The other day I saw the
first rounds of a hush battle that for endurance
promises to hold out till the Conclusion of Things,
when the last three-hall sign shall have been
* hurled back over the walls of Jerusalem
and the cracked bones of dead beings
are awash in their desolated cellars.
The row started when a colony of white
ants laid the foundations of their citadel
round a prickly-pear bush, and encased
the bottom stories of the pronged curse
in mud. As fast as the termites build
skywards, the anathema also climbs, its
top leaves at each stage becoming the
trunk of a new plant growing on the
roof of the nest. Now, I know of
nothing that will beat a vegetable
hedgehog, and am likewise ignorant of
the thing that a white ant won’t master.
So the plant will keep oh sticking its
tail through the insects’ chimney pots,
and the ants will continue to smother it
as it grows, until the Last Trump is
played, and the firemen of the Bottom-
less Pit are bellowing in a loud voice
for a wages board to reduce their hours
of labor; and when the final politician
who turned dog on his country has been
slowly boiled in kerosene, and there is
nothing more for the Devil to do but
turn the lights out and impale himself
on his tail, the night-watchman in
Paradise will look over the waste of waters
and see a prickly-pear leaf waving above the
flood With the sole survivors of the ant nation
sitting jii the dry side waiting for daylight.
* * * *
“ The Downy Bird When it comes down
to sheer friendliness, I’m for the butcher’s canary
or meat-hawk. This genial little songster counter-
balances its individual smallness by assembling in
vast hordes in the vicinity of anything which can
he eaten or used as a promising field for the rear-
ing of its young. There is a pub I wot of in the
Aberfeldy Valley (Vic.), where dinner can only
be eaten free from their attentions by sending a
boy to ring the dinner-bell 300 yards away in the
bush. Every butcher’s canary within earshot flies
madly to the spot, and by the time the stratagem
is discovered all the diners are safe inside, the
windows shut and the door barred. The boy has
to stay outside until the others have finished, but
ttie language of the birds, keeps his meal hot.
* * * *
“ Gil Gunn ia ” : “Camp Fire’s” scheme
for baffling bugs—namely, by warding them off
with a blanket—is (1) old, (2) useless. I have tried
and I know. If “Jimmie Pannikin’s” wayback
castle is really Howard-infested, he will be well-
advised to absorb the following straight tip: Let
him take half a pound of potassium cyanide, and
melt it in his bedroom. The Norfolk-haunted
chamber should be kept closed up during the
process. After an hour the place will be
habitable. “ Pannikin” should beware of touch-
ing the cyanide with his fingers; also, let
him escape promptly, and leave no eatable
nor any living thing that he loves in the room
while the fumigating is in train. If James is
desperate and cyanide is unprocurable, I would ad-
vice him to fix up a hammock to the walls of his
sleeping apartment with a strand of manila. The
enemy cannot cross rope, and thus the bush ex-
pert will sleep undisturbed, save for the impotent
curses of the Howards below.
A TRIFLING MISTAKE.
BRIDGET GWENDOLINE: "Take these coppers , and. the missus sez
go away wid yer noise and don’t play here."
“ San “ Bash ” (B. 9/2/’ll) reports that
transplanted eucalypti are attacked by hares and
rabbits, while the “self-planted” escape. The
former vegetables are no doubt attacked because,
when first transplanted, the treelings droop a
good deal, and in that state are in some way
tastier to the beasties. It is known among
gardeners that a transplanted seedling is most
liable to attack by snails during the drooping
stage, and, if protected during that period, will
be immune afterwards. I have known snails to
gallop miles to eat down a drooping young del-
phinium ; yet, save the plant from their jaws,
and it will afterwards provide a roostmg-place for
the shelly reptiles without being in any danger of
being eaten by them.
“ Moulamein Concerning chummy bush
birds. At one of our backblock camps an un-
tamed magpie shared the family life. Other
small birds he jealously drove from the place.
We called him “The Guardian Angel,” mainly
because, during severe heat, he perched above us
with wings outspread. When a beast was killed
he sat beside the slaughterer, his beak red to the
eyes with sheep liver. At such times his smile
was good to see. After an absence of three years
we re-visited and stayed a night at the old camp.
At daylight Maggie appeared and demanded
breakfast with as much assurance as though we
had never left. Then he took his old verandah-
perch and warbled a song with his waistcoat
visibly broader.
“Yarraman,” from the Remote North, on
weather prophecies:—
A tribe near Anson Bay (N.T.), acting under instruc-
tions from its wise men, recently built u small raft of
wood, charred it and left it on the beach well .loaded with
stones. When the tide takes it away the rains will come.
At present, there is very little rain, because some blacks
further down the coast are jealous of those that are here,
and their medicine men are sending strong winds to keep
the rains away. Only when they are overpowered and
fall asleep will rain come. So the old men of oqr tribe
are waxing fat, because the more presents they get the
more influence wnl they exert oyer the distant wind-
blowers, and the sooner will the rains come.

“M.V.”: Once and for all, both emus
attend to the hatching of the eggs. I don’t know
what shifts they work, but dad is off duty some-
time after sundown, when he booms a serenade to
ma. One curious fact is
that when leaving or ap-
proaching the nest the
prospective parents walk
on the points of the toes,
making a faint three-
pointed track, very diffi-
cult to follow ; and the
same track is never fol-
lowed twice. Directly the
youngsters arrive from
Eggville the old man takes
sole control, shepherding
and steering them towards
the best emu apples and
wilga berries in the
vicinity. Mum never in-
terferes in the l manage-
ment of the flock. When
two cock emus guarding
young families meet, they
immediately advance to
battle, leaving their re-
spective offspring huddled
together. The victor col-
lects both broods and
departs for his home-
stead. In red country I
have seen a big, white-
necked cock with 45 young
ones of various sizes, and
birds with 20 and 30 are
common. Another un-
mentioned fact is that in
the emu’s plumage there
are two separate feathers
on the one stem. In
fact, there are so many unmentioned facts about
the emu that I call only conclude the writers on
emus have obtained their information from a
cursory examination of the relative of the ostrich
at the Zoo. For their special enlightenment I add
that the ben bird mostly lays the eggs.

“ Tin dog ” : Before we who live in
Broome (W. A.) were hit by the cyclone the other
day we knew that something was going to happen,
or was happening somewhere, though we didn t
know what. The air was dead, yet there was an
extraordinary sea-swell. When the storm came
it lifted the bottom out of ‘Roebuck Bay, and de-
posited it in the town. Fish were blown clean
out of the water. A large octopus Was whizzed
yards inland ; after gaining his breath he started
to cakewalk back to his native element again on
the extreme ends of his tentacles. One pearler,
who saw the beast first, thought he “had’em,’’
but being assured that the pub kept only the
best of spirits- in stock, he set the monster down
as the biggest daddy-long-legs on record.

THE BEACHCOMBER.
[For The Bulletin.]
Yes, that is what they name me, in pity and contempt,
A man with none to claim me, ill-mannered and un-
kempt ;
A beer and whisky shifter, part madman and part knave,
A hopeless, aimless drifter, spent flotsam of the wave.
Well, it is no misnomer. So, 'when I hear it said .
“SeeBlank, that old beachcomber!” with shakings of
the head
In Pharisaic unction, I pay no heed at all ;
Not thence can rise compunction within me for my
“fall.”
The sun still shines above me, and warms me through
and through,
And as for “ none to love me,” I know that love, I do !
The fond affection clinging to money, lands and stock,
The swift revulsion swinging from him who “ takes the
knock.”
I’ve felt “ the tender passion.” I might have lived,
unbanned,
In quite another fashibn, in some much staider land,
If Phyllis had been fonder, if Corydon less green—
I do not care to ponder upon what might have been.
More sinned against than sinning at times, perhaps, but I
Am noway now beginning my paths to justify.
I take a reputation that I’m too old to mend,
For, high or low in “ station,” we’re level in the end.
I have my pipe to cheer me, I’m lying by the sea,
A native girl is near me, she knows no god but me ;
I wear a lava-lava, ns frequently as not,
And drink my fill of Kava whenever I am hot.
I hear the palm-fronds making their trade-wind-trembled
croon ;
The white-roofed combers breaking beyond the reefed
lagoon ;
Mesmeric iteration of what I’ve learned so well,
The simple incantation that weaves the tropic spell.
So slip the days behind me, no turmoil in their wake ;
So their successors find me, quite satisfied to take
What good the gods have sent me to counter-weigh the
past,
A dolcefar niente, oblivion at last.
POLYNESIA. JlAKI.
* * « *
“ Bush Botanist ” : “ Marloo ” puts
“ Nunawading’s ” “ tea ” or “ ti ’’-tree (B.
12/l/’ll) deeper in the mud than ever. Captain
Cook, like most YorkieS, was fond of plant bever-
ages, from extract of barley and hops to lime
juice. His botanist, Joe Banks, backed him up
in this. Hence in Australia and Maoriland the
young shoots of Leptospermum soopartum were
taken on board, and from the captain to the cabin
boy all had “tea.” From that time to this, that
species of Leptospermum has been called tea-tree.
There is also a Leptospermum in S’Africa called
the tea-bush. Thousands of times the young
shoots have been used to make a pleasant aromatic
beverage. I have often used them myself. The
Melaleuca, which grows in swamps, is the ti-tree,
or, more properly, tie-tree. The strips of bark
were used by the firsters at Ports Jackson, Phillip
and Arthur in tying up the wattles in the making
of their wattle-and-daub huts. Some Melaleucas
produce a fibre strong enough to hang the man
who first said the tea-tree was the ti-tree and the
ti-tree was the tea-tree.

“ A. William-Cann ” : My
withers are unwrung by the
intimation (B. 16/2/’ll) that,
henceforth, “J. Pannikin”
proposes to “ignore” me.
It is a way James has in
certain circumstances. A
month or two ago Jim
and I were divagating,
in quest of bush lore
and so forth, back o’
Turramurra (N. S.
Wales). A cry of joy
told me that some-
thing particularly
choice in the golden-
glory - of - the - bush
line was adjacent.
In a trice, or less,
“ Pannikin ” had left
the main road, clam-
bered over a low
fence and started
hacking pieces off a
flowering shrub. A
few seconds passed,
and then an ominous
howl, wherein rage
and amazement
fought for mastery,
jjpunded from a villa
“'on the right. Simul-
taneously an im-
mense Australian—
to judge by his
physique one of the
Best or Sunset Side
crowd—-surged clamor-
ously in the direction
of “ Pannikin.” Jim’s
decision was arrived
at with the speed of
the master-strategist.
He (James) decided to
ignore the enemy—and
to do it soon. I remem-
ber the old foot-racing:
days at Sir Joseph
Banks’ Ground ; I have
seen a Chow bookmaker
leave a baclcblocks race-
course with half a hun-
dred bilked clients in his
wake ; and I have watched
Liberal voteresses advanc-
ing on an electioneering
“at home”—in fact, bar-
ring the rush of capital
from the Commonwealth, I
have observed most of Life’s
speedy things in my time.
The ignoring of that suburbanite
by “ Pannikin ” beat the lot. It is said
that Jim reached Cowan Creek under the hour,
and was only deterred from continuing to ignore
his pursuer in a boat by the solemn assurance of a
party of motorists that the letter had long since
given up the chase. A quaint feature of this
episode was the fact that the shrub whose at-
tempted ravishment had caused all the trouble
was not a wattle, after all, but a unique specimen
of the Ranunculus Asiatious Superbissimus. This
the maddened householder informed me when he
returned, hot and dusty, from his effort to inter-
view my discreetly-exclusive travel-companion.
“Walt. K. Harris”: “Agram” (B.
26/1/T1) says he has been all over Australia, and
awards the prize for biggest trees to Nana Glen,
via South Grafton (N.S.W.). I was in that dis-
trict when my Bulletin reached me. Promptly
I looked for the champion trees, but I saw nothing
to alter my contention (Lone Hand, Jan., 1911)
that not only Australia’s, but the world’s, tallest
trees are in Gippsland (Vic.). If “Agram” had
said that the biggest trees on the N.S.W. North
Coast are at Nana Glen he would have been
nearer the mark; though I saw some giants in
muddy Dorrigo to run those of the Glen pretty
close. “ Agram ” also backs the gohannas in the
Glen as the biggest in N.S.W., and mentions one
which measured sft. 6in. If “Agram” will ex-
plore Mangrove Mountain (near Gosford) he will
get a drayload of specimens that length. I took
a photo, of one which measured sft. 7in.; and even
that giant didn’t make the local inhabitant a bit
astonished.

“ T. J.M.” : If everyone is finished curs-
ing the cow, I want to make a few remarks about
the boss. In this (Goulburn Valley) district he
is god. The cookies lean against the fence at
church on Sunday and talk about him till the
preacher comes. They rise at any time between
2 and 4 a.m. to feed him ; and 10 o’clock at night
sees whiskered visions, lamp in hand, letting him
out of the stable. When Cocky, jun., goes to see
his girl on .Sunday night, he must feed his horses
first; and when he gets to the old man’s place he
finds a few blokes talking horses. About 11
o’clock they start to disperse, remarking: “Oh,
well, my horses are in the stable yet.” “So are
mine, by cripes ! ” “ Oh, Bill ’ll leave mine out.”
So he gets a yarn to his girl at last; but if it’s a
long yarn, and he has to hurry far through the
moonlight, he doesn’t turn in “’cos the hosses ’ll
have to be fed by four.”
* * * *
“Poll”: “Skidadle” says that a dame
at Cairns (Q.) is the first woman he has known
to do wood-slogging to keep the pot boiling.
When the Chalk’s No. 1 mine at Carisbrook (Vic.)
was lumbering the shareholders with calls, a, 20-
stone woman had a contract for supplying part of
the wood for fuel. It was no matchwood either—
6ft. ironbark logs cut and carted eight miles by the
lady herself. But she was three women lumped
into one ! ’Twas said she proposed marriage to
an eligible bachelor thereabouts. Business im-
mediately compelled him to leave the district.
The lady continued wood-slogging for five years
or more, and then passed out suddenly, dying in
harness. Anyhow, the horse was in harness and
she in the dray.
POST AND RAIL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Melbourne, Wednesday.—Mr. Justice Hood to-day de-
cided in the Practice Gourt that a watercourse or a creek
was a fence within the meaning of the Fences Act. —
News Item.
The solution this is of a riddle immense ;
No longer need anyone seek
For water at ’Berra. Since a creek is a fence,
Then a fence must be surely a creek.
If I’m wrong I’ll be glad if you’ll all set me right
(I know I am foolish and dense)—
When the spectre of Drought haunts the capital site
Will O’Malley then build them a fence !

Bellerive: It only needs a simple accompani-

DAISY MAY.
I miss thee, sadly miss thee, Daisy May,
Like a dream from this world thou passed away ;
No more for thee I wait by the dear old homestead gate,
Thou art anchored with the angels, Daisy May.
Sad and lonely I wander, Daisy May,
Memories hover o’er me where’er I stray ;
The golden sunlight gleameth
And the stars of night doth beameth
Upon thy sepulchre in the churchyard, Daisy May.

fatal to make an after-dinner admission of this kind :
I found to-day a scrub turkeys’ nest,
And I took their eggs for food ;
After dining I laid me down content
In a calm state of beatitude.
A soulful poet should never be seen recumbent with his
hands folded over his waistcoat.. . .Norseman : Weha\e
taken the liberty of rejecting it . . .C.T.E.B. : It reads
like an illuminated address :
FAREWELL TO A FRIEND.
It is with feelings of regret
And a sprinkling of pleasure
That friends have come from far and near
To show our guest what great respect
We have for one so dear.
We all admire the busy life
That she has led amongst us ;
To sick and poor she’ll find her way
Through sunshine and through shower.
It is nice, easy verse to write. Here is a slight addition
by the Religious Editor:
Although I am not acquainted with the lady,
It affords me considerable pleasure
To note that her friends have rolled up in large quantities
to see her off.
The general esteem in which she is held
Is very gratifying—even to a perfect stranger like myself.
It is to be hoped that when she goes to visit
The sick and the poor “through sunshine and through
shower”—as mentioned above —
She is always careful to take her goloshes, macintosh
and umbrella.
Because this weather is really terrible.
One can’t depend upon it for an hour.
Is she staying long?

There is many a girl in a lighted pub
To whom I would be a brother,

THE
BEAST
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Part I. —/ please my wayward senses;
’Twas but a week or two ago
I drew a picture ; not for show,
But to adorn my studio.
“This virgin Sheet,’’ I cried, “shall hold
All prowling monsters new and old
That gruesome Fiction ever told.’’
A horrid Hydra first I drew,
A sensual beast he was to view;
And near him was a Loup-Garou.
Amid a terrifying flock
Of slimy Dragons, on a rock
I drew a Snark and Jabberwock.
Also a Bunyip I had drawn,
A Bunyip basking on a lawn,
Laved in the livid light of dawn.
And Gorgons and Chimaeras dire,
With heads held high and tails held higher,
Were spouting smoke and spitting fire.
Such frightful creatures as I took
From fact and fancy, brain and book !
The picture had a Jim-Jam look.
Part 11. The A wful Consequences.
A Friend came in : a man of grace:
An Ornament to any place;
A Bachelor of Arts; his face
With meditative wisdom shone.
He was a Leader-Writer on
The Daily Megatherion.
He saw my Picture, and he shied ;
He wagged his head from side to side;
His knee-joints trembled; then he cried:
“ Good God , the Labor party !" —and
Foamed, spat and slavered; couldn’t stand *
And when I helped him, bit my hand.
I shuddered at his maniac wrath,
But wiped his whiskers free from froth,
And flicked him with a moistened cloth.
And when his frenzy had decreased,
And foaming at the mouth had ceased,
He wrote a Leader on the Beast.
It was a funny thing to do.
But Leader-Writers hold the view
That Labor is a Loup-Garou.
VICTORIA.
P.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
A man who was remotely connected with
Australia’s first beer, passed out the other day when
Timothy Small died at Ryde, aged 77. His grand-
father, James Squire, arrived in Sydney by the
first fleet, cultivated hops, and established the
first brewery in Australia, at Ryde. It is well he
got in early ; had he waited long enough he would
certainly have heard that beer could not be made
in Australia.
Died on the Upper Orara (N.S.W.), aged
94, the Rev. John Bannatyne, one of the pioneer
ministers of the Seotchbyterian Kirk of Australia.
He was a graduate of Glasgow University, and
could keep silent in six languages—except at an
Assembly meeting, where no good Presbyterian
divine can keep silent at all. When he was much
younger he pioneered the roughest districts of
Maoriland ; but Dr. Lang induced him to come to
Australia and start shepherding on the North
Coast.
Thomas George Clarke, a big grazier with
holdings in the neighborhood of Wangaratta
(Vic.), died in Egypt the other day of pneumonia.
Tom Clarke, his father, was one of the first set-
tlers in north-eastern Victoria. His mother, who
is still alive, is a daughter of the late Joseph
Docker, a pioneer parson. She claimed to have
been the first white woman to cross the Murray.
Thomas George, who was born on a station 54
years ago, leaves a young widow, lots of money
and no children.
SCANTLEBURY-THE HILL END
CAESAR.
J. W. Colville, who has been 48 years in
the Vic. Public Service and 26 years secretary of
the Board of Health, has just retired by effluxion
of age. The Department that cares for Victoria’s
innards will suffer by parting with him ; but the
State will be no loser if he can help it, for he’s full
of political aspirations. He was among the Labor
party nominations for the last Kooyong contest;
but was thrown out in the final ballot, and will
now most likely keep his eye on the P’ran seat,
with a view to shifting Donald Mackinnon when
opportunity offers.
Thomas W. McKenzie, one
of the best-known figures in Well-
ington (M.L.) history, moved on
last week to the land where there
is neither longitude nor latitude,
and time is not. He was 84 when
the Man with the Scythe tapped
at his door. McKenzie sailed from
London with his mother in the
emigrant ship Adelaide, and
reached the colony in March, 1840.
Soon afterwards he was apprenticed
to S. Re vans, the proprietor of the
first newspaper published in M.L.
(the M.L. Gazette and Britannia
Spectator). For many years he
followed the rough inky way of the
time; was one of the founders of
the old Wellington Independent,
and afterwards the sole proprietor;
established the Wellington Mail,
also the Almanac ; and for long was
manager and secretary of the Times
(still in existence). A man of strong
personality, McKenzie’s influence
was very marked in local politics,
and his probity amongst the early
settlers and later citizens was
reckoned at par with the currency.
Wherever Charity was moving its
machinery, it was safe to look for
him oiling the wheels, and earning
for himself the Order of Merit in
the Promised Land. *
Artist John Loqggtaff is back
in Melbourne, increased in girth
and reputation. He has been away
in the Fog for about 10 years, and
has returned to seek some days in
the sun. During his stay he pro-
poses to meet a lot of Australian
friends and paint a set of Australian
scenes. The latter will be taken to England in
May, and will form the nucleus of a London
exhibition.
Rose Bay Lodge, another of the “historic
homes” of Sydney, is up for auction. It was
built and occupied by Dan Cooper, the first
baronet, and was the home for many years of
John Hay, President of the Legislative Council.
The lodge was the only possession of the Coopers
that Anthony Trollope envied them. At least,
Anthony said it was the only one.
Nasty remark of the Age :
Lord Dudley’s successor as Governor-General of Aus-
tralia will be Baron Denman. Thomas, third Baron Den-
man, comes of a purely professional stock—lawyers,
clerics and medical men. Lord Denman has nothing to
be ashamed of in regard to his forebears.
As the boarding-house missus might say, “Lawks,
mum, as soon as I claps eyes on the label on your
luggage I says, * This is a lady.’ Now, the last
party as was here ”
You can tell the immigrant ship from the
liner, nowadays, by the youths and children who
don’t crowd the liner’s decks, but do the immi-
grant’s. Kiddies by the score swarmed the Ran-
gatira as she came in last week. Two sets of
parents boasted 10 each. An ex-imperial ser-
geant brought his seven strapping sons along.
Another had four sons and three girls, who will
all have votes in a year or two.
When Iceberg Irvine, K.C., in Victoria,
applied to be made a K.C. in N.S.W. also,
Attorney-General Holman searched for prece-
dents as to the appointment of an absentee prac-
titioner. Not finding any, he threw the Iceberg’s
application out. In theory, at least, K.C.’s are
appointed who are locally available to assist the
Crown when needed; and with two new K.C.’s
“taking silk” this week, there are already 16
silk gowns at the N.S.W. Bar, not counting Dr.
Donovan, who has retired, and G. H. Reid, who
is in England keeping the dog dry.
The Argus recently printed an article
about suppressed Australian books. The writer
mentioned that the Parkes Government ordered
the burning of all convict lists in N. S. Wales.
But, before separation, copies of these lists—well-
bound books—were sent to all police out-stations.
The late Justice Kerferd found a case full of them
in the Sale (Vic.) courthouse, and ordered them to
be sent to the Vic. Crown Law Offices. After-
wards Kerferd became Attorney-General of Vic-
toria, and, visiting Sydney as an ambassador re-
garding something in dispute between the two
States, fancied he was coldly received in official
circles. Whereupon he recalled that case of
books, and chuckled over the way he had pre-
served what N.S.W. thought it had annihilated
forever.
D. G. Ferguson, who will be Acting-Judge
of N.S.W. Supreme Court in the absence of
Cohen, J., is a local product, an old Fort-street
boy and a B.A. of Sydney University. At one
time he was on the Sydney press, and has been a
member of both the N.S.W. and Queensland
Hansard staffs. He reported several speeches
made by Lord Rosebery, when that eloquent
nobleman was in these parts. Rosebery used to
say that Ferguson reported him more intelligently
than any member of the craft he had ever met.
The new Judge is a Common-Law man, about the
best “pleader” at the local Bar, and as full of
modesty and diffidence as he is of learning.
N’York papers report the death in an asy-
lum of Robert Caldwell, the chief witness in the
Druce case. Caldwell, who is known in the land
of the tin ham as “ the king of affidavit-makers,”
swore that he put 2001 b. of lead into a coffin and
buried it —the coffin being supposed to contain
the body of T. C. Druce. The case of the
claimant to the Portland dukedom was that T. C.
Druce and the duke were one, and that the bogus
burial, sworn to by Caldwell, was the eccentric
old aristocrat’s method of ending his dual life.
However, when the coffin was opened, it was
found to contain a body whose profuse whisker-
ings and other disfigurements enabled it to be
readily identified as the defunct Druce. So
Caldwell bolted with a warrant for perjury in his
wake. America, possibly as a tribute to his unique
lying powers, gave him sanctuary from British
justice till he died.
NELSON ILLINGWORTH, the Sculptor,
whom the Maoris have made a chief
out of love for his “grey mud-pictures.”
Many thousands of years ago the Maoris,
in their monster war canoes, came over the waters
from Hawaiki, and peopled Maoriland. With
them came the tribal wood carver, who used to
make ugly little gods with fat bingies and three-
fingered hands. His name was Raurakitai, and
he came in one of the famous Arawa canoes.
Nelson Illingworth, the Australian sculptor, has
been doing a lot of good work with Maori sub-
jects, and whilst living amongst them at Rotorua,
the brown men became so enamored of the grey
mud pictures that they made him chief, and called
him Raurakitai. Somewhere in this issue is a
picture which shows how he dresses the part.
Dead, aged 80, ex-Puisne Judge Robert
Patten Adams, of Hobart—“ Cookie” Adams, in
the familiar language. He was the last of the
three Judges whom the present Judges succeeded,
his confreres being th 9 late Lambert Dobson,
C.J., and A. Inglis Clark. Evidently he had a
great opinion of these two, for he always con-
curred with them. Till a year or two ago, he
was what the dailies called “ a picturesque figure ”
about town —but not much of a figure ; only about
4ft. In politics, he was a virulent anti-Sosher,
and most of the other things that his brainy col-
league, Justice A. Inglis Clark, wasn’t.
Albert Dorrington has got London by the
wool. The Pall Mall gives him the place of
honor for a serial, and booms it thus: “Has
broken ground where the Goddess of Science still
beckons from her unsealed pinnacles . . . Keen
vision combined with a perfect genius for dramatic
situations . . . startle and bewilder the human
mind.” The first story turns on the theft of £6OOO
worth of radium from a locked laboratory.
Sherlock Holmes himself might have been puzzled.
The stuff, it seems, was taken by a trained rat,
sent up a drain-pipe !
The Oldest Inhabitant:—
As Akenehi says (B. 2/3/T1), the red tiles of North
Sydney won’t need to blush for the family of Colonel
Ryrie, even if their owners do drop him—a square peg—
into the round hole of a Parliamentary seat, and subse-
quently blush for his knowledge of politics. Stewart Ryrie,
of Sydney andArnprior, Braid wood, was one of 12 brothers,
Scotch. He was in the Commissariat service—looked
after the tucker for the army—and served in the Penin-
sular and at Waterloo. At Waterloo he received from
General Picton the field-glass he(the General) used during
the battle. (Mem. : Picton was killed.) Stewart Ryrie
came to Sydney in 1825 as Deputy Commissary-General.
He married twice. His first wife was Anne Stewart,
sister of General Stewart, who was Acting-Governor for
18 days in Dee., 1825. General Stewart lived at Mount
Pleasant, near Bathurst, and is buried under some
tons of monumental masonry there. The second
wife was Isabella, third daughter of John Cas-
sels, of Arnprior, Scotland. She was the sister of
his daughter-in-law. Among the children of this
second marriage was Alexander Ryrie, M.L.C. ; the
latter married a daughter of Captain Alured Tasker
Faunce, who settled in N. S. Wales in 1832. The candi-
date for North Sydney, Colonel Granville De Laune
Ryrie, is the son of this Alexander Ryrie.
A few years ago, Benjamin and Mary
brought their long hair and gold teeth to Australia.
They were special agents of the Almighty, and
their particular purpose waB to collect a band of
zealots to follow them into an elysium near
Chicago. There, according to Benjamin, people
enjoyed an eternal existence freed from material
worries and mundane cares. Between them, the
couple actually secured about 80 adherents,
who gave up their trades and j*bs for the joys
of an exquisite immortality. Arrived at the
selection, however, they found that it was rather
spoilt by a primitive system of drainage. More-
over, they discovered that the boss crank was a
tyrant, with a sordid love of riches, and a despotic
control over the £6OOO he had got from the “con-
verts.” He exhorted them to starve their passions
by subsisting on potatoes and haricot beans, and
eschew marriage ; but, as regards himself, being
already, no doubt, sufficiently chastened, lie put
fine clothes on his back and better dinners in his
stomach. Finally, there was a revolt of the least-
deluded, and several have now left the tents of
Benjamin and the footstool of Mary; and the
whole beautiful piece of humbug is in danger of
collapse.

The Denman who achieved a peerage, and
thus made it possible, as things are, for his grand-
son to become G.-G. of the Commonwealth, was
Brougham’s colleague in the defence of Queen
Charlotte, when that Royal blackguard, the fourth
George, tried to divorce her. George abandoned
the illiterate woman when the first child was born.
As soon as he got formally to the Throne, 24
years afterwards, divorce proceedings were begun
in the House of Lords. Brougham and Den-
man no doubt had a weak fool for a client;
but their defence was so brilliant that the
business was dropped. The lady had previously
been shut out from Westminster at the
Coronation ; and the story is that the blow killed
her. Anyhow, she was dead within a month.
There was, by the way, a rather sensational and
more successful divorce matter in the Denman
family itself—in the second generation.
Sir John M. P. Fuller, the new Governor
of Vic., is the first “ moon-raker,” or Wiltshire-
man, to get into an Australian Government House
as lord thereof. The Fuller family has been
associated with West Wilts for a long time, and,
thanks to a large income, partly derived from
brewery shares, numerous members have taken a
prominent position in most matters. Sir John’s
father, G. P., was M.P. for the division for several
terms, though he was more at home driving a
coach-and-six than in the House. Son John has
had at least two curious election experiences.
One was when he unsuccessfully fought North-
West Wilts against his bosom friend. Sir John
Diekson-Poynder. The two candidates went
about arm-in-arm, sometimes speaking after each
other at meetings. The second was when he re-
won West Wilts for the Liberals. He had married
Miss Phipps, a charming Wiltshire lady—she
being, as he said, a worse Radical than he was—
whose father was an old Tory and chairman of the
Tory party association. The old man did his
best, by canvassing and speaking against him, to
defeat his son-in-law. Fuller, was deliberately
educated for a political a fair speaker,
though no orator, is of the jolly rather than the
serious type, has plenty of common-sense, and in
his county was regarded as strongly democratic.
L. McCLELLAND,
Victoria’s newest Railway Commissioner.
When E. W. O’Sullivan first passed out,
the N.S.W. unions tumbled over one another in
their desire to do something to perpetuate his
memory. A committee was formed to raise
funds ; but big strikes supervened, and the com-
mittee raised only a little over a hundred pounds.
It is, however, proposed that a new wing to be
built at the Trades Hall shall be called “ The
O’Sullivan,” and a statue of the departed en-
thusiast placed in the vestibule. He earned the
honor ; for, with all his financial faults, O’Sullivan
was a generous man to Unionism. In fact, about
the only good thing he was not generous to was
himself.
THE ONLY SOLDIER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Strange to say, in this very matter whereon Mr. Con-
roy attacks them the women have proved their prefer-
ence for the fighting man. Whom did they select
amongst the list of a dozen aspirants, including an ex-
State Minister, an ex-senator and other distinguished
politicians? Why, the only soldier amongst them,
Colonel Ryrie.— Argus (2S/S/'ll.)
To meet Colonel Ryrie.—The Mayoress of North Syd-
ney requests the pleasure of your presence at the Town
Hall on Friday, March 3, at 3 o’clock. R.S.V.P.
The “ Lib’ral ” from his feather-bed springs up to face
the foe,
While swarms of eager women watch their lovely
soldier go
Into the tea-fight. Strong his arm to pass fresh buns
around
When perfect-lady leaguers thump high heels upon the
ground.
No matter what the risks may be,
With all a curate’s ardor, see
The Liberal from his feather-bed spring up to hand the
tea 1
No feather-bed has power to hold the fighting Colonel
back
When Duty calls him plainly tj get busy on the track
Which leads ahead to glory, per the tea-pot and the host
Of cream-puff girls who think that he will Fisher spread
on toast;
Hail him, the fighter in the van,
Rushing around with tea, to fan
The flame of parlor wrath ! Pour on, courageous soldier-
man !
There is no ease in feathers with plain Duty calling loud
For fighting-men to down the bold, blood-lusting Labor
crowd !
Who will come forward bravely, with his feather-bed
forgot,
And aim old tarts with martial skill at all the Labor lot?
Here is the man ! Oh, see him take
The standard up for Duty's sake
And gallop into battle, armed with loads of tea and cake !
VICTORIA.
FRANK HENTY.

TRIED IT ON THE HORSE.
“When driving: from Tamworth to Bowling
Alley Point, one of my horses took bad with
gripes and got down kicking and struggling,”
say's Mr. Sam L. Pyrke, of Bowling Alley Point,
N.S.W. “Those who passed me considered the
horse would die. As I always carry Chamberlain’s
Colic, Cholera and Diarrhoea Remedy for my
own use in case of emergency, I put a whole
bottle of it in half a pint of water and gave it to
the horse. In less than a quarter of an hour he
was up and started to feed. It is Chamberlain’s
Colic, Cholera and Diarrhoea Remedy that I have
to thank for saving me a good horse.”*

Nagging Neuralgia.
+
This Woman Spent Weeks in
Agony Until She Tried the
Tonic Treatment,
Many who are tortured with neuralgia will
read with interest the statement of Mrs. J.
Adcock, of Bunburv, W.A., who was cured of a
very severe case by Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills.
Her experience is only one of many, as these pills
have cured a host of cases, and those who have
used them for neuralgia have gone on sound
medical lines. Neuralgia is a nervous disorder,
and experience has proved that there is no better
nerve tonic than Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. Mrs.
Adcock, whose husband has a store on Whites
Road, lives at the corner of Railway Parade and
Carey Road. ' She stated :
“ We were living in Derby, and the climate there
affected my nerves and brought on neuralgia. I
suffered dreadfully for weeks, and in spite of all
the remedies I tried I could not find any relief.
The pain was present all the time. I could not
sleep or eat, and fell away in looks. I would try
applications of hot salt and hot bran, and anything
that I might get a little ease from. Every nerve
of my face gave me pain. It would throb up to
my temples and affect the back of my eyes. I
could not see to read or sew. At night I could
not sleep. I would rise and walk about quite
frantic. It was in cold weather and I dreaded to
go outside, the nagging, shooting pains always felt
worse, and I would often have to bandage my face
to avoid a chill. ■ I don’t know how I got through
the day’s duties, but they had to be done. I tried
many remedies all in vain, then I read in a news-
paper about Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills, and Mr.
Adcock got them forme out of ourstore. They grad-
ually toned up my system, and drove the neuralgic
pains away. I am always glad to recommend
this tonic, for I have never had an attack since,
my health remaining capital. I took three boxes
altogether.”
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills are sold by chemists
and storekeepers, or. sent by mail, post paid on
receipt of price, 2s. 9d. per box, six boxes 15s. 3d.,
by the Dr. Williams Medicine Co. of Australasia,
Ltd., Sydney.*
