POLITICAL
POINTS
The clay when the Labor party was com-
posed of men with corns on their hands has
passed—-if it ever prevailed. There were two
by-elections in Queensland the other day. One
Labor candidate was a clergyman ; the other a
tutor.
Bigjohn Forrest is no happier than he was
a month ago. His Bunbury seat in the local
Assembly was captured by a Laborite the other
day. Newton Moore, ex-Premier and now on his
way to the Agent-General’s office, had been sit-
ting in it lately.
The Brunswick (Vic.) Council has decided
to float a loan of £30,000. The example of various
State Governments is certain to spread, and this
is the first Victorian instance to date. At such
times as the present nothing is easier than to
float a loan ; but there are a number of easier
matters than repayment, no matter what the
times are. Such an idea as striking a higher rate
is too simple ; besides, it might make Brunswick
councillors unpopular.
W.A. Premier Wilson is in a curious fix.
After all the scheming to get the Redistribution
of Seats Bill through, some one has found a hole
in it. Outside Albany is Breaksea Island, with a
population of six ; and Breaksea has been left off
the new electoral map, wherefore the blessed
Con’stooshun is alleged to be shivering. But it
isn’t shivering half as much as Wilson, who sees
the great jerrymander in danger of being cap-
sized.
Grant, the new Minister for Public In-
struction in the North, is reported to have told
his Rockhampton (Q.) constituents that he “re-
luctantly accepted a portfolio.” It is time some-
body invented a fresh way of accepting a port-
folio. Will some politician please earn The
Bulletin’s gratitude by saying, “I accepted the
position of Minister with joyful alacrity, and
intend to hang on to it as long as ever I can ! ”
“At present any claimant under a State
law had the right of appeal to the Federal Court,
and no protest was made against this over-riding
of State jurisdiction or sovereignty. The Flinders-
lane merchant recovering a debt, or the Collins-
street stockbroker pursuing a claim, had a right
of appeal which nobody questioned ; but when a
Sunshine worker asked for the same power, then
the sovereign rights of the States were in danger!”
—Andrew Fisher , on the Industrial Referendum.
Prime Minister Fisher made a good point
when removing the “nationalisation of the drink
traffic” red herring from the referendum trail.
“ We have read,” said he, “that the whole tem-
perance section of the community is up in arms
against our proposals because they will give power
to nationalise the drink traffic. The States can
do so now, if they wish. Apparently the people
who say these things think the States can be
trusted better than a Government whose every
member is a teetotaler ! ”
A Victorian Wages Board decided recently
that certain bootmaking work, hitherto done by
men operatives at 545. per week, may in future be
done by women at 20s. It is one of many sound
reasons for voting “Yes” on the Referenda
questions. Neither Queensland nor N. S. Wales
permits cheaper labor in the boot industry.
Which means that the Murray State manufac-
turers, while acting in a legal manner, will get an
inequitable advantage over the manufacturers and
operatives of the other States until there is some
authority to bring the conditions into harmony.
Melbourne Argus is only an unmitigated
State Rights’ advocate when there isn’t any
authority more Tory than the State in view.
When it can see something more Tory, then
sacred State Rights are all humbug. Just now
the old paper is quite peevish because it suspects
every morning that the Murray Government con-
templates scooping up the tramways. The idea
of the Argus at the moment is that the running of
tramways is the sacred duty of municipalities.
The whole explanation is that the municipal coun-
cils are still Tory bodies, and so the Tory Argus
harks back to them. The “sacred rights,” in
short, always belong to the Toriest body.
The Argus, and Fatman in general, wept
crocodile’s tears over the Victorian ironworkers,
and pointed out to them that they were making a
clear loss in wages of £4972 per week. Fat
always weeps the same tears; but when, as in
this case, other employers lock their workmen
out, he quite forgets to bewail the folly of em-
ployers in scorning their bread and butter. In
such a case the employers “ make a momentary
sacrifice for the sake of an abiding principle” ; and
Fatman applauds his brother vigorously for it.
The probability is that Edward Tregear,
the recently-retired Secretary for Labor (Maori-
land), will shortly enter politics, and the side of
the fence on which he will be was made clear a
few days ago. Sowise :
He would like to say that he saw in the Labor move-
ment, the only hope for the majority of the men in this
world, whatever hope they might have of another. He
saw in the Labor movement that desire for the spread of
human helpfulness and friendship, a feeling without
which it would be impossible to raise the masses, and the
masses could not receive it without their bodies were
first well clothed and fed. They must remove the
drunkenness, the debauchery, dirt and disease before
they could make room for the higher ideals which, like
other property, was at present the possession of people
with palaces and motor-cars.
Tregear says that he is glad to be quit of his
official job, so that he can be of still more service to
the workers. Which makes the above-mentioned
probability more probable.
HIS VALENTINE.
Premier Denham, who spent a foolish,
futile fortnight battling in Rockhampton (Q.) to
save the meek Tory lambs from the ravening
wolves of Labor, made the usual remark after the
election that the Rockhampton result did not
indicate the state of politics in that town. Some-
how the remark always reminds The Bulletin
of the lady who told Ferguson, the Broken Hill
strike leader of ’92, after his arrest, that “stone
walls do not a prison make nor iron bars a cage.”
“No, madam,” said Ferguson, as he looked out
through the grating. “ Perhaps not. But I find
them a wonderfully effective substitute.”
At McCutcheon’s meeting in Melbourne,
according to the Age report: —
Mrs. Morton, of the Australian Women’s League, urged
the ladies to make sacrifices to secure men to vote against
the referenda questions. Women had great influence,
she said, her first convert having been made in the
Garden of Eden.
What does the lady mean ?
NO FAULT OF HIS.
THE AVIATOR: “Dash it all! And my
doctor told me to give up sea bathing!”
There is one point which can’t be driven
home too hard and too often—the Commonwealth
Government does not ask for an atom of power
which the State Government does not already
possess ; it does not ask for leave to do one single
thing which the State is not entitled to do already.
Thereupon the question arises, is Fisher to be
trusted less than McGowen ? Or (supposing the
Labor party to be thrown out, as no doubt it will
be some day) is Joe Cook the monster who is
going to produce the dreadful things which will
drive Spoof Smith to rebellion?
Elwood Mead, who went up to Swan Hill
the other day to see how his “ novices ” were get-
ting on, found one man, who had landed in Mel-
bourne last Christmas, already making £2 15s. a
week from butterfat. Besides this he has pigs
and poultry, and was busy ploughing when
the irrigationist looked in. Next to him, another
new chum was drawing £3 a week from the butter
factory, and a third £2 18s. Some of the families
are still living under canvas; but they realise that
tent life in Australia isn’t quite the same as tent
life in the Cold Land, and are bogging in hopefully.
The other night, when Deakin was burb-
ling on the referendum at Ballarat (Vie.), he im-
plied that the nationalisation of Australian rail-
ways would be a terrible indiscretion. A little
later in his oral fantasia, however, he produced
this stimulating statement
There is a certain aspect of railway management which
relates to defence,, and there Australian interests pro-
perly override every other interest.
Thus Alfred makes out the strongest plea for the
nationalisation that he bitterly dislikes. Which
again demonstrates the futility of refuting the
gentleman’s arguments. He refutes them himself.
The crowd which shrieks “No” to the
Referenda, being very hard pushed for argu-
ment, thinks it has a trump card in the Home
Rule gag. It asks the world at large to consider
the “ inconsistency ” of a party which advocates
Home Rule for Ireland and “seeks to destroy
the complete system of Home Rule in Australia.”
There is, of course, no analogy. The Australians
of Perth and of Sydney are considerably more
alike and have more interests in common than
the Englishmen of Devon and Durham ; yet there
is no proposal to make Devon and Durham sep-
arate States. But, on the other hand, the Irish-
man belongs to a subject race which has never
been assimilated, and his interests are not those
of England. Newcastle possibly has, in its re-
lation to Sydney, some analogy to the position of
Ireland in relation to England. Both places have
been sacrificed for the bloating of the other place ;
and if the people vote “Yes,” Newcastle will be
a step nearer to getting Home Rule for itself.
Justice Sim’s administration of the Maori-
land Arbitration Court isn’t unlikely to lead to
trouble. Recently the Otago Trades and Labor
Council Wrote to Minister for Labor Millar tell-
ing him that, unless something was done immedi-
ately, the unions might have to be advised to
adopt some other method of settling industrial
shindies :
If it is intended to mark time and allow things to drift,
there is likely to be an industrial upheaval such as Maori-
land has never previously known, during which thou-
sands of innocent people will be made to suffer. If you
will take a strong stand and say this state of things shall
not continue, we have no hesitation in saying the Con-
ciliation and Arbitration Act will be as acceptable to the
majority of the workers as it was in days gone by.
One of the leading M.L. newspapers, in referring
to a recent judgment, said : “It would not need
many such decisions to bring the whole system of
arbitration into disrepute.” Another remarked,
“The Arbitration Court is doing its best to defeat
the wise purpose of the Legislature.” Still
another : “ A few more decisions of this kind will
Strain the patience of the workers to the breaking
point.” Millar talks about amending the Act.
But the unions reply, “ It ain’t new law we want;
it’s new Judge.” And, by the look of things, new
Judge it has got to be.
An old passage in the Yic. Assembly
in the early "’!)o’s is recalled for the
benefit of Spoof Smith and Solemn Lee.
“Thank God for the sacred right of
revolution!” cried Jack Hancock. “And
thank God for the sacred right of hanging,”
replied Dunky Gillies.
The Age and Deakin have forgotten their
political differences, and have become the hap-
piest of friends again. The latter now compli-
ments the former by quoting from its columns,
and the former retaliates by describing his meet-
ings as “ enthusiastic.” Yet with all their love,
inspired by hostility to the Referendum, they
contradict one another in a reprehensible fashion.
For instance, Alfred observed the other day
It has remained for the present Government to render
ttie virtue of Referendum next to nothing, for they have
put together four separate clauses with five sub-clauses,
and while you should have at least five votes, you have
to give one “Yes” or “No.” Isay that the proposal is
monstrous.
So far from declaring the proposal “monstrous,”
the Age recently declared :
They (the four clauses) hinge upon one another, and to
a large extent are interdependent. To put thi ui in the
shape of four separate Referenda (for the purpose of
securing a “Yes” or “No” on each different question)
would complicate and embarrass what is in itself a simple
proposition.
It is most disconcerting to find the leaders of a
campaign quarrelling like this.
SCREAMS FROM CROW'S NEST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He foresaw that if the referendum was carried, and
the Labor party dominated ail the legislation of Australia
through a central Parliament, they would be as close up
to a rebellion as it was possible for any people to be. It
all pointed to revolution.— Solemn Lee at Crow’s Nest
(N.S.W.).
When they found Mr. Bruce Smith exhibiting a con-
dition almost of hysteria and talking about “political
buccaneers,” and Mr. Lee indulging in an excited fashion
in prognostications about “ revolutions’’—when they
found conditions such as that agitating all these ordi-
narily placid gentlemen, they could reckon something
was afoot in which they had an interest. (Laughter and
applause.)— J. C. Watson.
“ Caw ! Caw 1 ” said the elderly crow. “ Caw ! Caw ! ”
His manner was wild and his voice was raw.
“ I never have seen
The country so green !
Fanners no more on overdrafts lean !
Not a trace of drought,
And nowhere about
Is anything wanting its eyes picked out!
The country is prosperous, there’s no doubt!
From what 1 can see
There’s nothing for me ! ” . . .
“ I stnell revolution ! ” croaked Old Crow Lee.
“Caw! Caw!” screamed the solemn old crow, “Caw!
Caw !
There’s death for certain in Labor-made law !
Each orderly crow
Will very soon know
When up in blazes his tail feathers go !
Don’t linger in doubt,
For murder will out!
My nose tells me there is powder about!
There’s trouble in store for each crow who’s stout!
Our nests they will glow
And our blood will flow !
Labor is loose! ” yelled the terrified crow.
“Caw! Caw!” yelled the maddened old bird, “Caw!
Caw ! ”
Red visions of blood in his rage he saw.
“ Friends ! Listen to me •
Give ear to Crow Lee !
They’re going to rummage in ev’ry tree ?
Each jay and jackdaw,
His brood and his squaw,
Will all be subject to Federal law !
Look well, my friends, to your sticks and your straw !
I fear for the worst!
Our race is accurst!
I feel that something is ready to hurst! ”
“ Caw ! Caw ! ” yelled the crow on the tall, dead tree.
“ Observe us waiting for trouble, we three !
Smith, Deakin and I,
We flap and we cry,
And yell for our lives to the empty sky !
There’s rain in the east,
And every beast
Has acres of grass—but we want a feast!
But nobody seems to care in the least.
Don’t stir up the foes !
Why, afiy fool knows
That fighting and gibbets are bad for crows ! ”
“ Law ! Law ! ” croaked the trio of crows, “ Law ! Law !
The rats of the Caucus at red-tape gnaw !
Oh, let us be bound
By those in the ground !
Constitution-makers lie all around !
With reverent ‘ Caws ’
We honor their laws
And cling to our perch with determined claws !
If they cannot rule us—then blood and wars !
/teed the dead , who know,
Or the blood will flow,
Is the final word of the watchful crow ! ”
N.S.W.
E. FRANCIS.
BALLADE OF LOST SHACKS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Sydney’s State-run flats destroy the family life and
strain the marriage tie. The inmates, after having given
them a fair trial, are so disgusted with the result that
they openly express sorrow for the loss of the once so
bitterly contemned and abused private landlord of the
old days.— Sydney Evening News (15/2/’11) :
They long—the common people—for
The hovels which they used to know ;
They languish for those dear old homes,
Whose roofs and walls were burst and low.
From out their Socialistic flats
They gaze, with sorrow in their eyes,
And brood upon those lost delights—
The Shacks of Private Enterprise.
The dwelling which the State provides
Discourages the typhoid germ ;
Consumption may not flourish there,
Nor filth nor gloom for any term
However short; which causes grief,
And rage, and scorn, and shocked surprise,
To folks who’ve lived with Death, among
The Shacks of Private Enterprise.
The State flat lacks the stuffy yard
Abutting on the ruined lane,
The long-dead hound whose corpse obstructs
The vast and clamant open drain ;
Likewise the garbage he p wherein
A wealth of nameless Horror lies —
All of which luxuries pervade
The Shacks of Private Enterprise.
Envoi.
The Socialistic Tiger tears
At Ferry Co. and Sugar Trust;
Beneath his devastating claws
The Marriage Tie is rudely bust.
The landlord grabs his capital
And flees abroad with plaintive cries ,
The worker stays, and hungers for
The Shacks of Private Enterprise.
K.S.W.

QUALITY—BUCHANAN WHISKY
“BLACK & WHITE.”
A
WOMAN’S
LETTER
Stdnev, March 6, 1911.
Mr Dear Arini,—
So at last Lord Dudley really is retiring. For
about two years I have been regularly hearing, on
“absolutely unimpeachable authority,” that he
meant to return to England within a month.
When the grotesque libels in regard to him were
being circulated most busily, “well-informed”
people dashed about saying that he meant to flee
before the impending storm “this very week.”
I fancy they cherished a mental picture of
the G.-G. departing disguised as a coal-
heaver or something, on a tramp steamer.
A humorous feature in connection with the
official announcement of his departure is the
excited rush on the part of some of the news-
papers to mention that they foretold it all along.
So might Wragge take credit to himself when the
next drought arrives. There are prophecies which
simply must come off if one waits long enough.
The new man. Lord Denman, is a curious
appointment. Till a few years ago he was better
known as an accomplished steeplechase rider than
anything else. lam told by one who has met him
that he looks like a hoy of 25, and is an excellent
public speaker. He married the only daughter
of a millionaire.
The Joseph Wards, of M.L., and their daughter
leave Wellington to-day, and if the vessel makes
a good run they ought to be here next Friday
forenoon. If they don’t, the Women’s Patriotic
club will be what the London policeman said he
wasn’t when the Suffragette slapped his face —
perturbed. _ The ladies of Bull’s Chambers have
rushed out invitations to an afternoon At Home to
the two ladies Ward, the invitees having pro-
mised their presence by cable.
On the following Saturday afternoon the Presi-
dent of the M.L. Association, Mr. E. Owen Cox,
will be “At Home” at the A.B.C. Rooms, in
Pitt-street, to meet the M.L. knight and his con-
sort. These joy-bones (no, not jaw-bones) thrown
to Society during Lent are specially acceptable
because of the season. Society rises from its
ashes with alacrity to seize and gnaw them.
But the dinner to be given to the M.L. Premier
on Saturday night will bo the Feast Beautiful.
Tickets, 255. each ; Paris House catering ; diners
to choose their own wines: the banqueting hall
(St. James’s) to Tie transformed into a Maori
kianga, or village, with deer’s head, native mats,
and carvings from M.L. Tourist Bureau, and the
motto, in Maori, “He is the great link that
unites us,” glaring from an arch in front of the
platform.
“Sunda)',” remarks Montgomery, of the
Bureau, “the Prime Minister desires to observe
as a day of rest.” A very natural desire after
such a crowded Saturday. On Monday the visi-
tors depart Coronationwards.
Drift-words of the Hordern-Bingham wedding,
blown hitherward, tell mainly of the elegance and
distinction of Mrs. Hordern, the bridegroom’s
mother, as she appeared in a gathering where
good looks were nearly as numerous as the
wedding presents. Her gown “developed” (a
really good word that, and much in use in dress-
making houses of quality) in Sydney, was a cream
chiffon over pale mauve satin,"with special extras
in the way of silver embroidery. A loose coat of
the chiffon shpwed the lines of the wearer’s tall,
slender figure, and was trimmed with point de
gaze. Satin and feathers are mentioned in con-
nection with her hat, and diamonds and pearls
increased her face-value.
Thq importance of being Mary (or any of its
variants) is not appreciated by the Maries of this
State, It was a thin congress of mostly-thin
Maries that answered the Mayoress's call to the
Town Hall in connection with the Queen
Mary Coronation Gift Fund last week. City
organist Truman was pealing out the first
item ; of the usual Wednesday .afternoon’s
programme grave allegro moderate when
some 25 Maries (and variants thereof), look-
ing like delegates to a pessimists’ conference,
filed gravely—allegro moderate—into the Mayor-
ess’s rooms, sat down dispiritedly, and gazed
blankly at Mayoress Allen Taylor, Mrs. Severn
and civic aide-de-camp Layton. Mrs. Taylor and
Mr. Layton were the only persons in the room
who were not named Mary, Maria, Molly or May
(save, of course, a few press persons, who are
really not persons at all, only Intelligences).
Where was General Gordon? As the principal
Maria in this State, he should have been present.
General John Maria Gordon’s loyalty must have
white ants. Perhaps the Army is Unsound !
There is no ; enthusiasm about the Mari-gold
job. The N.S.W. Maries think their gold and
coppers might be better disbursed than in gifting
a lady who already has everything under Heaven
that money can buy. A souvenir from the Maries
of Queen Mary’s own circle of friends is a just and
reasonable suggestion. Lady Mary Trefusis/who
started the Mary mania, ought to be told to go
and get occupation.
Mrs. Taylor addressed the dismal company,
the third button of whose collective blouse was
divorced from its legal hole, and whose general
hat looked like an AH Night Vigil in the rain.
Mr. Layton then made a hurried reconnaissance,
found Miss Mary Sutter, and made her hon.
treasurer of the fund. Mrs. Severn, as already
mentioned, is hon. sec.
Concerning the foregoing: I cut the following
guff from a London daily :
“ Will you please accept my three stamps for our dear
Queen gift, as I have always taken a keen interest in all
her doings? She was married the same time as myself,
and all her children came the same time as mv own,’only
] have two girls and four hoys. Hoping Queen Mary will
have a long life and happiness, I remain, Maria .”
This letter, in simple spelling and handwriting, from a
woman in Warwickshire, was received by a member of
the executive committee which is arranging the Corona-
tion gift to the Queen from the women and girls of the
Empire named Mary, May, Marion, Marian, Maria, and
Marie. The contributions are coming in at a rate which
bids fair to establish the success of the pretty idea.
Isn’t the expression “simple spelling” the quin-
tessence of charity ?
The two Irish delegates who are due in .Tune,
should cause a flutter among the marriageable
maidens of the Commonwealth. Mr. Hazeldon
has been described by one who should know as
“the best-dressed man in the House of Com-
mons.” His photograph discloses a romantic-
looking youth with straight features and the
crisp, wavy hair beloved of romanticists like Vic-
toria Cross and Marie Corelli. He is a University
graduate, and the richest man in the Irish party.
Mr. Redmond, his colleague, is a son of John
Redmond, and is M.P. for North Tyrone. His
mother was one of the Daltons, of Orange.
When the made-in-Germuny bigamist comes
out of Wormwood Scrubbs, after doing his 10
years, he will be rather out of date for further
conquests. That is one comfort. His first mar-
riage was contracted 21 years ago; so he must
liave been old enough to be the father of the
youthful Ella Caspers, who was his last victim.
Speaking in the light of belated wisdom, musical
Sydney is rating the brother of the contralto for
not making inquiries about the creature who
called himself Ogilvie. But the young man only
reached London shortly before the wedding at
which he gave his sister away; and for that
matter how rarely do parents or guardians play
the sleuth-hound when an apparently desirable
suitor is in the leading r6le ! Men who possess the
bigamist habit usually have good, plausible man-
ners, and practice is supposed to make perfect.

Nothing remains of the £3OO subscribed by the
Sydney publ c for Miss Caspers. Some months
ago the co-trustees of the fund, Dr. Stewart
Mackay and Mr. Southwell, hearing that her
engagement was “off,” and that she was in want
of money, cabled her, in two instalments, all that
remained of the money.
Miss Doreen Milner, whose marriage to Lord
Linlithgow, son of our first Gov.-Gen., occurs
about Easter time, has a stature that lifts itself
almost into the blue ether (whatever that may be).
She is a vertical six-feet-two. Much too tall for a
woman. Not once in a mandarin moon will you
see grace and over five-feet-seven go together.
However, Australians aren’t going to for-
bid the Hope-Milner banns because the
bride-elect has seven inches up above other
tall women. By the way, Miss Milner is a
first cousin of the Mrs. (Major) Sitwell, who was
in this State with her rather sick husband some
j-ears ago. Lord L. and his bride will come to
Australia soon after their marriage, so a London
paper says.
The W.S.P.U. will have hatpins ready for Miss
Muriel Matters when she gets back to London.
She has got engaged, so Adelaide asserts, panting
as it speaks with excitement, to one of the Pro-
fessors at the Conservatorium. During her stay
in Adelaide the good-looking Suffragette seemed
to adore and be adored, and He made the musical
setting to the recitation she gave.
Mrs. Dora B. Montefiore is back in Sydney
after some years’ sojourn in England. She is the
lady who, with the help of her maids, barricaded
her house near London when the income-tax
collector called, as a protest against the il-
liberalism of the Government in not granting
women the suffrage. Mrs. Montefiore is not
in political line with Mesdames Pankhurst, Law-
rence and the rest of the suffragettes who want
the franchise for women householders only—on
the same lines men have it. She asks for adult
suffrage, and was hon. sec. of the Adult Suffrage
Society. Her pamphlet on “The Position of
Women in the Socialist Movement” is weighted
with wisdom and rebellion.
Miss Alice Ireland, of Drummoyne (Sydney), was
safely bestowed upon Gordon Inglis, formerly of
the Sydney D.T. literary staff, but now of George
High Commissioner’s staff, at Mayfair (London),
recently. There was quite a gathering of Sydney
University men to give them.a good start down
the road that, as Stevenson said, “lies long and
dusty to the grave.” O. A. Ireland, who gave
his sister away, was one; G. V. Portus, a
Rhodes scholar, was another ; R. S. Waddy, also a
Rhodes scholar, was another ; and C. E. W. Bean,
the S. M. Herald’s young man in London, was
another. H. P. Cronshavv, the managing reverend,
was at Oxford with C. G. Wade and Primate
Wright, or they were at Oxford with him—l don’t
care which as long as I don’t have to bear part of
the recriminations —and the spaces were filled in
with Australians abroad, rice, Londoners at
home, confetti, Irish lace, breakfast at the Cri-
terion and more good wishes than could be packed
into the departing motor-car.
Another cherubic trousseau is on the mind of
the designers, and under the needles of the
workers. Miss Wall, of Randwick, will be the
wearer, and Dr. Dick, of the same suburb, is the
winner—of the bride-elect, of course.
I see the S. M. Herald has been bewailing the
plight of our Art Gallery, and, like the thor-
oughly consistent anti-Socialist sheet it is, has
been calling on the Government to “do some-
thing” in the matter. What can the Government
do except abolish the trustees, of whom, by the
way, the Herald’s “Mr. .Tames” is one? The
two pictures which he bought in Spain the other
day deserve to be led .briskly, to the auto-da-fe,
and there dealt with according to the Inquisition
methods of their country of origin. Of course,
the N.S.W. Gallery hasn’t the money to spend
that the Melbourne and Adelaide institutions
have. We possess no Felton or Elder Bequests,
for the reason that the wealthy N. S. Welshman
has not yet been born who considered his cash
well bequeathed for Art. But more than
enough subsidy money has been squandered in quite
recent years, on arrant trash, to buy many of
the pictures the Herald clamors for. The trouble
is our present trustees don’t understand that it is
desirable in the public interest that the Gallery
should have such pictures ; nor would some of
them appreciate the value of the works if they
fell over them in broad daylight.
Now is the time when the indent merchant
whom one encounters on the ferry-boat, and the
Gentleman One who pervades the matutinal
tram, talk heatedly about the curse of Labor
domination, and glancing, loweringly, from their
paper, cry that all who are not innately evil or
mad must vote “No” to the Referenda ques-
tions. Time and again have I essayed to dis-
cover from various prosperous males of my
acquaintance the inner meaning of the trouble. In
vain. Their demeanor under cross-examination
becomes absolutely shifty. Almost I have been
inclined to suspect that, to them, the whole
Referenda problem is an insoluble mystery. Any-
how, I beg to submit the following short cate-
chism, failure to satisfactorily answer which
should be held to disqualify daily-paper students
from dogmatising till after April 26 next: —
(1) What is the Excise Act (1906)?
(2) Give a brief summary of (a)C.J. Griffith’s decision in
regard to the New Protection scheme, and (6) of the dis-
sentient judgment of Isaacs (J.) on the same matter.
(3) State shortly, giving reasons, the time, if any, that
will elapse between an affirmative vote on the Referenda,
and an outbreak of revolution and bloodshed in N.S.W.,
as predicted by Mr. Bruce Smith M.P., Mr. bee anti
others.
(4) What is the meaning of a “ Common Rule for Aus-
tralia”? Do you disapprove of it? If so, why?
(5) Analyse the Trade and Commerce Clause. Mention
a few of its possibilities, (a) assuming that Federal
legislators all go raving mad ; ( b ) that they remain sane.
(6) Define “Section 65,” “robbery,” “tacking on,”
“spoliation bv the Commonwealth,” “Revolution,” and
“ right-thinking citizen.”
Ip my opinion, the harassed wife who dashes the
above at her aggressively “Liberal ” spouse, sud-
denly, and before he has had eight or nine hours
to get the questions up, will have him so
thoroughly discredited and cowed that he will be
a; bearable companion for years.
i The other day I picked up a newspaper and
noticed that “ Mr. Rupert Oarington ” aspired
to be a director of the Gas Company. Just that
ahd nothing more ; and yet it amounted to a whole
sfermon on the local investor’s blank indifference
to titles, family connections and such like things
which weigh heavily elsewhere. The aspirant is,
presumably, the old gentleman who married a
daughter of Mr. Horsfall, of Widgiewa, years ago,
aind is now settled in Sydney. He is a brother of
tjie festive Carrington who, in his hot youth,
governed N.S.W. for a space. He is colonel of
aj local volunteer regiment, and owns a pair of the
Ipngthiest and yellowest moustachios in the Com-
monwealth. Also, he went to the war and got a
H. 5.0., and was named Smith till 1880, when lie
qbtained a Royal license to call himself Caring-
tpn. All these things would be eagerly mentioned
liy one seeking a directorship in England ; but in
Australia they cut no ice.

i Life is brighter by a charm since the kodak
ctame into the life of man and woman. There is a
kodak exhibition raging at Baker and Rouse’s, in
George-street. Providence in His mercy sent it
ho make a luminous hole in the greyness of Lent.
There are 248 specimens of His mercy. The first
is from the kodak of her Majesty the Queen
ilother, and the last from the snapper of Living-.
ston Hopkins. Between the Queen Mother and
“Hop’’there are a host of well-known and un-
known humanity ; but their work is awfully good.
Picked specimens all.
Who are the “two Sydney society belles” ad-
vertised by the Standard Theatre management as
making “ their first appearance on the stage in an
unclothed shadow dance” ? Also, is this another
result of the surf-bathing craze? There seems a
certain significance about the fact that the play-
house where this turn occurs notifies itself as
“ next to the Fire Brigade Station.”
Mrs. Short, the daughter of Mr. Griffin, a
Haberfield resident, who returns with her hus-
band to their adopted home in Alaska by the
Makura, bears the uncommon baptismal names of
Australia Zealandia Americania. Her father
named her first after the land he came from,
secondly for the land of her birth, and thirdly for
the continent to which he intended to migrate.
The girl has herself carried out her geographically
minded father’s intention. Burdened as she is
with the names of two continents and an 800-
miles-long island, heredity should count, and
there will surely be a little Alaska and a small
Klondyke in her family.
The Smiths are always doing something. There
have been so many of them since the time of their
common ancestor, Tubal Cain, that there must Vie
a good poll of striking Smiths. One Mr. Smith,
a tenor, lias carried off a singing scholarship at
the Adelaide Conservatorium. A Miss O’Dea, a
mezzo-soprano, annexed the other scholarship.
There were 28 competitors for the two bay-leaves,
but a third, an extra, was awarded to Miss Prim-
rose James. The three birds come out of the
aviary of Prof. Winsloe Hall. You may remem-
ber his wife, Georgina Delmar, singing hero in
opera. If you don’t recall her voice, you must
remember her eyes and her opals, or were they
turquoises?
AMacquarie-streetsurgeon was seen a few weeks
ago to dash forth from his house, holding liis tele-
plione account in one hand and the ’phone itself,
torn from the wall, intheother. Hehurled the Ar-
e into the gutter, muttered an incanta-
tion, put the bill in his pocket and re-housed
himself, looking like a man who had crowned a
day with a deed.

Press, is going hence to be “amongst those we
noticed ” at the Coronation. His friends are
making him a presentation of something yellow
and sterling.

little while. Mr. Talbot—you remember, tall hut
honest (he is 7ft. upwards), who was Calves pilot
star? He is getting up something to commemor-
ate the visit; but for the life of me I cannot re-
member what form the function is to take. Per-
haps the Destroyers are to be “ at home ” in the
water. Time will tell.

THANKS FOR PUBLISHING HAIR
FORMULA.
Remarkable Results Described by Reader.
Dear Editor, —I want to thank you for the
remarkable results I have secured by using the
hair formula which appeared in a recent issue of
your valued paper. As directed, I went to my
chemist and had him put up 3oz. Bay Rum, loz.
Lavona de Composee, and dram Menthol Crys-
tals. He told me- that this preparation was un-
equalled for hair and scalp troubles, but, I did. not
look for the astonishing- benefits which followed.
For a long time I had been troubled with dandruff
and falling hair, and my hair had become so thin
and lifeless that I feared I would become com-
pletely bald. I applied the tonic twice a day,
rubbingdt ipto the-scalp with the finger tips, and
yont can imagine my delight when at the end of the
third! day II found that the dandruff and scalp
itching had completely disappeared ; within two
weeks the falling out had entirely ceased, and now
at- the end of eight weeks the thin places are
covered with a thick growth of new hair nearly
six inches long. For the benefit of others who
suffer as I once suffered I would suggest that you
publish the formula again.
The preparation was also used by my husband,
who was not only delighted with its refreshing and
invigorating effect on the scalp, but says “it is the
best hair tonic he has ever used.”
Gratefully yours,
TINA H. PRATT.
NOTE. —The formula mentioned in the above
letter was published some time ago. Readers are
cautioned to avoid applying where hair is not
desired*

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, March 6, 1911.
My Dear Yarrie,—
The opening of the Autumn meeting at Flem-
ington on Saturday was a success from the social
point of view ; but the business-like racegoers,
who don’t care a dump for fashionable clothes or
the beings inside them, had a dismal time, for the
favorites were laid low with ghastly regularity.
“Back royalty” would have been a profitable
motto, had the army of baffled punters only known
it. Queen o’ Scots made the big win of the day ;
Royal Fleet was the only favorite to get safely
home ; and Comedy King, after a hard gallop,
beat Trafalgar by a length.
Afterarather soggy summering, Fashion crisped
up at the Newmarket. The vice-regal box was
packed with interesting clothing. Both G.-G.
Dudley and Gov. Carmichael were in grey sac
suits, and, like the aides, wore white gardenias in
their buttonholes. The Countess wore butter-
colored lace bows in a deep cream hat. The
Duchess of Bedford wore some pearls and her own
wrinkles. It is quite a pleasure to see an old lady
wearing her wrinkles in these days of massage and
beauty parlors. Her hat was English—and
dowdy. The terms are synonymous when ap-
plied to hats. The English representatives always
brush up in the matter of headgear after a vice-
regal term in Australia ; it seems to do them
nearly as much good as a visit to Paris. The
Carmichael lady reared an excellently groomed
and hatted head from the depths of the box.
Mrs. Tony Hordern, whose wedding was the
event of last week, wore her bridesmaids’ white
and Royal blue in the box, and showed a dazzling
complexion. Festive sky-blue plumes waved
from another exquisite coiffure in the plush
seats. Beatrice Madden wore roses in her hat.
Lord Dick Nevill shone for one of his last few
appearances before his departure. He will be
missed from our little social round. The pair of
Governors went away before the last race, and
left the women folk to gossip alone. Splendid
creatures in livery dashed about with suit cases
and the leather cages of the stove-pipe hats, and a
little later the great men disappeared into their
motors in Inverness capes.
The Gaud Mayor and his Mayoress, who may
soon be Lady Davey, promenaded the lawn. . She
wore a coat of chain-mail woven from cut jet, and
black plumes waved bravely from her helmet.
Mrs. Jim Purves, in mourning black, drifted out
for her first race meeting. She appeared to be
very proud of her newly-admitted barrister hus-
band. Doctor Stawell accompanied a lady who
was hidden completely beneath a monoplane hat
with propellers of mole-colored choux.
Some anxious fashion-platers wore the new
Laplander hat, which concoction was invented
in honor of either Peary or Cook (I forget which).
It has a tight band of suede round the head,
smothering ears and hair and almost quenching
the eyes; from this band a quantity of silk flops
back in the lop-sided craze of the moment like a
very drunk Tam-o’-Shanter. Those girls must
have been almost stifled, for the spectators in the
stand, though garbed mostly in light frocks, were
briskly fanning themselves all the afternoon.
A smart little tea party happened after the
Newmarket at the Grand Hotel. Mrs. Geoffrey
Syme was the hostess; she is a small brunette,
and her frock was the color of iced rock-
melon served with sherry. When she appeared
on the lawn she unfurled a parasol that was
like the palanquin roof of an Oriental princess.
The new monster upside-down handle rose from
its middle, and it drooped into yards of white silk
fringe from blue and white silk embroideries.
One other purple awning of much fringe and
great acreage paled into insignificance before it.
Mrs. Syme’s sister, Miss Greta Garnett, who
helped at the little party, wore a Marcos Stone
dress covered with pale pink bows.
The Women’s Hospital is spreading its new
wing for the further shelter of the sick. Mrs. ex-
Mayoress Burston laid the foundation-stone. She
was one of the chief workers fov the bazaar that
dug up the funds last year. The addition is to
consist of a new infirmary and a septic ward. So
many people were interested in the ceremony that
it was a crowded function. A drove of kind-
hearted and feather-hatted dames crushed their
fine dresses as recklessly as. if it were a
Cup meeting. But, in spite of all their
crowding, the speeches from the mere men
orators could not be heard ;. the fine rounded
periods drifted overhead, and dropped in ir-
ritating scraps on to the close-packed listeners.
Archbishop Clarke, during his turn, spoke of the
children having the advantage of the institution’s
care for the first two years of their lives. “Non-
sense!” exclaimed an ex-committee woman.
“ Two years! Why, we couldi not afford to keep
them two months! Too many other mothers
coming along and demanding oar care. Twelve
days is the average time we> keep, the babies, if
the poor little mites are in good, howling health.”
Premier Murray and Gaud Mayor Davey also
lauded the work done by this hospital. In fact,
it has so much, to doi that, in. spite of the thou-
sands that women-workers have-collected for it, it
started the year £2ooo' in. debt.
The last will and testament of the late Malcolm
McEaeharn was dragged into an English law
court lately on a question of “ interpretation.”
Poor, sick Malcolm was evidently much troubled
about the last will which he signed seven months
before his death. He hitched two codicils to it on
the day of signature, and two more codicils after-
wards. Daughter Mary’s legacy was originally
weighted with certain conditions re her matri-
monial hopes; but these were amended on a codicil.
The question of interpretation turned on a matter
of blanks. These were left for the testator to fill
in; but he ignored one of them—by design or
accident—and the unfilled blank was of vast im-
portance to the widow. As the will stands, she
inherits the whole of her husband’s Australian
estate, amounting to about £250,000, as well as a
part of the English estate, which is equally corpu-
lent. The trustees suggested to the Court that
Malcolm meant the two estates to be “ pooled,”
more or less. The learned Judge, however,
decided there was no evidence before the Court to
indicate that the dying man had not intended his
wife to be an enormously wealthy relict. Mrs.
McEaeharn, as all Australia knows, was an heiress
‘‘on her own”—one of the daughters of J. B.
Watson, the Bendigo millionaire. Another
daughter was widowed the other day, when
cricketer “Billy ” Murdoch lost his wicket.
The Alfred Hospital Fair is almost ready for
launching. A bridge party in aid of it happens
at Cliveden on March 14. The tickets, which are
a humble half-crown apiece, are obtainable from
the Mayoress or at Allan’s. Charitably-inclined
folk will pay in their winnings, if they happen to
accumulate any. Work competitions are raging,
and the products will be sold at the Fair.
From an ex-Alfred nurse :
Oh yes ! we are going to have a horse-raffle too for the
Alfred bazaar. A well-known Melbourne undertaker has
presented the P’ran stall with a pony. I don’t know his
idea ; perhaps he thinks one good turn deserves another.
March 1 was St. David’s Day; so the Welsh
section chartered a steamer and went down the
bay with the Prince of Wales feathers fluttering
in the salt breeze. The jolly voyagers sang all
the way there and back ; also the Welsh language
raged fore and aft. This year, the proceedings
were extra-joyous on account of the new Prince,
whose investiture is to be a feature of the forth-
coming Coronation whirl. His health was drunk
many times with strange and baffling toasts. The
Russian language is considered one of the most an-
gular, and therefore deadly for purposes of assault;
but consider the barbed-wire entanglement of
syllables which surrounds the rampant gryphon
crest: “ Y Ddraic coch a ddyry cyehwyn.”
The Beauty Competition which has been hap-
pening on the screen at West’s Pictures these last
two weeks was, of course, preceded by a private
gathering of the rival goddesses. The originals
of about 20 selected photos, were summoned to
West’s Building on a Friday afternoon, in order
that Mr. Lyall, the operator, might run the
camera over them. It was as nearly an informal
gathering as could be, and with one or two excep-
tions, the girls (who ranged from very young to
decidedly older) soon forgot to feel nervous.
“Hats off” was an absolute command; but
“hair down” was optional with the sitters. One
by one they posed on a revolving pedestal, and
the photographer “took” them from back to
front, whilst they simpered gently over a bunch
of flowers and toyed with a fan. They mostly got
through the ordeal without a tremble. One jaunty
spinster, who had evidently been filmed before,
scored a point by throwing flowers and kisses to
the audience she was picturing in her mind ; but
one of the prettiest candidates made the foolish
mistake.of peering languorously into space, under
the impression that she was doing deadly work
with her eyes. The voting at the end of last week
was in favor of No. 24 for first prize. No. 24 is
said to be a Williamsonian stage girl, handsome
in fact, and picturesque in her method of posing
for the camera. No. 12, the second in the running,
is also an advanced student in the art of looking
pretty.
The new military regulation relating to the
retirement of senior officers is causing a stir. The
way that ages are mentioned in the morning paper
is enough to give the hardiest old campaigners
twinge. Some of our defenders must know now
how a woman feels when she is presented with a
census paper and told to put down in black and
white how long she has been on earth. In 1921,
quite.’ a lot of people will escape the ordeal by
taking to the air for a day or two. Colonel
Ballenger, whatever his years, is pretty nearly as
youthful as his two little daughters who have just
reached the hair-up stage. They are good horse-
women, ride astride, and are their father’s most
attached A.D.C.’s.
Miss Essie Perrin, who is in England, and Mr.
Fred. Kcrouse, who is in Melbourne, are to be
married soon. As there is a long, weary journey
between them, they intend to divide the distance
and get the knot tied in Ceylon. The prospective
bridegroom will go to the Island of Tea and wait
for his dainty bride. Miss Perrin will stay with
tea-planter Gordon and his wife. Mr. Gordon
will act the proud and heavy part of father, and
give the guest away. Afterwards Mrs. Essie-
Perrin-Kcrouse will become a fair Australian and
settle down in Melbourne. The bride-to-be has
just recovered from an attack of diphtheria.
Fred. Kcrouse haunted the cable office during the
anxious time, sending and receiving.answering
budgets about his lady’s progress.
Dr. George Cuscaden’s doctor son, W. J., has
asked Miss Beryl Eadie if she will; and the lady
will. She is a daughter of Dr. James Eadie, now
of Balaclava. Another medical engagement is
that of Dr. Shorney, of Adelaide, with Miss
Millicent Berry, daughter of Henry Berry, of
Kew.
Miss Charlotte Campbell Langtree, a twig of
the Langtrees of Toorak, is on the verge of matri-
mony. Her buttered toast in the days to come
will be-passed by a Major Milner, of the Royal
Irish Regiment,, who has relatives in “Who’s
Who.”
A grey-headed romance will shortly be sealed
at a suburban altar. The lady is Miss Eva Mary
Dent, daughter of Captain Dent, of this city,
and the man is Mr. J. M. Rylah. For many
years he conducted the higher arithmetic in the
counting-house of the Sargood people, and is both
a widower and a grandpapa ; but out of his books
he dug somewhere the secret of perpetual
juvenility. In manner and gait he is younger
than most men of 35, and his smile would make
a “flapper” even of a woman of 35.
Ex-newspaper man Beaumont Smith, nowadays
secretary to William Anderson, is to be married
in a couple of days. He is engaging to buy frocks
next summer for Miss Elsie Fleming, a tall, graceful
girl of almost Titian coloring. After the wedding-
the pair voyage to Europe.
Two items of gossip-interest re the Horderri-
Bingliam wedding. The fair bride, young as she-
is, has a year or two of advantage over the youth-
ful groom. And it is remarked with awe that the-
Sydney guests for the wedding were conveyed’ to
and from Melbourne in a special train.
Aviator Hammond during the week gave two
public shows of flying at Altona. Boats and
trains were “ laid on”; but the exhibitions hap-
pened at 5.30 in the mornings, which meant a
sweetly early start. The early birds who caught
the fly rave about the “opal tints of the
sunrise.” Hammond has done a good turn
for Nature by inducing these people to
get up early for once and look aloft.
He has accomplished some wonderfully good
flights in the calm, early morning; but, so far,
he’s “ afraid to come home in the dark or the
strong daylight either. Before the sun gets to
business is his time for racing trains, skating
along jagged stone walls, and darting seaward.
There is a hope in the Hammond heart that the
gilded youth of the town are being bitten with
the air play. The youngest of the Baillieux—one
of the members of the clan left behind in the
general exodus toward Coronation —was the first
Melbourne man to spend a fifty-pound note on a
flight as a passenger; and Philip Schuler, son of
Editor Schuler of the Age, also took a trip.
Soprano Mary Conly farewelled in Melbourne
Town Hall on Thursday to such good effect that
she was forced to come back for an encore good-
bye on Saturday night. Freddie Beard backed
up the “Inflammatus” with his choir and the
organ, and played the ballad piano accompani-
ments for the brilliant songstress as cap-
ably as he had guided the pale blue and
pink choir girls in the more imposing
items. Mendelssohn’s “Hear ye, Israel,”
beautifully sung, was a gem set in a fine pro-
gramme. Baritone Frederic Earp sang a song
composed by Frederic Beard—a delicately roman-
tic effort called “ Love’s Reflection.” Du Boulay
and his violin were added at the Saturday con-
cert, and Kerry thundered at the organ.
Mrs. Edwin Millar, who is one of the Coronation
jaunters, was nearly kept at home by a bad motor
accident, in which she was considerably cut and
bruised. However, she managed to get away on
the Malwa, and hopes that the long voyage will do
the necessary nerve-soothing. The shaken lady
took her young niece, Belle White, along with her
to the city of crowds.
Wilfred Beaver, who is captaining one of the
little expeditions that are in search of the missing
Staniforth Smith, is a Melbourne man. He used
to live in Brighton and on the Block, but is now
a Resident Magistrate in the Western Division of
Papua. He has been helping to look after our
Possession for seven years. Beaver and his
fellow searcher Ryan have taken the launch
Taaneera, which they will leave at Goaribari, and
make a dash inland behind Sir Arthur Gordon
range, following the lost party’s tracks as far as
possible. They are going to have a hard time, for
they will go as far as they can make their rations
stretch.
Ernest Clarke, the “ Lone Fisherman,” was
back at Melbourne in time to jaunt down to
Flemington and see his horse, Eyeglass, win a
race on Saturday. The grave, good-looking
bachelor, the despair alike of maids and widows,
had been away in Maoriland on his annual trout-
fishing expedition. Ernest is the complete
angler—the good-natured man who, because he
“ wouldn’t hurt a fly,” takes a fiendish delight in
landing the speckled fish which never sees a fly
without wanting to consume it.
It was whispered broadly that a harem skirt,
with a pretty actress within its discreet folds, was
to walk down Collins-street, from one big chiffon
store to another, in the most crowded hour of
Saturday morning. Awful cables about; the mob-
bing of English and Continental fashion-pioneers
were evidently too much for the lady’s nerves, for
she did not materialise. Now, I guess, she must
be grieving in secret over the £5O bet that Mrs.
Gossip gravely' asserted she was to rake in if she
dared.to do it.
An astounding story comes by cable from
Russian Turkestan about the eating by wolves of
all but two of a wedding party numbering 118.
The unreliable submarine thing asserts that the
last to be killed were the bride and bridegroom,
and that they were flung to the pack by the des-
perate pair of survivors. This is the second time
I’ve heard of the wedding party being also the
wedding breakfast. The other furiction happened
in the South Seas. But in that ease the relatives
of the bride and bridegroom did the swallowing.
The swallowed were a guileless group of specially-
invited guests from another island.
From “Rosna” of Adelaide:—
Her Ex. opened another bazaar last week.
Gawler had a race-meeting the other Saturday, and
hordes of city folk were there. A £2O dividend right at
the tail-end of the proceedings was the most exciting
circumstance of the day.
The T. E. Barr Smiths leave for Britain at the end of
the month.
The Agricultural Show has come again, and the Duke
and Duchess of Hayseed, their suite and their motor-
cars are crowding us mere modest citizens out of house
and hostel into the park lands. Sundry attractions have
been provided for the visitors. One evening there was a
prize-fight, at which, by the way, a perfect lady was alleged
to have attended, masquerading as her brother man, A
pressman declares he suspected the lady, taxed her with
it, and the confiding soul fell on his heck and confessed.
There is also Bilanderson’s “ The Chance of a Life-time.”
By the way, our Mary Aimlessness is mostly due to the
Backblocks aristocrat, who is now too affluent to let his
•daughters go to service. Country towns are even wor?e
off in the Mary Ann respect than the city. One good-
sized northern hamlet hasn’t a maid from end to end,
and only one washer-lady for the whole population.
The new little, blue little King’s Theatre opened on
Tuesday eve, and the popular “ Dandies,” transplanted
from the Exhibition Gardens, are to make melody there
till further notice. Its blue is of a restful shade, and the
[ square gallery and dress-circle are so constructed that
from the side seats you have a beautiful view of the ceil-
ing and your neighbor’s hattery. The present show is a
:gay and lightsome one, and the opening night was spread
with Tolleys, Bevans, Gordons, Buchanans, Wendts, and
the great Others-too-numerous-to-mention family.
The Barbarossa denuded S.A. of some more population
last week, among the wanderers being Miss Hay, daughter
■of the late Major Hay. Her mother was a Stirling, sister
of S’Lancelot, and tile damsel goeth to Britain to live with
some of the maternal relatives.
The women of the non-party political association have
taken in haiid the question of a memorial to the late good
Catherine Helen Spence, and a committee of weight and
intellect will shortly show the world that the question is
not going to be shelved, like some other memorials we
are too polite to mention. Suggestions to date are : (1)
To promote effective voting ; (2) to purchase the ground
and building for a kindergarten at Bowden ; (3) portrait
for the Art Galleryand (4) building to be erected for
women for use for political purposes. If Miss Spence’s
wishes or tastes are to have any bearing on the scheme,
number 3 can be struck out at one blow, for she wAs
eminently not' the woman to want her picture in any old
.gallery. Probably the effective voting scheme would be
the truest compliment to her memory, but we should
need a second Catherine Helen Spence to carry it
•through. And we haven’t many of them.
Kubelik, who advised the'copper-haired youngster,
Daisy Kennedy, to take her fiddle to Europe, has been
handing more compliments and advice to her in Vienna.
She played to him for an hour, and he returned the com-
pliment for a similar period. He says she has mastered
the Seveik method already, and concert experience
is her greatest need. He has also promised to use
his mighty word with managers for a London appearance
for the damsel. Daisy and an elder sister “batch”
together in the Austrian capital. The rent of their
rooms includes hot rolls and coffee every morning. By
the way, every Viennese wants to learn English, and
there are plenty of pupils for the student who wishes to
help finance herself in that expensive city. One Austra-
lian girl had a fierce-moustached young doctor-pupil, who
insisted on learning “ I bleed, thou bleedest,” “ I shall
kill,” “ We will dissect ” !
Those people who did not care to grapple with the
original French may now read “ Vers la Lumifere,” the
novel about Us, published last year by our citizeness,
Mrs. Marie Lion. George Robertson has just issued a
translation under the title of “ The Black Fearl,” illus-
trated with some goodly photographs and reproductions
of paintings by the author’s charming sister, Mrs. Mou-
chette. It is an interesting story, and the existence of
certain live South Australians among the characters
makes it doubly entertaining to Adelaide.
The Commonwealth Club, always kind to hungry
visitors, last week fed Mr. G. H. Knibbs, who manages
the Commonwealth’s arithmetic. He has come here to
take our census.
Sincerely yours,
JOHANNA.

SHOTGUN CARTRIDGES
“NEW RIVAL”
It’s the way they shoot in a gun that marks the differ-
ence between “New Rival” shotgun cartridges and other
kinds. They always shoot, and shoot accurately and
uniformly. This is brought about by their method of
construction and loading, together with the extensive
experience of their manufacturers. The loading is all
done by automatic machinery, which exactly measures
the quantity of powder and shot, seats the wads
evenly and crimps the mouth of the cartridge firmly.
All guns shoot better with “ New Rival ” shotgun
cartridges. They are blue in color and have a corruga-
tion around the head to prevent its pulling off.

HEARTBURN AND INDIGESTION.
Heartburn is a . symptom of indigestion, and
consists of a most distressing and gnawing pain in
the stomach; often with sour, acid belchings,
nausea and fainting. When the food is only par-
tially digested, it gives rise to an accumulation of
wind or gas generated from the excess of acidity
from the imperfectly digested-food. To overcome
this complaint you should, eat sparingly of plain
digestible foods, and take a course of Dr. Morse’s
Indian Root Pills, which will strengthen the
digestive organs, and remove all acidity from the
stomach. These pills do not merely alleviate the
trouble, but get at the cause, and thus perma-
nently overcome the trouble.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
The final Test at Sydney was joyful in its weird
unexpectancy. On Friday the sticks were drawn with
Australia 281 for the loss of three wickets, and the crack
batsmen still in the pavilion. Saturday was bright; so
the cricket fiend rose early, cut his whiskers, sharpened
his pencil and “ records ” bump, and to the number of
24,321 rushed to Sydney Cricket Ground to revel in a
mammoth total. Never was greater promise of a bumper
score. But the bottom fell out, and instead of a bats-
men’s feast it was a bowlers’ banquet. The Australian
tally was 345 for five wickets when Schwarz seized the
leather. Then there was chaos. Trumper, Hill, Hans-
ford, Armstrong and Cotter fell before that wily bowler,
and the side ivas in a heap for 364—0n1y 19 runs after
Schwarz commenced his butchery. His figures oil the
day were five wickets for six runs, and on the innings six
for 47. Great was Schwarz !
But he must share his glory with wicketkeeper
Sherwell, who was cat-like in his agility. Schwarz
tempted Hill, Ransford and Cotter, and Sherwell did the
rest. No other visiting stumper has shown such excel-
lent work as Sherwell; up to the end of the first innings
of the last Test he had stumped nine and caught eight-
-17 of the SO Australian wickets which had fallen in the
Test campaign. Great is Sherwell!
Had the Africans been nearly as terrible with
the bat as with the ball, the game would have been won.
But they were not, and amidst the banter of an un-
generous crowd, the Springboks were butchered for 160.
The slayers were Armstrong (two for 17), Whitt.v (two for
33), and Hordern (four for 73). The day ended with
Africa hiding its face in a follow-on, with one wicket
down for 33 runs. But on Monday Africa was once more
serene. Zulch smashed the bowling for 150, and Faulkner
slashed it for 92, and altogether 368 were accumulated
for six wickets. It was a match of startling changes and
reversals of form that would drive a bookmaker crazy.
The end came on Tuesday, when Africa was dismissed for
401. In Australia’s second innings Macartney hit up a
brilliant 56, Bardsley went down before a straight one at
39, Ransford notched a duck ; and Trumper (74 not out)
and Kelleway (24 not out), with sundries (5), provided the
wherewithal for a seven wickets victory.
. * * ■* *
The previous week saw the Springboks put up a
stern fight against N.S.W. ; but just as Hannibal failed
to save Africa 2000 years ago, so Schwarz and Sherwell
failed to save it last week. N.S.W. scored 436 and 458—a
total of 894. Africa responded gamely, and smacked 408
and 442, and lost in an aggregate of 1744 runs by only 44.
Which was a mighty close thing.
What ruined Africa in that case was the sudden
awakening of Macartney. After scoring 15 or so in the
campaign, he suddenly burst, and, giving scarcely a
chance, smacked up 119 and 126. Now, how was Africa
or anyone else to know that Macartney would burst in
this amazing fashion? Even the most case-hardened
Mac-barracker was stricken dumb when his idol followed
up this exploit by making 137 in the final Test. It is
told that Macartney went to Alec Bannerman and got
himself polished up. Anyhow, the sudden bursting of
Macartney is the talk of the place.
The first All England cricket eleven had an ex-
perience which never came the way of another visiting
team—they were in a wreck. The trouble happened to
the City of Sydney, in almost the spot where the New
Guinea piled up the other day. All the cricketers
were got ashore safely.
* * * *
The benefit match to cricketer Warne, of
Carlton, Will commence on the Melbourne Cricket Ground
to-morrow (Friday), when Australia will meet the Rest.
* * * *
Up to the end of February J. W. McLaren,
the Fortitude Valley (Brisbane) fast bowler, had taken 50
wickets for 211 runs in first-grade cricket. And McLaren
doesn’t present sundries by the dpzen to the other side,
either.
• * * *
Cricket was mostly tedious in Melbourne last
Saturday. The bowler of the day was Maerow, of Rich-
mond, who took seven Northcotes for 32. No other
trundlers'were at all destructive, but some of the bats-
men were most offensive. In the moist heat McKenzie
made 192. for ’Roy against North, despite the fact that
the enemy tried nine different bowlers. Hartkopf, 166
not out for University again t Melbourne, carried, his score
to 183. Melbourne, in response, produced a person named
Johnstone, who whacked 111 not out. Warne and Hick,
of Carlton, showed a craze for scoring at Essendon’s ex-
pense —the former 95 and the latter 100 not out.
Much interest clung round the meeting be-
tween Healy and Longworth, at Sydney Domain
Baths on Saturday, in nhe 100yds. sprint. The memory
of the unexpected defeat of Healy by the youth from
Rose Bay was still fresh, and followers of the sport were
anxious to see how the risen and the rising stars would
compare in the shorter distance. They, apparently, had
made up their minds to save themselves for the final burst,
so the first 50 yards was not a cracker, and the lame ducks
in the race kept up easily enough. Not till 50 yards had
been covered did the stars really begin to,move, and then
there was a successionof spurts which brought them to
the 75yds., with Healy in front. With a magnificent
effort Longwortli ranged alongside Healy and let himself
go. He kicked up the water like a White Star liner, and
tore great holes in it with his arms. Healy was hard
pushed, but managed to win by a touch, and a miss was
as bad as a mile to Longwortli. There is no denying the
fact, though, that Healy is in the unpleasant position
of knowing that always alongside him is the
man who will, sooner or later, seize his king-
dom. Longworth is the rising star. It was a great
race at the finish, but the dilatory tactics of the first
half reduced its importance from a time point of view.
They took 60 seconds to cover the 100yds. Longworth said
afterwards that he is a long-distance man. But that is a
mistake. W.hen he has learned to use his great strength
in the short sprint he will be just as formidable over 100
as 880 yards..
As if to bear out his remark, the race for the
880yds. championship of the Ma State was a mere pro-
cession. Hardwick, the holder of the title, did not, of
course, defend it, being on the high seas. Longworth’s
only competitors were Cecil Healy and S. C. Wright, of
North Sydney. It wasn’t a race. Longworth led from
start to finish, and beat Healy by 50 yards. Healy passed
his licking on to Wright, whom he left. 40 yards behind.
Longworth’s time was lluiin. 31S-‘C., while Kieran’s
record is llmin. 2 l-ssec.
* * ■ * *
There is a Spring freshness about the sculling
world. Harry Pearce and Sydney Kemp will, it is.under-
stood, row for the championship of the world ; and if
Pearce can down Kemp, it is understood that "Richard will
be accommodated with his pet stake, £5OO, and requested
to show his title deeds to the championship. The idea of
a race between Pearce and Arnst is causing much talk,
and if it does come off, as everyone hopes it will, what a
day we shall have on the old Parramatta !
* * * *
However, Pearce is not the only Richmond in
the field. Among the swarm of athletes who will try
their luck in the Parramatta Hundred is one Thomas
Lawson, of Maoriland. He stands 6ft. 2in. in his socks,
weighs 13st. 31b., is apparently made of twisted steel and
toughened wire. He can walk, run, jump and fight with
the best of them, and he is in Sydney with a view to
clearing things up. If he sweeps the sculling board he
will attack Arnst. One way and another Richard has a
fairly strenuous time ahead of him.
■A * * *
The gentlemen who are running the Parramatta
Hundred are likely to find themselves swamped by suc-
cess. No less than 41 scullers are anxious to take part in
what is probably a record field, fleet, or flood, or what-
ever it may be. No less than five liandicappers are
struggling with the problem of arranging the allowances.
On the day of the final, March 26, there will be a display
of decorated motor-boats, and a race for the Ladies
Championship of Australia and a wager of £3O in skiffs.
The lady contestants will he the present holder, Miss
Gertie Lewis (married since she won the championship),
and Miss Kathleen Ryan, championess of the Northern
Rivers.
* * * *
The eight-oar championship of S. Aus. was a
horrible fiasco this year. The Port River was rough,
and naturally the Port Adelaide crew are better water-
men than those of the city, who practise ordinarily on
the shallow and sheltered Torrens Lake. At the end of a
mile, three of the four boats were full of water. Mer-
cantile sank, and the steamers “ stood by ” to help.
Adelaide and University got to shore, emptied their
boats, and made a good fight for second place. Mean-
while Port had covered the course alone.
* » * *
“ B.” : There’s no sensible reason why the
wrangle with “ Not-at-All ” should be prolonged. After
the third Test was gathered in by S’Africa with the help
of Sundries —a large pile of which was due to the no-ball
defaulting of Kelleway and Cotter—this humble person
suggested that “ the Australian selection committee has
the glorious option of smothering players who persist in
their efforts to present ‘the ashes’ to the other side.”
The Australian bowlers aforesaid “didn’t persist”; on
the other hand, they took the hint, showed that their
lapse was carelessness, and have been cricketers of high
standard ever since.
MACART NETS
LITTLE WAYS.
In the first three
Test, matches he made
15 runs. In his last
three innings he
made three centuries.
J. N. Crawford is getting restless already. He
announces that he will throw up his billet as a master at
St. Peter’s College, Adelaide, at the end of the present
term, and may probably go “on the land.” His breach
with Surrey county is understood, however, to be
quite healed now, so pretty well anything may happen.
Since coming to Australia Crawford has been simply a
bowler ; but on a recent Saturday he showed some of his
old-time batting, knocking up a chanceless 182 in two
hours on the Adelaide Oval against the bowling of Wright
and Travers.
* * * *
In the Melbourne Oofball’s discussion of the
proposal to abolish rule 29, which prohibits the payment
of players, a delegate beautifully put it that, under con-
ditions of “ non-payment,” “ the players are asking more
than the clubs could afford ; last year our men demanded
and got £447 out of a total revenue of £680.” Another
delegate suggests district football and: an amalgamation
with the League. His motion to that effect won’t come
on for a fortnight, by which time anything may have
happened. Whatever eventuates, Vic. football is a sort
of hashmagandy at present, and it might not be . a bad
idea to get the Noxious Trades Board to investigate the
subject.
* * * *
When the great Oofball question was under
official discussion in Melbourne, a representative of the
University F.O. wanted to know “whether we have to
deal with a game or a business?” Which was highly
characteristic of “gentleman amateurism.” Football,
of course, is a game, like cricket, hockey or lacrosse ; but
the fixing up of contests which draw hundreds of pounds
of o-ate-money is a business. Therefore the players are
engaged in assisting a business whether they get paid
for their services or give them gratis. Cricket is
none ■* the less a game because a number of the
players earn their living at it. Practically the pro-
fessionals are paid out of gate-money, for if
there were no gate there’d be ho fixed salaries for the
honest pros, or fees and “ expenses ” for the fraudulent
amateurs. Nor is football any the less a game because
professional players are now throwing aside all pretence
of secrecy in accepting payment. In the original and
true sense of the word, an amateur could play no part in
any game or sport which takes place within any sort of
enclosure where .money is charged for admission. The
Melbourne University fellows are obviously employed in
somebody’s business if they kick about in a gate-money
show, and the question as to whether they are playing
the game for love or money is beside the point.
* * * *
On Tuesday of last week the Vic. Oofball
League decided not to pay players openly. On Wednes-
day the Association agreed “to reimburse players for
their services.” The League has been boss body ; but
this action of the Association means that it must tumble
down in a hurry from its attitude of pretending to a
virtue which everybody knows it doesn’t possess, and
follow the Association’s suit; or else keep its secret pay-
ments up to a scale high enough to prevent the rival
body buying up its men. Many League delegates thought
that, with professionalism admitted, the good player
would open his mouth too wide. He does that now, and
every time he opens his mouth the League puts its oof
in i,; - „ „ ,
* * * *
Adelaide has had a fight for what called itself
the Lightweight Championship of Australia, and Hugh
Mehegan covered himself with honor in it, both as boxer
and as sportsman. It did not go the agreed 20 rounds.
After the second, Jim Armstrong showed that he was not
good enough. In the third, he was down for 8 seconds,
and then for nine ; but he battled along most gamely till
the ninth round, when he went down four times, and
finally stood up helpless. Here was Mehegan’s chance.
He took it ; but not in the usual way. He stood back
and refused to hit a man who was unable to hit back.
Then the gong went and saved Armstrong ; but Micko
Walsh, referee, stopped the fight and gave it to Mehegan,
with the loser gamely but ungratefully clamoring to be-
allowed to go on and get stoushed some more.

2.2oyds. run; “Dad” Wheatley (Vic.) for the half-mile
and the mile; and Harding (M.L.), Marden, Lynch
(N.S.W.), Larking (Victoria), and denies (Tasmania) will
nibble at the middle distances. Davis(Melb. ’Varsity), the
hurdler, is unable to leave the shop, as it would interfere
with his medico exams.; so Frank “ Moroomba” Brown
(Vic.), who was the Australasian champion hurdler a few
years back, has been selected. Harold Hardwick will try
for the 100yds. and mile swims, with Maorilander M. E.
Champion and J. Lord (N.S.W.) to assist,, Hardwick will
be in the heavy-weight boxing contest, too. E. W.
Schnieder, wrestler and bikist, is to represent this little
bit of the earth in the Hackenschmidt business. Wilding
(M.L.), Doust (N.S.W.), Heath and A. W. Dunlop (Vic.)
will be within coo-ee of the tennis courts. New South
Wales wants about £250 for its representatives’ exes., but

standing the damage of his own trip. “Bustle” is the
order of the day, and the hat is being taken round vigor-
ously. • *'
Tile Vic. Athletic Association, finding a few
coppers in its pocket at the end of its year, has dropped
them into the hat of the people who guaranteed the
awful Athletic Pageant of 12 months ago. The Ass.
isn’t legally liable; but it is going to keep on digging
until it has raised enough to pay off every penny. Ten
shillings in the £ has now been wiped out.
* * * *
Died last month, at Southampton, Charles
Barr, perhaps the greatest yacht-skipper the world has
known. He sailed the America Cup defender Columbia
when she heat, Shamrock in 1899 ; also, Shamrock 11. in
1901; and Reliance when she beat Shamrock 111. in 1903.
Deceased was only 46, and his trouble was heart disease.
*** * -
Young George Gray’s opponents in England
can put some pathos into their complaint that he com-
pels them to “ sit and get cold on it” whilst he is piling
up his losing hazard breaks. If Fred Lindrum grew
weary of watching whilst his young friend was scoring
400 or 500 in an innings, what about the woes of Diggle?
The Englishman has to sit and nurse his cue whilst Gray'
makes 1402 in 115 minutes t The monotony of the old
spot stroke was as nothing compared with this, for
the player was always on the move from side to side
of the table, and his scoring was much faster than
Gray’s. The Australian remains always behind the baulk
line, and is not required to shift his feet more than a few
inches. When the billiard world has satiated its wonder-
ment in watching the new specialist there is bound to be
a demand for a means of stopping the losing-hazard
monotony. Professional billiard playing must be attrac-
tive to live, and variety of strokes is its only abiding
attraction. But the rules of the game as regards hazard-
strike from the “ baulk crescent ” can’t be amended, with
fairness to Gray, until he has won the championship. If
Stevenson is well advised he will arrange a test match
with the Australian phenomenon as soon as possible
before the novelty has worn off the young-
ster’s monotonous game. A championship meeting
between the pair would draw the biggest “ gate ”
there ever was for a billiard show ; nor would the easy
victory of Gray—which seems an- inevitable result—
immediately exhaust public interest in the competition
between “ all round ” and losing hazard play. But if
Gray is allowed to go on makihg hacks of Diggle and
others in exhibition games, there wonß be much gate-
money' left for a championship match with Stevenson or
anybody else.
* * * *
“ Klifton ” : With the object of defeating the
bicy'cle thief, a Melbourne citizen has invented a handle
which can be affixed to or detached from one’s machine
at will, A person having to leave a bicycle out in the
street can, by a simple movement, render it headless,
and thus block the thief pretty effectively. Certainly'
the thief might be a trick rider, who could do without
handles, or he might haye a spare handle up his sleeve ;
but the owner will have to risk that. Still, what’s the
matter with a small padlock fastened round the chain?
* * * *
A queer thing happened at last Geelong race-
meeting. The Peach ran second in the Connewarre Purse.
The judge (J. O. Inglis) says that, before streaking for
Melbourne, he told his assistant to hoist The Peach’s
number below the winner’s. However, that official mis-
takenly put up the figure belonging to Brig o’ Doom Now
the V.R.C. rules say that the judge must make any alter-
ations in his decisions inside half an hour. So the
“bloomer” has to stand, with all its attendant bad
language and injustice.
The Raid of the Aeroplanes is being rehearsed
in France. On Saturday last, Captain Bellunger left Pau
for Parisiwith a fleet of aeroplanes in battle formation.
The affair would appear to have been successful. What
with armored motor-cars, raiding aeroplanes, submarines
and other recent and devilish inventions, war is beooming
a filthy business in which no one will find any glory.
* * * *
True Scot is a real gentleman, who does his
duty in any state of life to which he may be called.
After winning a lot of races, he broke down at the Ade-
laide Cup meeting last May. Since then he has had a
season at the stud, being considered a hopeless proposi-
tion so far as racing is concerned ; but at Caulfield the
other day' he ran fourth in a big field. Braw Scot, winner
of the Hawkesburn Handicap, is a younger brother.
