If the racing at the V.R.C. Autumn Meeting
could be kept up to last Saturday’s level of interest, the
attendances would get a lot nearer to Cup-time figures.
The public saw some sport on Newmarket Handicap day.
It was an occasion for the breaking of time records. The
winners didn’t “ fluke ” home ; they had to get there on
their merits. And.the best thing about the racing was
that, although it was not better for punters than the
average bookies’ bean feast, it was mostly a confirma-
tion of form. Even the sour surprise which the
Wilson stable sprung on the public in the St.
Leger go was within the bounds of reasonable
expectation. Dinaus won the Caulfield Guineas
in brilliant style, and subsequently trained light, after
getting a bad bumping in the Eclipse Stakes ; and be-
cause those subsequent performances were,over longer
distances than the Guineas course, the spry little son of
Bobadil was wrongly assumed to be a squib. In the
Leger race Danaus went out to make the pace, and stayed
in front to the bitter end, just as J.P.A. did in the Vic.
Derby of yore, where Emir and Sweet Nell, his stable com-
panions, were the public fancies. In this case the punters
took even money about Bobadea ; but if its despised
stable companion was able to win from end to end in ex-
ceptionally good time, they had nothing to complain of.
The Newmarket was spoken of as a boil-over,
inasmuch as Blairgoiir, after running brilliantly over 5£
furlongs, and seven furlongs at Caulfield, failed to repeat
its flyaway form up the straight six furlongs at Fleming-
ton. In this case, the opposition was stronger, the pace
was faster from the outset, and all the horses that Blair-
gour had beaten for the Futurity were meeting him on
much better terms. Of these only Desert Rose, the
N.S.W. mare, showed an unreasonable reversal of form.
She went out at 33 to 1, and finished absolutely
last at Caulfield; whereas she was backed dpwn
to 15’s at Flemington and finished second, with
Queen o’ Scots and Broadsword fore and aft of her. For
the rest the running was satisfactory enough. Broad-
sword, the most dangerous opponent of Blairgour on
paper, would probably have won if he had got nearly as
good a start as Queen" o’ Scots did. He was never in the
front rank until the end of the struggle, and then he,got
within a length of the winner.
The advantage of being a bookmaker is very
evident in the result of such races as this. There were
■29 runners, and most of them well backed. The first
and second favorites start at 7 to 4 and 5 to 1 respect-
ively. Neither wins. Consequently the public all round
is left lamenting'; and yet the result is riot a turn-up.
The 20 or so rank outsiders are never in it, with the
exception of England, a 60 to 1 chance, who finished
fourth. The winner is actually third favorite, and .four
of the first five favorites till Ist, 2nd, 3rd and sth places.
All the same, the books collectively win tens of thou-
sands of pounds on the deal, because Blairgour ran dis-
appointingly, and Broadsword didn’t happen to get a
gbod start.
Up to last Saturday, the visiting gees were doing
very badly agginst the local talent in the Melbourne
autumn competitions. Possibly they will have com-
menced to assert themselves before Thk Bulletin is pub-
lished. Victoria, seemingly, has three unquestionable
w'.f.a. “cracks” in Comedy King, Trafalgar and Alawa,
and it is good to see the trio being raced against one
another by owners who get more satisfaction from a vic-
tory than from the cash value of it. They had a great
go for the Essendon Stakes (1£ mile), in which Trafalgar
was expected to'turn the Melbourne.Oup tables on Comedy
King, if ever he could do it,. The Wallace horse was
almost jaunty in his fitness; Comedy King’s coat glistened,
-and Alawa, although a bit above himself in condition,
looked quite capable of snatching a victory if the pace
was slow at the outset. To obviate which, possibility
Trafalgar’s owners started a pacemaker in one Bibben-
luke, who, by the way, barely succeeded in taking the
lead from Son of the Marsh for the first half-mile. When
the two leaders were spun out Trafalgar went ahead.
The idea was that Comedy King might tire under his
Bst. 111 b. burden ; but the black horse proved too good
for Trafalgar after all. He won by a length in the record
time of 2min. 31£sec. It was the race of Trafalgar’s life
thus far ; but he carried 61b. more than the winner, and
couldn’t sprint with him when the crisis was .reached.
Alawa had been practically knocked out by the other two
champions three furlongs from home, yet he fairly beat
the other four starters for place money.
An illustration of the honest uncertainty of the
Turf—as-distinguished from the uncertainty which is
fraudulent—was supplied by the imported horse Eye-
glass at Caulfield and Flemington. At Caulfield, he was
reckoned by trainer Scobie to be a moral for one of the
minor handicaps. He never gave his backers a hope.
Trainer could only say that Eyeglass ran a very bad race.
There were no excuses for the defeat. A week later, at
Flemington, the stable had some misgivings as to how
the animal would perform, but this time Eyeglass ran
gamely and won a hard race.
“.Timothy Tufthunter” : Read this in a
London paper for January 6 about Australia :
“In this country nearly as much is spent upon
cigarettes as upon national defence.” When we
have been Japanned, think of this for an epitaph:
“They preferred cigarettes to Liberty.”
C. Y. O’Connor, one of the best engineers
Australia has ever known, strongly advocated an
overhead railway from East Perth through Perth,
so that the city should not be carved in two by
banks and cuttings crossed by bridges. That whs
as far back as 1896. But the Government kept on
bridge-building and city-bisecting; and now the
Barrack-street bridge-widening calls for resump-
tions and heavy payments, principally because
the people who didn’t know wouldn’t listen to the
man who did knowi

Concerning the home of the lately-deacl
“ Jimmy ” Russell: —
Barunah Plains Station forms part of the immense
slieep-run of the Mercers which, no longer than 50 years
ago, comprised all the rich country between the Barwon
and the Leigh (Vic.). Afterwards the squatter kingdom
was cut into six or seven fine stations. The original run
was called Weatherboard. Its present name is Inver-
leigh. There was nothing between Weatherboard, in the
elbow of the Barwon and the Leigh, near Geelong, and
Golf Hill (which belonged to the George Russells), on the
upper fork of the Leigh towards Ballarat. The Mercers
owned this great piece of land as a sheep run ; but they
lived in Scotland and left it to a manager to work. They
had another gigantic place for cattle and horses at Mount
Mercer, near Ballarat and Buninyong. There were no
fences and consequently no paddocks ; the sheep were
shepherded by day and yarded with movable hurdles at
night. One Bob Bass was a famous shepherd on this
run, and his wife helped him to move the hurdles. A son
and daughter of the couple afterwards carried on the
>hepherding job as a sort of hereditary privilege. Bass
wars an old Van Demonian, but, for all that, was
a fine old chap, much trusted by his employers.
Most of the men employed in that part, at that
time, were of the same type. They were the earliest con-
victs of Tasmania, and formed a sort of aristocracy. A
later crowd were counted unmanageables, and from them
the earlier “forced immigrants ” held themselves aloof
with all the uppishness of any other older aristocracy.
The tyranny of English laws and the ferocity of English
Judges helped the early squatters to man their vast tracts
of country. But tyrannical laws and ferocious Judges
sometimes did better for their victims than they in-
tended ; for many a hard-working honest man of the Bass
type carved a niche for himself in Australian wool his-
tory, and filled it to the great profit of his family.
YOU DON'T KNOW WHAT A
* SMOKE * IS TILL YOU'VE
TRIED MY CIGARS
o^y\>
I’M going to have my Cigars smoked over every
part of Australasia—and the most confirmed
pipe-smokers are going to be the biggest
smokers of my Cigars, too.
Talk about joy! Why, to sit round a camp
fire, over a billy of tea, pitching to your mates,
each of you smoking one of my Cigars, joy isn’t
in it! It’s just simply bliss!
As the yarns go round, the blue smoke
curling upwards in a fragrant cloud, the
smooth cool Havana flavor lingering on your
lips—l tell you, you'll spend some of the
happiest hours of your life.
%<lt£h
Letters are coming in to
me in shoals from all over
Australasia, praising up my
Cigars. Take one I've just
got as I write this, for in-
stance—it's from a chap on a
cattle station up in Northern
Queensland, He writes:—•
“Those DIADEMAS Cigars
ofyoursare just alright. I've
always smoked a pipe—but
no more. Not that I’vegiven
up pipe-smoking altogether;
but when I want a real smoke
—something rich —I light up
one of your DIADEMAS,
and give myself up to its
pure enjoyment."
To make you acquainted
with my Cigars, and prove
their value, I make you this
FREE OFFER of
5 or 6 CIGARS
you may select.
Send for 100 of any of
Remit the amount, and
I will deliver promptly,
postage or carriage paid,
to any address in Aus-
tralia.
Smoke 5 op 6 of them, and if they’re
not superior to most Cigars at double the
price, send the remainder back to me, and
I’ll return every penny of your money —no
charge made for the Cigars you’ve smoked.
My Cigars are made from the very finest
mild, rich mellow Tobacco, the filling or inside
heart of the cigar being all straight long
, leaf. The Cigars are hand-made by skilled
workers, and every box has to pass me before
it goes out to a customer ; and is guaranteed
to burn freely and draw easily.
There s no “catch” about this offer—it’s just
plain and honest. To prove it, you can refer
as to my financial position and way of doing
business to the Manager of the Commercial Bank
of Australia, Collins Street, Melbourne. He’ll
vouch for me.
Send down for a box to-day—once you smoke

from me. When ordering, say whether you
like light, medium, or dark colors,

MEDLOW BATH, Blue Mountains, N.S.W.
MAJESTIC HOTEL.
COMPLETE arrangements have been made by the new proprietor (Mr. W. H. Burgess), late of the Grand
Hotel, Melbourne, for the comfort of visitors during the forthcoming season.
Medlow Bath is an ideal resort. The crisp mountain air is a delight, and the mountains, in their
Spring garb, appeal to lovers of the picturesque. Arrangements can be made at the Hotel for motors,
carriages and riding tours.
Motor parties can be arranged any time to start from the City Office —or your own residence—direct
to the Majestic Hotel.
To pedestrians Medlow offers ever-varying attractions. The views in the mountains are superb, and
hours can be spent in walkthrough the Hotel grounds.
Hot-air pipes are laid in many rooms. Daily Motor trips are made from the Hotel to Jenolan Oaves.
Further particulars can be obtained from the Hotel, or from the Sydney Offices, 42 Oastlereagh Street.
W. H. BURGESS, Proprietor.

NO more positive proof of the worthiness of an “ Alcock ” billiard table could
be given than by the splendid performance of George Gray, the young
Queensland player, in his match with Fred Lindrum, jun.
A Standard “Alcock” Billiard Table was used, and certified to by the
Amateur Billiard Association of Victoria (which is affiliated with the Billiard
Association of Great Britain and Ireland).
Gray* who made 836 —831 coming off the red, and thus made two world’s
records —used Crystallite balls, and the marvellous accuracy with which they came
off the cushions, the ease with which the most difficult shots were accomplished,
speaks for itself of the merit and superiority of “ Alcock” Billiard Tables.

IN BANANA LAND
Bishop Duhig, in his Lenten pastoral, re-
marks re the Bible in State schools business :
‘‘As servants of the State, Catholic teachers are
free to give the Scripture lessons provided in the
State-school curriculum.” Quite true. But the real
trouble is that, no matter what may be the con-
scientious objections of Catholic (and other)
teachers, they are not free not to give the Scripture
lessons. Conscience is coerced to make a Wowser
holiday.
Some papers, like some politicians, go right
round the clock. The Rockhampton Record,
once the child of Stewart Hartley; was, in State
politics, the most Tory rag in the Northern inky
way. But Hartley died, and younger fingers took
the pen. To-day, the Record is a Labor paper,
and has just put up a good fight for Adamson.
On the other hand, the Rockhampton Bulletin,
which was at one time the hope of the Labor
party, travelled round the other way, and has now
reached the 12 o’clock midnight of Tory gloom.
Mt. Morganaire Walter Hall keeps in re-
membrance the spot where he and Luck made a
fortune for him. His cheques for £5OO each have
lately reached one hospital at Mount Morgan, two
at Rockhampton, and two at Brisbane. There
were plenty of other Morganaires ; but they have
drifted into chronic absenteeism, and the Fog has
blotted Queensland out of their memory. Mount
Morgan hospital, by the way, is one of the hap-
piest in the State. Every toiler in the mines has
a small fraction docked from iiis pay, and, though
lie practically doesn’t know it is docked, it gives
him the right to a hospital which he has good
reason to be proud of.
Though the Bible in State schools ques-
tion has been settled by Bunanaland’s
the accounts of the Labor members who forgot
their platform have yet to be audited. Crawford,
who represents Fitzroy, was one of those who
forgot that the platform said—“ Education,
secular and free.” He recently sent his subscrip-
tion to the Fitzroy W.P.0., Mt. Morgan. A
special meeting was held, and Crawford got his
Bs. back, and this resolution with it: “That the
subscription be not accepted, and that Mr. Craw-
ford be informed that he is consequently not a
member of the Fitzroy W.P.0.” Now, Crawford
has written to the sec. of the Parliamentary Labor
Party for a helping hand; and meanwhile the
Fat elector of Fitzroy chuckles and thanks the
Devil once more for sending the insectarian curse.
Harry Mikalsen, a driver in the Royal
Artillery at Brisbane, lately climbed again to the
top of Coonowrim, one of the Glasshouse Moun-
tains. He was the first man to scale the peak,
which had been previously considered inacces-
sible. That was on March 11,1910, and he has got
to the summit three times since ; but says he
won’t tackle it any more. On his last trip he
was accompanied by Tom Roberts, a local timber-
getter, and Tom Welsby, a well-known Bnsbane-
ite. Welsby had climbed the other peaks of the
Glasshouse many years ago; but lie’s not as
young as he used to be, and had to give in when
about two-thirds of the way up Coonowrim.
Many years ago the blacks living thereabouts told
Welsby that no Binghi had ever scaled the peak,
which was said by them to be the abode of devils
of a particularly virulent type.
Trout just managed to hold the Enoggera
seat, vacated by Hawthorn, for the Denham
Government. The new member is a local butcher
: and a decent sort, whose reputation for letting
the beef bill run on has undoubtedly saved
Premier Digby from a nasty bump.
“The Jester” : Re W. H. Ramni’s claim
on the Bananaland Government for £5OOO, for
discovering that inoculation with the blood of a
recovered beast is a preventive of tick-fever in
cattle. My experience is that the immunity is
only temporary; and if a beast which has been
inoculated should be over-driven or knocked
about, it is liable to take tick-fever without hav-
ing any ticks on it at the time. Moreover, if
employed on milch cows it causes them to go dry.
In these circumstances, what is the use of the
inoculation, and what possible claim can Ramm
have on the Bananaland Government ?
If ever a town was politically bought and
bought in vain, that town was Rockhampton at
the election of 1909. It was notorious that the
Government candidates on that occasion owed
their victory to the lavish promises of the expen-
diture of Treasury money on railways, local
works, etc. Then Higgs came along and settled
the hash of the Federal Conservative candidate.
Now Laborite Adamson carries the much-bought
town into the Democratic fold, and all the mag-
nificent local railways have been promised and
built to no purpose. Even the proposed Ten
Million loan won’t save the Tory hash much
longer.
A MATTER OF CONSCIENCE.
MR. SOKER (astounded): “ What? You voted for reduction? Why?"
MR. BOKER : “ Twenty-eight pubs in our district, my boy. Can't .get round 'em all in
one evening. Must reduce 'em!”
“ The Interpreter ” to the rescue : —
You can scoff if you like, but that parson is quite
correct about the Waybaek runs and their retinues of low
women. I struck one such place when I was seeking my
fortune in the Mulga— struck it one afternoon wheri my
form contained little but a longing for home and
mother, and my boots were trying to surround the two
tenderest feet outside of Sheol. There were 73 Flossies
on the ranch on my arrival. There had, at one time, so I
heard later, been 204 ; but, business Wing slack, they had
resorted to cannibalism. Hence the Shrinkage. Jumbuck
and his missus had long since gone the way of all sinners
who import Flossies and overlook the tucker department.
My arrival was greeted with loud crie$ of joy, and the whole
73 bore down on me as one Flossie. I, being young and shy,
blushed. That was my last blush* on earth. I have a
hazy recollection that J was torn limb from limb and
eaten raw, though I may have been boiled first. I have
been killed in a Victorian train and carried away, limp
and lifeless, by victorious Norfolk-Howards in a Sydney
doss-house ; but death at the hands of starving Deliluhs
is the worst death of all. If that parson is going to wipe
those places out of existence, please forward him one
tobacco tag at my expense.
“Bromide” on the “ livipg white ” cam-
paign :—
I have been kicking about the Darling Downs the last
few weeks, and know now that there is one part of
Queensland where you can walk about without falling
over a colored alien every yard or two. They are here
all right, but not as plentiful as in other parts, mainly
because a big percentage of the people won’t patronise
them. The Conservative Downs cocky is evidently a
much better Australian than the so-called “good-Labor-
ite ” shearer “ Leo Lear ” has been fraternising with. I
reckon the crowd “Leo Liar” knows takes A.W.U.
tickets when it goes shearing because it’s got to. That
lot talks Labor and Union until your head aches—when
it is out West. But when it gets back on the farm, or
into the city, you never find it taking an active part in
anything. No doubt it-supports the Chow hotel and
Ram Chunder all right—the “ good ” Laborite doesn’t.
And one needs to choose as carefully in Brisbane as in
the North. I have padded the hoof for several hours
going from pub to pub in the city looking for a “ white ”
house, and couldn’t find one. Mr. Beerpump tells you “ I
can’t get a white cook.” In Brisbane, mind you. He’d
get white help all right if his best customers turned heel
on the place as long as it was manned by the discolored
sort.
The Optimist.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Optimism is the ability to take a brig-ht view of the
misfortunes of other people.— Recent definition.
WHEN some folk collar others’ cash
Or scoot with others’ girls ;
When raging villains madly bash
Some other’s teeth of pearls;
When other men sit down and mourn
The girls they haven’t kissed,
I bear those woes with lightsome mien—
I am an Optimist !
When some one kicks my neighbor’s toes,
Or grinds his tender corn ;
When grog-blooms on my neighbor’s nose
Induce his wife to mourn ;
When neighbor Jones has broken bones
From neighbor Jenkin’s fist,
I greet them both i n happy mood —
I am an Optimist!
When other men with others’ wives
Will “carry on ” too free,
And desolate each other’s lives
And flit to gay Paree;
When he who’s ta’en his neighbor’s grain
Doth call it lawful grist,
I have no grief for theft or thief —
I am an Optimist!
’Tis bad enough to have to weep
A toothache of your own,
Without a-spoiling of your sleep
Through hearing others groan ;
When grumblers moan their fortune flown
I never even list,
But take to flight in pleasant plight—
I am an Optimist!
I always strive with eager zest
To never have a care,
And always do my level best
No others’ grief to share ;
But if the whiners come my way
I always bid them “ Hist!
My view of life is always gay—
lam an Optimist! ”
QUEENSLAND.
F.B.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, March 4, 1911.
My Dear Thargomindah,—
Your Mario has often looked with longing eyes
at the charming strip of green turf at the back of
the Legislative Assembly House, and pictured to
herself how well it would fid in as a background to
a well-run tea fight. The same idea seems to have
struck Mrs. Holmes A’Court, and last week she
planted there half a hundred small, rose-decked
tables and three times as many folk to sit at them,
stationed a “ Made in Germany ” Band in the
hinterland, laid in a large stock of ice creams and
sweets, and the thing was done. Everyone who
was anybody went along and shook daughter
Gladys good-bye. The farewelled will leave in a
fortnight’s time for America, there to wed a
cousin. Mrs. A’Court said “how-de-do” in a
black and white striped grenadine gown, and
Miss Gladys was in ivory crepe. The very latest
bride, Mrs. Carl Palmer, shone in a French con-
fection and a fringed tile ; and a pretty maid from
Melbourne, Miss Davidson, who came with Mrs.
Jopp, made many conquests.
Miss Dorothy Brockway (whose doctor-father,
by the way, has written a new book to follow the
readable “Castle Cavarkas”) has returned from a
three-years’ visit to the land of fogs. Most of
that time she put in at the Southport College of
physical training, odd time being given over to
study in the Big Smoke itself ; and she is now
prepared to teach the local infant how to enlarge
a chest and broaden a back and grow up a solid,
straight Australian. To watch the young lady
skip and heave clubs one would imagine she was
like the American canvasser : “ One-haff caaper
wire and one haff fiddle-string.”
Celia Ghiloni, in emerald green clothes, hit
Brisbane in its most hittable eye on Tuesday, and
before it could recover itself Hugh Ward had
jumped upon it, disguised as the Professor in
“Rector’s.” Palotta’s gowns and the Judge in
buttons were the next two most popular things
in the effervescence, which was suspected of being
something shocking, but which in reality
shouldn’t hit the genus Wowser half so heavily as
the “ Waltz Dream,” over which he entirely
neglected to bleat.
Talking of the profession, Miss Tempe Pigott
is here on a very brief holiday, after six months
in the country managed for Cohen by Joseph
Ward. She waxes eloquent over the “happy
family ” air which fittingly pervaded the “ Lover’s
Lane ” Co. This week she will be in Sydney to
rejoin the co. for the Palace season.
Brisbane is to have the first look at the Hawaiian
singers, instrumentalists and “Hula” dancers,
whom Harold Ashton discovered on his way back
from America. In its native undress, the “Hula”
is said to be (and my informant was speaking first-
hand) a measure to which it would not be advis-
able to take a Young Women’s Guild of Christian
Helpers; but it has had clothes hung round it so
that the comment of the Y.W.G.C.H. may be
turned aside.
A returned tripper wails to me of the discom-
fort of travelling overland by the Sydney-bound
mail train. The 27 hours’ journey is bad enough in
itself, but it is made even more depressing by the
failure ohi the part of the management to make
those small improvements in the service which
mean so much to exhausted all-nighters. I can’t
say how much justice is in the complaint, but the
great and increasing number of people who travel
by boat would seem to give it some support.
So glad you liked the cultivated voice of Miss
Lena Hammond in the “ Elijah.” She was bred
in Brisbane, and comes of a singing family ; but
she is one of those quiet, hard-working people,
apt to be overlooked when limelight is being
served out. What that girl wants is a good,
healthy trumpeter, with twin-screw wind-power.
Mention of trumpeters naturally turns my mind
in a military direction, and reminds me that
Brisbane has another Lieut.-Colonel on its list in
the person of the energetic Hubert Harris, who
commanded one or two Australian contingents
during the Chovvth African war, which General
Buller’s announced book promises to make more
smeilful than ever. In his spare moments H. H.
is the busiest man alive; what he must be like
when he is busy I tremble to think.
Constable Burke, of Brisbane, a John (both
by profession and by christening), has been
singled out by George V. for a King’s
Police Medal. The valiant officer grappled
with a desperado who was shooting wild
and free in the merry town of Croydon. Unfor-
tunately, he did not get in quickly enough to save
the marked-up victim of the man with the gun,
but he did his best, and, when it came to the
royal ears, their owner said : “ It’s good enough
for me.” So the medal’s on its road.
Elaine Feez, with the musical fingers and Titian
hair, departed from Sydney last week, intending
to pick up more music made in Germany. Arrived
in the land which is blessed with two Providences,
the other one having the front name of Bill, she
will, I hear, be received into the family of her old
master as a lamb which has strayed awhile.
Two returnees of note are the Slades, mother
and daughter. That is to say, they haven’t struck
this city as yet; but a pal ran into them at the
Australia, looking bonnie after a lovely time in
Egypt. There will be joy in more than one gal-
lant uniform-covered breast when they do reach
Pinkenba once more.
On “ the very best ” authority, Thargomindah,
my dear, I am informed that July will see us
with a new military commandant, as Col. Lyster
is to retire. Who the new man will be, goodness
alone knows. But if Providence could see a way
to give us some 1 hing young and dashing—well,
we would be grateful, that’s all.
G. W. Gray, who sits in the elderly Legislative
House, and is a good sport, is in your city this
week, watching the Springboks trying to scoop up
what is left of the ashes with a cricket bat.
Mrs. Gray is with him at the Australia, where, I
hear, Premieress Denham, with her daughters,
will be discoverable in a few days’ time.
Among the other Queenslanders at your harbor
side are Mrs. George Munro, Mrs. Lex Craig
(who will return to us by the Otway on the 20tli),
and the Ernest Baynes women-folk, who still
linger in the blue mists of the Fly Mountains.
Yours affectionately,
MARIO.
Dr. D. F. Macdonald, who fought the
cause of a White Australia in the “blackest”
district of Bananaland, and carried out original
research work in regard to the causes of the earth-
hunger disease, passed out recently in Liberia,
W. Africa.


THE HILL END GOLD MINES.
The Story of the Great Amalgamation at Hawkins Hill.
• . V** * o’
An Interview with Scantiebury by Jeffries.
The story of Hawkins Hill is a fascinating
one. The first of New South Wales’ quartz
fields, it flung fortunes in the faces of its
pioneers for single crushings. Breaking out
stuff for the mill, the miners frequently
came on what was almost solid gold, or
hunks of gold slightly adulterated by frag-
ments of quartz. Men went on to the field
destitute of even blankets, and in a few
months rooted out fortunes. There has been
no field on earth like it. Fortune herself
was never more capricious. And when the
men who had taken tons of gold out of
claims barely large enough to decently bury
themselves in, sold their mines, for what in
those days were fabulous sums, to com-
panies, the gold did what they called “gave
out.” That is to say, it dropped to a few
ounces per ton, and then the people who
had been accustomed to playing with masses
of gold, and to making fortunes in a few
months, disgustedly faded away. Scattered
over the area known as Hawkins Hill were
170 gold mines, and the field was like Eng-
land in the days of the Heptarchy. No
Egbert appeared to consolidate it, and it
faded right out of the list of the world’s
great gold fields, after having done prob-
ably more than any other field on earth to
establish the gold supremacy of Britain at
a time when the wealth of plundered India
had been squandered; and helped to make
Australia known the world over as a land
where gold could be found in solid masses.
And what has been done on that ancient
field is an alluring story. Two years ago I
met Scantlebury, gripsack in hand, on the
railway station. “Where to?” “Hill End.
A fine book for you some day,” he laughed
as he dashed for the train.
Night after night he talked of this
bewildering Tom Tiddler’s Ground, and ex-
pressed his belief that there was almost un-
limited gold there yet for the seeking. And
he talked of the difficulties, of all the con-
flicting interests that still, ghostlike,
haunted the dead El Dorado. He battled on
bravely, making explorations, crush mgs, and
working heroically to bring about an amal-
gamation. The trouble was that on Hill End
there was a wonderful belief in the place.
The wealth of Croesus was hidden away
there. Some day must be found, and he
would hang on, spite of fate, till that great
day of discoverey and fortune came. And
it was against this blind faith that Scantle-
bury battled. Against this fanaticism he
could bring only cold logic, and the possi-;
bility of getting the public to supply the
money for a further investigation of the
field. And now, impossible as it seemed
right up to a few weeks ago, he has man-
aged to bring about the consolidation that
alone can restore the field to its ancient
glory, and, who knows, discover a Golden
Mile in New South Wales.
Then he launched his prospectus. All
conflicting claims have been adjusted,
and are offered to the public under the name
of Hill End Amalgamated. It rests with
the public to say whether work shall go on
or not. Many queries have been made as to
why this great achievement had not been
carried out before, and I went and asked
him a number of “whys,” and this is what
he said:
“Twenty acres of the 93 acres now offered
to the public produced over a ton of gold to
the acre, and I shall not have to live many
years to see it produce another ton to the
acre; for it produced over 20 tons of gold
within 400 feet of the surface. And if
there are not tons of gold still left above as
well as below the 400 feet, I am a mining
fool —one of the biggest in Australia. And
that is saying a lot.
“Remember the surface area of Hawkins
Hill is only half worked. Look at the
plan. It does not require any mining
knowledge to see that the eastern boundary
of the 23 little claims occupying less than
25 acres ended just before the crown of
Hawkins Hill is reached. Also, that though
a few rich specimens were found by one
Bill Adams on the surface, the reefs were
invariably poor, and it was not until Carroll
and Beard reached 80 feet that any rich
gold appeared in the reefs. And it was
only then that the rest of the claim holders
went hammer and tongs down. Hence the
little rush because of Bill Adams’s Sunday
morning find early in ’69 did not develop
into a big rush until over a year. Mean-
while the leasing system was adopted and
the crown of Hawkins Hill, as well as the
eastern slope of it, was occupied by a num-
ber of 5 and 10 acre gold leases. Several
tunnels were started to intercept these
other surface reefs at from 500 to 750 feet
below the crown of the hill.
“The rock on that side is very dense, so
progress was slow, and so, although several
thousand feet were driven, not one tunnel
was carried into the defined auriferous
channel, consequently all the reefs, and
there were many of them, beyond the east-
ern boundary of the famous Hawkins Hill
claims, which reefs were equally poor on
the surface, remained in their virgin state.
“Now, that is a serious statement to
make, but it is made deliberately, and with
knowledge aforethought. Had one of the
tunnels been continued from the eastern base
of Hawkins Hill, these reefs would have
all been cut within 1500 feet, and another
500 to 1000 feet would have intersected all
the reefs worked in Krohmans, Beyers and
Holtermann’s, Paxton’s and other claims at
700 to 800 feet, or 300 to 400 feet lower
than they have ever been worked.
“So here we have a field, the richest in
Australia, lying derelict and dormant, with
only one-half of its surface area touched,
and I most solemnly declare that there is
no other gold field in Australia with such
a record remaining in such a condition.
That is a stigma on N.S.W. mining intelli-
gence and enterprise which with the public
help is going to be removed. Dead Haw-
kins Hill, as sure as I’m alive, is going to
be revived, and will produce many more
tons of gold, and will continue its great
gold production many years after I am
dead.”
* * * *
This sweeping and emphatic statement
moved me to ask some questions.
“Can you say why this amazing field has
remained ‘derelict and dormant’ all this
time? In fact, there are several ‘Whys’
that I wish to put to you.”
He replied: “Well, to your first. Be-
cause of the absence of the consolidation
which it has taken me more than a year to
bring about.”
“Well, why didn’t amalgamation take
place while the claims were rich?”
“Of all times that is when it is most im-
possible, simply because they are rich. Re-
member, they were not equally rich, neither
were they simultaneously so. I remember
when I was working in the Bendigo mines
it took ten years, to amalgamate four
claims on one line of reef. Mines are never
amalgamated when they are rich. How
many years ago is it since they first talked
about amalgamation of Broken Hill? I re-
member writing in The Bulletin about it.
And yet, athough the benefits-of amalgama-
tion are as patent there as elsewhere, not a
single amalgamation has taken place. Ten
years ago it was proposed to amalgamate
all the mines in the Golden Mile, Kalgoor-
lie, and only one has been brought about,
namely, the Oroya with the Brownhill.
“Producing mines also produce vested and
conflicting interests, and while these exist
adjustment .of values for amalgamation is
impossible. When they become poor they
lean towards each other. And I stepped
into the breach at Hill End when that camp
had reached its most pathetic starvation
stage. Had I gone before, like others, I
would have failed.”
“Well, why were they not amalgamated
when the famous bonanzas gave out ?”
“Here’s the answer, from ‘Gold Mines and
Mining in Hawkins Hill,’ published 1880.
‘lf the owners of the small claims between
the Star of Peace and Krohman’s could
safely surrender their titles so as to work
the area by one company, capital would be
forthcoming.’ Up to that time and for
some years afterwards N.S.W. Mining Law
compelled the giving up of one title before
another for a larger area could be ob-
tained, and, as it gave no preference, each
one had to take his luck in a new gamble.
Consequently, no one would take the risk,”
“Well, why on earth weren’t they amal-
gamated when this fool law was changed?”
“Well, many attempts were made. The
Nitrate King, Colonel North, of Chilian
fame, sent his representative to the field
specially for the purpose; and prepared to
pay a lot of money. But not as much as
the owners were prepared to ask. Also,
there was such a number of them that what
seemed a modest price became a ruinous
one by reason of the multiplicity. Harry
Newman, of Lucknow, secured a number of
the claims for £70,000 cash and would
probably have brought about the amalga-
mation and floated a company in London to
work the field but for the death of the
Duke of Manchester, who had visited ’.lie
field when the scheme was inaugurated.”
“And why were they not amalgamated
when the Government subsidy of £30,000
was proposed?”
“Simply because of the subsidy. It
might have been done before, but not after-
wards. As Government Geologist Pittman n
says: ‘Hawkins Hill can only be success-
fully worked by a powerful company, as the
necessary operations require a very consid-
erable amount of capital, and the subscrib-
ing of the capital would only be warranted
by the possession of titles to a considerable
area of land.’ ”
“Well, after all, why was not work con-
tinued without amalgamation? There must
have been some gold about?”
“Make no mistake, it would have been had
the rich gold remained. But it didn’t.
Let me try to picture to you the transfor-
mation that took place. Krohman, ac-
cording to translations I have had made
from his German diary, had been raking
out gold at the rate of from 100 to 1000
ounces per ton for several crushings. Then he
sold his claim for 80,000 sovereigns, and
went back, and at the first crushing he pro-
duced for his shareholders a ton of gold,
worth £95,000, and paid his shareholders ±1
per share dividend. It was the first, and,
as far as I know, the last. Beyers and
Holterniann sold their mine for £70,000,
and from a 16,000 ounce crushing
tney paid a 75 per cent. dividend.
But I can find no record of any other.
‘.Northumberland Jimmy’ got two tons
of gold out of the smallest claim on
the field, and then sold to a company, but
I can find no record of that company pay-
ing any dividend. These things made
men mad. All sorts of companies were
floated. Amalgamation was never dreamed
of —more probably people considered the
advisability of cutting Paxton’s 125 feet
claim into four or five, after two tons pro-
duced 4200 ounces. The reefs without a
single exception remained, and can be seen
to-day. No change of country came in, but
a mighty change of richness. The gold
did not entirely disappear. But it dropped
from many ounces per ton to less dwts.
‘Such a falling off was there!’ Think of
the moral effect on these people who had
been accustomed to hearing of tons of gold
being cleaned up. One instance: A
report of 1872. ‘Great disappointment is
felt at Beyers and Holtermann’s last crush-
ing only going seven ounces to the ton. It
is a mistake to anticipate large yields
when comparatively inferior stuff is being
put through.’ Seven ounces to the ton,
and they are greatly disappointed! There
was disruption, chaos, if you like, but not
amalgamation.”
“Well, why is so much money—£7o,ooo —
It’s a tremendous swag.”
“Well, I’m hopeful that it won’t be
wanted. But it’s better to have too much
than too little. You can’t mine without
money, but that has been tried too often
in this country. If less than half this
money is required, the money will not be
wasted. There will be no extravagance or
whste if I can help it. But it will be
there if it is required.”
“And what are the chief aims of this
amalgamation ?”
“To work Hawkins Hill as one great
mine,-instead of the 170 which once occu-
pied it. Just as the large areas were a
good thing for the squatters in the old days
when population was scarce, so closer settle-
ment on Hawkins Hill in those days was
excellent, but large areas are wanted at
Hawkins Hill to-day.”
“Give your reasons.”
“Every tunnel site can be availed of.
The little claims were compelled to work
with shafts. So they sank over 50 of
them, and altogether they did enough sink-
ing to have gone down to 4000 feet instead
of 400, had it been confined to three shafts.
They did enough tunnelling to pierce Haw-
kins Hill from one end to the other, and
from one side to the other, had it been
confined to two tunnels instead of 20. So
the tunnelling was in vain, but the drives
are there waiting to be extended now the
ground is in possession of one company.
This company will of course have its own
stamp mill and other equipment. In the
old days the claim owners were dependent
on public batteries. I assume that
they had over 100 managers. Think
of the offices, secretaries, etc., they
had to keep up, and the 40 pubs (24 on the
road to Bathurst), all had to be maintained
out of the mines. There were seven hotels
there right up against the shafts on the
crown of the ridge, bleeding it.”
“Now, I want to ask you this. These
are worked-out mines. Now, WHAT HAS
BEEN LEFT?”
“To start with, the worked stopes are
full of mullock, many thousands of tons of
it, and 2000 tons crushed an average of
6 dwts. There is a big dump remaining
on the surface. Many years ago a bat-
tery was worn out crushing over 30,000 tons
of it. What it went I don’t know, but I
do know that men are making a living on
it to-day, and have been doing so for up-
wards of 20 years And no man can
make a living by culling and dollying a
mullock dump without leaving enough gold
behind to pay for crushing it It is exactly
the same as with the dredging areas all over
Australia and the world. It was the cus-
tom for children to watch for the tipping
of the mullock, whereupon they would
scramble over the heap for specimens. So
that if the mullock on the surface which
has been so impoverished is still good
enough to crush, then-the chances are that
there are dividends in that below, and the
2000 tons crushed is proof of it.
‘Then tnere are big ore faces left Bear
in mind that up to the last few years it was
impossible to get at these because all the
working shafts had fallen in. Access has
now been given by the Consolidated Tunnel,
and during last year several old
levels and workings have been cleaned out.
You can see the faces at Beyers and
Holtermann’s, Kroliman’s and Paxton’s, and
other reefs and formations which were too
poor to work in the 70’s, and consequently
abandoned after some hundred feet dt de-
velopment and prospecting work on them
had been done. They were too low grade
to pay, but the low-grade proposition of
the 70’s all the gold-mining world over, is
the high-grade proposition of to-day. These
faces have been sampled by bulk crushings,
the lowest of which was 12dwts. to the
ton, and the average has been an ounce.
Another cleaning up will take place at the
end of this week, and I saw the stuff in
the battery paddock ten days ago, and I’ll
wager that it will go over an ounce to the
ton. I have satisfied myself beyond the
shadow of a possible doubt that these aban-
doned faces will pay to work to-day.”
“What about the chance of the recur-
rence of bonanzas?”
“We’re not building on that. This is
not an off-chance proposition, but, if I take
Bendigo, where I worked for years as a
miner, as a guide, there is every prospect oi
their recurrence. Why, 25 feet below the
tunnel level last year the most easterly
worked vein became enricned. Three
crushings averaged 10 ounces to the ton. I
don’t go much on it, because the vein
is small —very small. But it is evidence
that the high-grade ore is likely to recur at
any time. Had the Bendigo mines stopped
at their 300 to 500 feet poor zone, there
would have been no Bendigo to-day, and had
the same conditions there existed they
would have stopped.
“No doubt the intersection of these reefs
by ‘indicators or droppers’ accounted for
their phenomenal richness in the vicinity of
contact. These ‘droppers’ did not confine
themselves to one reef; they passed through
one to the other, therefore each was rich in
turn. If that is their general nature there
are several reefs that have not been inter-
sected, and the untouched reefs to the east
must be intersected at higher levels. To
illustrate: The Hawkins Hill reefs have
an easterly underlay of from one in four to
six feet. The ‘droppers’ have a similar
underlay in an opposite direction. Now
the reefs and ‘droppers’ are contained in
an auriferous channel several hundred feet
wide, so that the intersection that would
take place in one reef on the eastern side
of the channel would be several hundred
feet above the intersection of the reef to-
wards the west. In some cases intersec-
tion has taken place, and it only remains
for development to prove what effect con-
tact has on other reefs.”
“Have any rich deposits been missed?”
“I do not know, but should think so. I
remember some old working being exposed
in a deep gorge at Hillgrove by a tremen-
dous landslip, shewing how antimony de-
posits had been missed by a few feet. If it
were possible to open the old claim on Haw-
kins Hill like the pages of a book, undoubt-
edly some missed bonanzas would be ex-
posed.”
“What do you propose to do in the way
of prospecting, development, and equip-
ment ?”
“To keep the present battery running to
sample the different faces exposed, also on
the mullock dumps as well as that in the
stopes, and to erect a big battery when war-
ranted by the sampling done by the little
one. To thoroughly clean out old levels, and
to expose other faces of stone. To extend
the present tunnel further east, to intersect
other reefs known to exist in that direction.
To start a deeper level tunnel, and to clean
out the Star of Peace shaft, and to do some
prospecting at the bottom.”
“In what ways does Hill End resemble
other great Australian goldfields?”
“In many ways. In all producing gold-
fields such as Bendigo, Ballarat, Charters
Towers, Gympie and others permanent gold
is invariably associated with other metals
and minerals, such as galena, graphite,
plumbago, mica, black, greasy lava slates.
And we have them all in the mines of Haw-
kins Hill. It is almost a replica of Ben-
digo in its surface geological features. It
is the only place I know of in N.S.W.
where you have a wide auriferous
channel containing several different lines of
reefs, and distinct formations extending
over miles.”
“And what are your chances of pulling
this through—how is the money coming
in?”
“Not so rapidly as I would like. But we
are receiving share applications from all
over New South Wales. Queensland has
made a start. I anticipate Westralia will
this week. Victoria and South Australia
would but for their Chaffinch debacle. The
applications are numerous enough if thev
were big enough. It looks as if
we will have to get 2000 shareholders
before the list is filled. What I would
like to conclude with is this: If 10 per
cent, of The Bulletin readers of this inter-
view will subscribe for an average of only
100 shares each, the issue of 400,000 shares
will be accomplished. And I would like
you to advertise the application form below
this yarn, so that it will be ready for every
reader who wants to become a Hill End
Amalgamated shareholder.”

The Inky Way.
When tlie last English mail left, still another paper for
the enlightenment of J. Bull with regard to Australia was
breaking out of the shell. The new chick is ta be called
the Australasian News, and is to appeal to the intending
emigrant at a ha'penny a time.
“ Eryander ” : The dignified-looking but clums3 T Roman
notation is gradually dying the death of the superfluous
—even for monumental purposes. The maximum of cum-
brousness was reached with ISBB—MDOCCLXXXVIII.
With 1900 we came down (or should have come down) to
MOM. That is, M = 1000 ; CM = 1000 less 100 = 900. Hence,
the correct notation of the present year of grace is
MOMXI, and not, as I observe it rendered on the title-
pqge of some official books, MDOCCOXI.
“Ivanhoe ”: “ Penman ” (B. 12/l/’ll) utters a melodra-
matic wail about the “ white-chokered blackleg ” in the
inkslingers’ camp. Why should a man be damned in this
way because he happens to be a parson ? Lawyers,
doctors, travellers, school-teachers and many others can
turn an honest penny by a little scribbling; but if a
parson, who doesn’t draw anything like as g'ood a salary,
tries to do the same, “ Penman ” and his kind fall upon
him with loud shrieks. Why shouldn’t a parson write if
he can? I have known more than one parson-contributor
to The Bulletin. At his best, James llebblethwaite was
amongst the most gifted versifiers that the Red Paper
ever knew. If “Penman” finds that the parson writes
better than he does he needn’t get mad about it; let him
go and break stones or kill pigs or do something else that
he is fitted for.
* * * *
A new evening paper is projected for Hobart, in con-
nection with the Daily Post. The only evening sheet in
the island is the Tasmanian News, which, after 25 years
of battling for a more or less adequately buttered crust,
under several separate proprietors, finds itself tangled,
and is offering to sell itself to any ambitious youth who
aches to own a newspaper. But, so far, the ambitious
young man with the price of the paper’s redemption in
his fist, has not heaved in sight The trifling sum of
£4OOO would, it is said, about buy the show ; which
doesn’t seem a great deal to pay for a going concern like
an evening paper in a city of 40,000 inhabitants, most of
whom can read.
• * * *
Tasmanian Writers and Artists’ Union, wearv of
waiting for the settlement of the squabble between
the Melbourne and Sydney press gangs, has decided to go
ahead on its own. A scale of wages has been drawn up
for all men on daily paper staffs, from sub-editors to
copy-boys, and the proprietors are being asked to meet
the scribes in conference thereanent. The Union is
affiliated with the Tas. Trades and Labor Council, and
has for president Labor-member W. A. Woods, and for
secretary P. L. Griffiths, an ex-MEUCURY senior reporter,
who is at present trying to add to his M.A. and LL.B.
the right to be described as Gent One.
* * * *
“O. N. Lines": “Martin,” in his reply to a recent
paragraph of mine, says: “If he considers the stony
tracks of Sydney any easier for the free-lance writer to
travel than those of M.L. or Tasmania, he had betu r
forget it. Since perpetrating ‘ Übique, the Scientific
Bushranger’(a matter of 12 months or so ago), I have
earned about £2 journalistically." All I have to say to
“ Martin " is that, if he can’t earn more than £2 in 12
months on the ink road in Sydney, it is up to him to
carry the blame. I’ve got no brief for the bulk of the
Sydney publications as encouragers of stray literary
talent, but 10 and 20 times more money can be made in
Sydney than in the whole of M.L. and Tasmania put
together. I know men—more than one or six—who, on
lines, are making in Sj’dney from £3 to £5 a week easily.
This on the dailies; and probably none of those lines-
men would join the permanent staffs (although some
have had the opportunity), for the reason that
their conditions are much better as free-lances.
There are not too many senior staff men in Sydney receiv-
ing £6 a week, and less receiving £7 and £8; and
“ Martin ” can take it from a man who knows, that they
earn all they get. All hours look alike to the proprietors
of the big dailies as far as staffmen are con-
cerned ; roughly, it comes to seven days and
nights a week, starting about 10 a.in. and finish-
ing one side or the other of midnight—proba-
bly a year’s average would work out at 12
hours a day. But the linesman is different. He is not
at the beck and call of a nervous or cantankerous news
editor or chief reporter. When he drops his day’s work
into the sub-editorial lap he is finished. Night work is a
rare occurrence, and his average hours, counting his
search for copy, would not be more than seven per day.
One of the linesman’s difficulties in the past was to
keep his account down ; although I grant that that
difficulty has been considerably reduced within the
last couple of years. Not long ago linesmen on the
Sydney News were making up to £lO a week—two
nien in point being Claude McKay (now secretary to
J. O. Williamson) and E. J. Hill (now secretary to the
Public Service Association). Possibly “ Martin ’’ is refer-
ring to what we call “original manuscript” writers—
stories and verse —but that would hardly fit in with my
original statement, as is shown by the subsequent para-
graph of “N.0.T.0.N. Lines ” (B. 10/11/’10). In any case,
if an “original manuscript” writer cannot make more
than £2 a rear in Sydney, he ought to get busy—at
something else.
* * * *
“Rata Joe”: The rapidly-growing feeling amongst
Australian pressmen to arise and say things to the pro-
prietors ought to wake up the Maoriland Institute of
Journalists. Back ir. the old days when it started, it was
instrumental in obtaining for staff-men an annual holiday
of 14 days, since when it has done nix or even less. Its
constitution might have been framed at a conference of
bosses, so much does it seek to benefit.those thankless
persons, and so little does it hold out to the grafting
pressman. There are 10 clauses in the constitution;
seven of them are in the direct interest of employers, and
of the other three, only one is of faint importance.
Years ago a small section of members sought to drive the
association under the wing of the Conciliation and Arbi-
tration Act, and a leading barrister, who was then lion,
counsel, advised that it could be done by throwing over-
board proprietors, editors, leader-writers and such like
ornamentation, and forming a reporters’union. Such a
drastic course, however, was too dreadful to be thought
of by the majority ; besides, the employers would have
felt hurt. So the gentle Institute went its useless way,
stopping occasionally to pledge the toast of “Journalism,”
or —when the sound of salaries being sliced down reached
its ear—shaking its head mournfully, and shouting
“ Gadzooks ” in a whisper.
“X.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The cross marks the exact spot where the crime was
perpetrated. Vide evening paper.
This is the place where the deed was did ;
Where the new-laid corpse w>as vainly hid ;
Where John Dunns bold of its legs got hold,
And pulled it out from beneath the mould,
X!
This is the place where the squeal rang out,
When the victim stopped the well-aimed clout,
That knocked him flat on his own door-mat,
And smashed his skull and his black hard hat,
X!
This is the place where they dragged the pool,
Three feet from the bank (by a two-foot rule),
And found the clock, and the corpse’s sock,
And the poker, bent by the fatal knock.
XI
This is the place where the womenfolk
Of the neighborhood heard the silence broke
By such awful screams—since when, it seems,
They’ve heard the row in their troubled dreams.
XI
This is the place where we made the scoop,
And billed it big, with a large black whoop,
Re the corpse’s leer, in the moonlight clear ;
And the sorrowing undertaker’s tear.
X!!
This is the place where the clue was found,
That made the cops some suspects impound,
They’ve lumbered three, and are filled with glee—
For the rest, our late edition see !
XM!

The Australian medical brotherhood is
grieving that Frederick Treves, the great surgeon,
will not be at the big medical congress next
spring, as arranged. Treves himself is a patient,
and his medical advisers won’t let him come.
Grown weary of waiting for the Sydney
and Melbourne journalists’ unions to settle their
differences and get the all-Australian Union regis-
tered, the Tasmanian Writers and Artists’ Union
has presented the newspaper proprietors of
Hobart with a wage-scale, and has asked them to
give it their kind and early consideration. There
are indications that the claims will not be enthu-
siastically agreed to. and in the event of trouble
an attempt may be made to obtain “free labor”
horn outside the Island. But it is unlikely that any
pressman worth his salt will rush an invitation to
swoop down on the Tasmanian job of his brother-
scribe. The job is not much of a prize, anyhow,
and as the trippers and tourists have about used up
this season’s supply of the Speck’s summer climate,
there is very little in Tasmania to induce much
immigration at present. The Tas. W. and A.
. Union, being a straight-out industrial organisa-
’ tion, affiliated with the local Trades Council, is
looking for some assistance from other unions, in-
cluding the Typos. Linotypes are such highly in-
telligent and extremely sensitive machines nowa-
. days, that any attempt to feed them with
non-union “copy” might be resented.
Sir John Sinclair, of Caithness, the shocked
Scotchman who says Australia is the saddest
r country he has ever seen, betrayed his grim Cale-
donian concern right throughout the Common-
wealth tour of the Scottish Agricultural Com-
-4 mission. At one big farm, near Beeehworth
* (Vic.jSir John’s astonishment on finding the hands
still a-bed at 5.30 a.m. was so great that he could
eat no breakfast.
Dan O’Connor keeps turning up. When
last heard of he was going round to various ladies’
schools in England broguing about the bright
prospects, matrimonial and otherwise, which Aus-
tralia offers. Daniel’s silver oratory must have
an effect. Canada’s advertisement for 50,000
brides was a big enough thing in its way ; but
C mada hasn’t got a Dan O’Connor to talk its big
thing. Australia can anticipate a steady flow of
Entrlish beauty during the next few years.
Australian farmers and others have lost tens
of thousands of pounds this year through sprouting
in the bags—partly because of the excessive rains,
partly because of something else. In its effort to
protect the farmer the Commonwealth law pro-
vides that the standard wheat sack, besides being
of a certain size, shall weigh 2jlb. But the natural
villainy of the Indian jute manufacturer has led
him to, “load” his bags with a preparation of
cheap sago flour, so as to bring the flimsy material
up to the weight. This swindle is bad enough in
itself; but the worst of it is that sago flour acts as
a germinator and stimulates the growth of the
wheat roots. If the bags were manufactured in
Australia, under proper supervision, this disaster
would be avoided ; but the farmer fancies it pays
him to patronise the cheap black labor of India.
Now he is finding out that it doesn’t.

In accordance with the provision of Section 113 of the Companies (Consolidation) Act 1908, we report to the
Shareholders as follows :
We have examined the above balance-sheet with ttie books at head office, and have had access to the branch
accounts transmitted to the head office from the colonies. We have verified the correctness of the cash, money at
call and short notice, investments, and the bills of exchange held in London ; and having obtained all the informa-
tion and explanations we have required, we are of opinion that such balance-sheet is properly drawn up so as to

the explanations given to us, and as shown by the books of the company and the said branch accounts.

Bibenduni s Tyre Lectures .
No. i.
On the front wheels.
It stands to reason, gentlemen : The safest combination of Tyres for the front wheels
is that which will reduce the possibility of side-slip to the minimum, and so enable you to
travel in greater comfort and save your car from many of those severe strains which so often
entail costly repairs. That front wheel combination is a
Non-Skid and Square Tread
—with the non-skid on the left wheel, of course, seeing that the rule of the road is “keep to
the left,” and that, in most cases, it is along their sides that roads are wettest and greasiest.
Michelin non-skids, unlike so-called non-skids made wholly of rubber, do not wear
down into ordinary smooth treads. They are efficient all the time because their treads are
protected by stout lea'her and solid steel studs.
Michelin Square Treads, besides bemg moulded in one piece—which makes them so
much stronger than ordinary plain covers —are so made that, if cut, the incision has a
tendency to close rather than to open. I'he advantage of this is that water and dirt cannot
find their way in nearly so readily.
ALL THE LEADING AGENTS.
WARNING. —Don’t buy Michelin Tyres at less than current prices.
They may be of old manufacture. Purchase only from our Agents.

Aidenn.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
BY the banks of the chrysolite river,
That flows to the opaline sea,
There are leaves of large healing a-quiver,
And golden fruits ripe on the tree ;
And, wrought with the wonder of ages,
O’ermarked with the mystical Seven,
Is the little gold mosque of the sages
In a bright river suburb of Heaven.
Aflash in the scintillant weather,
The domes and the palaces rise ;
A-flutter with pinion and feather
Are all the blue paths of the skies ;
The cerulean ether is clouded
With tufts of immaculate down,
Where the wings of gay angels are crowded
O’er the clean, hall-marked pavements of
town.
A twenty-four carat cathedral
Grows up like a rose on its stem,
With glittering lights tetrahedral,
And fretting of lustre and gem ;
And round the best parts of the city
Are mansions of saintly delight,
Wrought with rubies as red as God’s pity,
And diamonds as clear as his sight.
Far-stretching by river and harbor
Are gardens of blossom a-gleam,
Deep Edens of vine-arch and arbor.
Mist-dim with the silver of dream ;
And a music trails over and after,
Wind-pure without sorrow or sigh,
Like the soul of a child’s happy laughter
That has wedded the pipe of the sky.
Beyond are delectable mountains,
Ail keen in the airs of the west,
Attune with the spray-song of fountains.
Aglow with dim roses of rest,
Leaning out of an ether of wonder,
As lined with a pencil of morn ;
God chained to their summits his thunder
The day that young Laughter was born.
Afloat on the fire-hearted ocean
Are ships that creep up from the night,
Clear sails with the statelier motion
Of white birds that wmg to the light;
And thefreightof young souls that they carry
Is light on each crystalline keel ;
Bride-sweet is the gladness they marry
To the heart-sweet of rapture they feel.
The keels grate the emerald shallows,
A music leaps out from the land.
Oh, wings that were swift as the swallows,
Fold now, for your bliss is at hand !
Plunge anchors of gold in the waters —
The hour of new joy cannot wait—
The City of Laughter’s tall daughters
Wave welcoming plumes at the gate.
Who comes through the portals resplendent ?
11l fits this bright guise with a saint—
No whiskers of holiness pendent,
No anchorite looks of restraint.
A maker of jest in world spaces,
A gardener of riotous bloom—
Will the angels avert their pure faces,
Will the saint with the keys give him room ?
Lo, wide where the glories are thronging,
The voice of his welcome runs clear
From angels, a-tiptoe with longing,
“ The maker of laughter is here ! ”
And up through the heavenly spaces
A tumult of gladness is blown,
Till the clear rainbows shake in their places,
By the pillars of Allah’s white throne.
He had planted the flower of his jesting
By the weary, brown pathway of years!
A bloom that grew skyward protesting
Against a world’s weed-growth of tears;
And now for that one rose of lightness
The portals of pearl open wide,
Great Allah leans low through the brightness
And calls up his son to his side.
Pale ghosts with dim halos of sorrow
Creep in without welcoming cry ;
But for him is the splendid to-morrow
In the banqueting halls of the sky,
Where the feast of the song never closes
’Neath the mark of the mystical Seven ;
For the God that made sunshine and roses
Has a throne of high laughter in Heaven,
By the banks of the chrysolite river,
Where the lilies and asphodels kiss,
There are leaves with dull harpings a-quiver,
And harsh voices that grumble at bliss ;
For Allah, in wonder of pity,
Has built for their special delight
A wowser-mosque, far from the city,
In a second-rate suburb of Light.
But wide in the scintillant weather
The domes and the palaces rise;
A-flutter with pinion and feather
Are all the blue paths of the skies;
And music trails over and after
Where the high throne of Allah is set,
’Mid the clear, holy chords of white laughter
That holds all the worlds in its net.
s.s.w.
DAVID McKEE WRIGHT.
The Cat and the Statue.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THERE are some very bad statues in
Melbourne—including that of Fitzgibbon,
which you will see jtwsfc as you cross
Princes Bridge into the-St. Kilda-road.
One. forearm of the Fitzgibbon effigy juts out
at right angles. If it had been otherwise Con-
stable O’Rafferty would not have written his
classic first report, and the Victorian Police Force
would have been denied much innocent laughter.
All this happened ages ago, and O’Rafferty has
become a senior-constable. But he never passes
the Fitzgibbon statue without blushing. Yet it
takes a good deal to make a senior-constable
blush.
Once upon a time three small boys found a
deat cat in a gutter in Little Lonsdale-street.
After they had dragged it about on a piece of string
for three days the infuriated inhabitants began to
notice something wrong, and chased them out of
the street. They went down to the Yarra bank,
where, as evening fell, they gave the cat a swim,
always on the end of the string. Cats do not like
water, as a rule, but this one didn’t object,
except in the one way possible to defunct
organic matter in summer time. Even the small
boys were convinced at last that, as a plaything,
the cat was no longer possible. From this you
may judge the full infamy of it.
So the boys tied a stone to one end of the string
—the cat was at the other—and carried their
burden up to the Fitzgibbon statue. They threw
the stone over the arni, and hauled the cat up. It
was beautiful, brigjit moonlight, and the boys
pnjoyed the effect of Fitzgibbon holding the dead
cat by the tail. Many passers-by noted the effect,
too, but their enjoyment was marred by a certain
objectionable quality inherent to the cat.
Constable O’Rafferty was then a raw recruit
from a little country town. He had been in uni-
form for six wee)**, and liked himself very well
in it. He had been placed upon the bridge at the
evening change of section, and was simply burst-
ing with the desire for a case. So far, he had not
justified his position in the force. Young Pickens,
who had joined at the same time as himself, had,
alone and unaided, run in a drunken woman a
week ago, written a report on a fine, blue official
form, appeared in court against his prisoner and
secured a conviction. Some fellows had all the
luck.
Meanwhile the cat on the statue dissolved
slowly in wafts of odor as the soft southerly
breeze blew along St. Kilda-road. One of these
wafts smote Constable O’Rafferty in the midriff,
and all but slew him. “ Wan iv thim dahm fish-
hawkers bin emptyin’ his harry into the gutther
agen,” murmured the constable. You will per-
ceive by bis speech that O’Rafferty was of the
material from which excellent policemen are made.
Slowly he tracked down that grisly smell, and
at last, with the craft of an expert ’possum-
hunter, gkied the cat against the moon over Gov-
ernment House. As he got it, another gust of
radio-active cat set him swearing and spitting
again. He looked up at the station clock, perched
high on its skeleton tower. Eleven o’clock ! The
senior was due at a quarter-past. This suspension
of offensive fclidae upon the image of Melbourne’s
greatest citizen was probably a terrific offence.
Here was a case at last. It was not a pleasant
case, but duty is not always pleasant. It was
highly indecorous to allow the cat to remain
where it was. So O’Rafferty, climbing carefully
up to windward, cut the string, and the cat fell
with a sort of phosphorescent glimmer of odor
Upon the grass-plot beneath the pedestal. The con-
stable, with many bad words and frequent expec-
toration, took it back to his point, holding it care-
fully by its remaining two inches of string, and
walking, like Agag, delicately, lest he should jolt
it. As he reached the little sentry-box on the
middle of the bridge the senior came up.
“All quiet?” he asked.
“Yis, sorr,” replied O’Rafferty. “Exceppin’
Oi found this " —he thrust the horrific mass under
the senior’s nose—“hangin’ on ould Fitzgibbon’s
stattv.”
“Hell !” cried the senior. “Take it away—
pah ! ”
“ PhWat shall Oi do wid ut, sorr?”
The senior was annoyed. From a distance of
10 good yards he spake: “Do with it! Better
take it to Russell-street when you’re relieved, and
write a good long report about it. Then take it
down to Billy Davidson, at the Morgue. Unless
you want to eat it.” The cat flung its aura play-
fully at him, and he departed without further
sarcasm.
O’Rafferty was not a clever man, otherwise he
would not have been in the Victorian Police Force.
Sarcasm was wasted on him. His chance to
write his maiden report had come. At 4 o’clock
he clove his way into the inquiry-room at
Russell-street and set the cat down on the
senior’s chair while he wrote his piece. The
senior in charge was not in charge. He
was chatting with the telephone orderly and
the roundsman of the Clarion in the next room.
“Const. O’Rafferty 34,201,” wrote that young
man. “I have to riport that at 11 o’clock p.in.
last night, in consequence of noticeing a bad smell
on Princes Brigde, I made inquireys and found
the boddy of diseased hanging from the arm

room, plaintively. “Must be a dead rat under
the floor, Mac.”
“Maybe, Jim,” replied Mac, the telephone
orderly. “ I noticed it a while ago ; but I thought
it was your breath, only I didn’t like to say any-
thing. Some blokes are so sensitive.”

wards made heartfelt remarks concerning the
sanitation of the court.
Sitting on the kerb, outside the police barracks,
the senior-constable, telephone orderly and re-
porter read-the report with howls of admiration,

Clarion roundsman in exchange for a woefully
indifferent cigar.
