Ta-Mahinna.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
MOON islands out in the shimmering
splendor
Of the moon’s white pathway of silver
fire;
Moon shadows touching a dream-world tender—
O Ta-Mahinna ! 0 heart’s desire !
Were you only a dream with a dream’s surrender,
A moon-spun glory that must expire?
A fate-flung phantom out in the distance,
A rose in the greyness of life’s grey chart,
Shall I wrestle with fiends in a fool’s resistance—
O Ta-Mahinna ! my restless heart
Craves thee again with a fierce insistence,
And we are drifting—apart ! —apart!
O Ta-Mahinna ! Shall Time’s dividing
Quench the white lustre of moons gone by ?
Is there no land of a great abiding
For the beacons that beckoned from lip and eye
Love’s lost stars out in the darkness hiding
Beyond the gates of the grave’s good-bye ?
No lotus land where the gods are tending
The lone lost stars of our exile drear ;
No glad green twilight of peace descending
From Love’s own land of the Always Near,
Where we’ll gather the blossoms of joy unending,
With the faith of the children that know not
fear ?
In the radiant lap of a land unsmitten
By death and darkness and dreams that die,
Where no soul walks in the darkness bitten
With the listless languor that makes no cry,
Shall they live in the essence of things unwritten,
When the hills are dust and the seas are dry ?
Who shall say ’—While the dead are sleeping,
Arid fettered souls in their prisons fret—
If this be the end of our time of weeping?
We dream it may be, and yet!—and yet—
If one should waken with pulses leaping
For glad remembrance—and one forget ?
Ever the hunter of lives is calling—
Calling for sacrifice fair and fresh ,
Where the strong souls wrestle their bondage
galling.
And the weaklings languish in bond and mesh ;
Shall he ever have ruth for life’s red rain falling—
For the strong soul cleaving the quivering flesh?
Nay ! —it is written-!—the pale years fleeing
Like a train of ghosts to the far away,
Bear all our treasures beyond our seeing,
And the earth-heart yawns for the senseless
clay
That has lived and labored and spent its being
Like the swift, sweet breath of a summer day.
Ah Ta-Mahinna ! My heart is yearning
For life’s full measure ! —and life is o’er.
And I am hungered and sick with learning
The weary wisdom of world-old lore.
This is the end ! —God ! —the slow sands turning—
And then the darkness —for evermore.
VICTORIA.
MARIE E. J. PITT.
On the Land.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
f P'l HEY told me to see Smith —Jim Smith
of Showery Plains. They said I would find
Jim a well-informed and interesting old
settler.
I found him in the cow-yard, waving a bucket
at a herd of milkers.
“ Ah, yes ! ” Smith said, “it makes good read-
ing, does all this blessed preaching about the man
on the land making money for himself out of
crops and cows. It suits the money-lender and
the middle-man and the cove in office all right,
doesn’t it ?”
Here he broke off and ran to block a cow from
doubling back to the paddock. He blocked the
cow, and turned to me again.
“Making money for himself out of crops and
cows, eh ! ” Smith laughed—a short, choky sort
of laugh. “Now, look here ’’ —standing the bucket
on a corner post and placidly removing his old
slouch hat—“ if there’s to be the great Judgment
Day that we hear so much about—if a Grand
Jury is to weigh all our actions and award us all
our just measure of fire, or molten lead, or
whatever it is to be, then all I’ve got to say is,
God help the boodlers ! ”
I said I was sorry he took such an extreme
view of things.
‘‘You needn’t be sorry,” Smith answered.
“ I’m only one among a great many that take it —
that have to take it. This farm here, which I’ve
no doubt is a profitable looking concern to you.
with all those buildings and yards and machinery
about it, I bought for £2 10s. an acre. I got it
from a syndicate, and I had 20 years to pay by
instalments. Do you follow me ? ”
I followed him.
“ Well, to meet those instalments and at the
same time to make a living for the family, I
worked and grafted from daylight to starlight
year in, year out, and so did the wife—living on
just the bare necessary food, and doing with the
scantiest bit of clothes that you could think of.
We were not exactly like John the Baptist, more
like a whole family of him, with the difference
that perhaps we worked a lot harder. Ah
yes ! it was all work. There was never any leisure
or pleasure about it. Whenever there was any bit
of a gathering on —a picnic, a concert, a little race-
meeting, and God knows they very seldom happened
along—we could never dream of showing up. In
the first place, we could never afford, to give any-
thing towards it. In the second place, our clothes
were a lion in the path.
“ Anyway, as seasons came and went, we reaped
some pretty good crops, and some were that bad
that we didn’t reap them at all. But by dint of
constant plugging and battling, the soil, on the
average, yielded enough to clear the place of debt,
and to keep us all alive, and pretty humble.
The ghost of the Syndicate ceased haunting
us in our sleep, and the little home at last became
our very own. That was a great moment in my
life. Shall I ever forget the relief it brought !
Heaven may be all it is cracked up to be; but if
it holds only half the happiness the parting with
that Syndicate brought to me, then I’m prepared
to crawl on my hands and knees over precipices
and glass bottles to get there.
“ With the money which hitherto had gone to
meet principal and interest we now began to
get a few comforts. And how they were wel-
comed ! A brighter and softer side of life
started to reveal itself to us—aside we had hardly
known before. And we began to plan little
schemes for improving the holding, and bettering
our position—schemes laid out upon modest lines.
In short, we decided to go in only for such things
as we could afford to pay cash for as we went
along. Up to this time, mind you, we had only
erected a rude slab hut —it stands over there still
and does service as the kitchen—a cow-yard,
and a bit of fencing. And had we continued along
those lines, I would be a different man to-day.
But there’s the rub !
"A sudden change came over the district. I
don’t know why, or how it came. It certainly
was not because farmers were making any more
or any less out of produce than they had been
making all along. But new-comers from the
south came crowding down upon us—new-comers
who might have been agricultural Solomons in
their own cabbage gardens, for ought I know to
the contrary, but who knew little or nothing of
the conditions here. Well, they were all piloted
round the country by the Government, at our
expense, and they talked themselves hoarse about
the vast heritage they saw us sitting down on,
and declaimed loudly of the beauty and the
wealth of it all, and never grew tired of declaring
that our bit of earth was leagues ahead of theirs m
the South, which people were falling over one
another to buy at £5O an acre! And everything
they uttered was hungrily devoured, and printed
in large letters in the newspapers, and a lot that
they didn’t utter at all was printed there. Then
a young town started to rise up all round the rail-
way station. Town allotments were auctioned,
and purchased at big figures. Costly two-storey
pubs were erected. Commission agents opened
offices. Then, with horses and sulkies and supplies
of optimistic views on land values, they started
tearing through the district in the Devil’s own
hurry.
“ This new order of things was a staggerer to us
old hands. For the life of us we couldn’t make
out what the rush was all about, neither could we
see what there was for the ‘ town ’ to exist upon.
But when the Bank of North Jonah came along
and erected a branch office and hung out its luring
signboard, we were more then ever perplexed.
Some of us began to suspect the presence of a
secret gold-mine somewhere. Ah yes! it was
more of a wonder to us than a late frost. We
gave it all up at last, and sat down and waited
for a solution. We hadn’t long to wait. The
manager of that branch bank propelled himself
through the district at greater speed than the
commission agents. And in turn he called upon
us with a full explanation. This is how he ex-
plained matters to me :
“ 4 Now,’ said he, sitting down in the old humpy
that was, ‘ what do you reckon this farm of yours
is worth, Smith, if you were going to sell it ?’
“ " Well,’ I answered, ‘ I reckon I paid as good
as four-quid an acre for it, and if anyone wants it
I wouldn’t take a boh less.’
“ He laughed a sad, cynical sort of laugh, and,
looking wiser than a whole hospital full of doctors,
said :
“ ‘ Do you know what its value really is ?’
“I got a fright. An awful suspicion that my
hit of earth, after all those years of toil and worry,
was only worth about five boh or so, crept all over
me. and I went cold. Anyway, I kept silent
and waited for the worst.
“ ‘ Ten pounds an acre! ’ he said.
“ 4 What l’ And I jumped to my feet like a
scared wallahv. ‘ You’re joking ! ’
“‘Possibly twelve' he added, looking as calm
as though it were cucumbers we were talking
about.
“ That was far too much for me, and I went off
on a go-as-you-please round the room. I must
have done about 20 laps before my senses were
restored.
“ ‘ Why, dammit, then, I must he worth £4oool’
I yelled, stopping in front of him.
“ ‘ If there’s 400 acres in it you are,’ he said.
“ 4 There's 420 acres ! ’ I shouted.
“ 4 Then you’re worth £4200.’
44 1 didn’t say any more. I grabbed him by his
two lovely white hands and shook them for joy.
4 4 When I had done shaking he laughed a short,
grizzly sort of laugh, and Said he could under-
stand my feelings. Then from that he got right
down to business, and what that manager didn t
know about his side of the business wasn’t worth
knowing.
“ 4 What you should do with this place. Smith,’
he began, 4 is to improve it properly. You want
a good barn, and a bore and windmill, and large
haysheds and a lot more machinery, and all that
sort of thing.’
44 1 was delighted to hear my own idea of things
confirmed by such high authority, and right away
let him into the secret of our little scheme —that
old-fashioned scheme of going slowly and paying
for everything as we could afford it. He laughed
one of those wretched, disparaging laughs that
always makes a fellow feel lie’s a fool.
44 4 Pshaw /’ he said; 4 that’s no good to you.
Our bank will advance you all the-money you re-
quire, rightaway, up to6opercent.of its full value;
but, of course, you would have to put it into im-
provements.’
44 That started me thinking in a dazed sort of
way, and while I was thinking he got further
down to business. He gave me a lot of pleasant
information about the beneficent side of banking,
and showed me clearly that by pursuing my own
wretched idea of finance I would only be marking
time. Also, before he left he gave me about
£2OOO out of his bank at 5 per cent., which I, at
various times, called found for later on.
44 That’s five years ago ! ” Here Smith paused
a while, and stared dreamily at the dim horizon.
44 And my farm, now worth £lO an acre, doesn’t
yield me any more than it did when it was only
worth £2 10s. But”—lie paused again—“my
yield to the bank is a lot more than it was. On
that £2OOO I’m paying 7£ per cent, now instead of
5 per cent. In fact, I’m just about working for the
bank. And whenever there’s an election about,
somehow I’m always voting for it !
44 Ah, yes,” Smith concluded, lifting the bucket
from the corner post, “it’s wonderful what we
farmers are making out of crops and cows —in the
newspapers !”
